THE RED PAGE .
The controversy about the title-deeds of an
Australian poet can be easily dismissed. The
material thing is the deeds, N not the title. If
the argument is as to the exact value of our
“sea-girt virgin sprawling under the southern
stars ”
The persistence of the ahoricinal valuation of virginity
recurs continually in our verse and prose : witness the
hawker’s recent lied-Page phrase (above quoted) ; Arthur
Adams’s assertion re Maoriland, that “ Memory has no
chaster virgin ” ; and so on. Hardy has never succeeded
in convincing the average man that “ what stilt abode in
such a woman as Tess outvalued the freshness of her
fellows”—that “ the gleaning of the grapes of Ephraim
was better than the vintage of Abiezer.” Philosophy-
conquest from the mountain of hereditary instinct is
hardly one grain of sand per century.
as a literary force ; —as to how much her
plains, scrubs, mountains, flora and fauna, are
worth in iambics and anapaests ; —then, of course,
the fact that Daley is an Irishman and Ogilvie a
Scotchman must be weighed in the balance. But
for purposes of general literary discussion Ogilvie
and Daley are just as much Australian poets as if
they were Australian-born : their poetry has been
liberally inspired by Australia, it has been written
and published in Australia, and in the mind of
Scotland and Ireland and the rest of the world
their work is classified as Australian work.
* * * 9
Some arbitrary definitions—
Emotion—a, cerebral reaction to sense-messages,
and particularly an instinctive, aboriginal, pris-
tine, racial reaction ; i.e,, a reaction to the strong-
est, simplest, most familiar impulses— to joy, fear,
comradeship, love of woman, home, country.
Intellectual emotion— a cerebral reaction to sense-
messages, but particularly an individual reaction ;
— i.e., a reaction colored less by ancestors than by
parents, altered by personal experience, by books
read, society met, the environment of the ego con-
scious and unconscious. Usually intellectual emo-
tion is not so strong as emotion; it is always
harder, colder ; but it is subtler, and sometimes
finer and sweeter.
E.g. : When Edwin hugs Angelina on the upper deck of
a moonlit Manly ferry-boat he gives and receives emotion
(price Id., plain). When it isn’t Angelina’s night out, and
Edwin is alone on the ferr.v-boat, and he is a romantic
kind of Edwin, and murmurs Daley’s
“ I love the Night ! Bright Day the soul shuts in ;
Night sends it soaring to its starry kin.”
or Stephens’s
“ Oh, summer-night of the South ! Oh, sweet languor of
zephyrs love-sighing !
Oh, mighty circuit of shadowy solitude, holy and still 1 ”
or Whitman’s
“ Press close bare-bosom’d night—press close magnetic
nourishing night !
Night of south winds—night of the large few stars !”
then Edwin is (partly) a victim to intellectual emotion
(price 2d., colored).
.Fancy tethered to earth.
Imagination— fancy freed and soaring beyond
the stars.
* * • *
Now it is possible to make arbitrary mea-
surement of the poetical characteristics of some
Australian poets
Daley intellectual emotion plus fancy.
Quinn =emotion plus imagination.
Ogil vie=emotion plus fancy.
Kendall=intellectual emotion plus imagination.
Gordon=emotion plus fancy.
Stephens = intellectual emotion plus fancy.
Lawson=emotion.
Boake—emotion plus fancy.
Bayldon=intellectual emotion.
And so on. Of course spheres overlap : Daley
is occasionally streaked with emotion, Quinn with
intellectual emotion ; the imagination of Kendall
is rather in the atmosphere of his poems than in
the poems; Boake’s and Ogilvie’s fancy wings
higher than Gordon’s —sometimes almost reaching
imagination ; Stephens’s intellectual emotion is
often close to emotion. And so on.
The justice of this classification should be ob-
vious to any careful reader, though there is room
for debate on minor points. The difference be-
tween Ogilvie’s “ The Near-side Leader,” for
example, and Quinn’s recently-printed “ The Camp
within the West,” is easy to see. Quinn's verses
have no distinct reference to anything visible or
tangible ; there are human figures dimly seen, but
they exist only to give shape.and colour to an
emu .ion : they are as impersonal as the winds and
tides, and the emotion they suggest is almost
as cosmic as the winds and tides. Ogil-
vie’s verses refer to a horse, one particular
horse; and the horse’s appeal for sympa-
thy is skilfully justified and phrased so as to
leave a fine poetical impression. But it is
not the same kind of impression as Quinn gives.
Ogilvie’s verses reflect pure emotion ; Quinn’s re-
flect emotion plus imagination. Crudely essenced,
Ogilvie’s is the poetry 1 of a weary horse; Quinn’s
the poetry of weary humanity. Whence in-
directly the dogma that Imagination, the fine
flower of Poetry, is a transition from concrete to
abstract, from individual to general, from tem-
porary to eternal.
Fancy is an attempted transition, a half-way hero a
light, which fails. ’
And the deduction: the more
objective is merged in subjective, reality in dream,
the loftier and intrinsically better the Poetry.
1. Extending the deduction to the whole field of Art:
this is precisely what paint-impressionists are striving to
express. The “impression” is an endeavour to soften
the hard outlines and crude hues,of the Real, to suffuse
the suoject with the atmosphere of mystery and romance
— i.e., of Poetry.
2. This deduction is not necessarily a “ decadent ” for-
mula, though it verges' on the doctrine of Verlaine’s “ Art
Poetique,” extolling the hinted hue before the gross
colour. For a dream can shine like scarlet and remain a
dream. Idea! Poetry has the sun's heat in the moon’s
lighi : Night and Day unite to make it magic. Maybe
tins was Verlaine’s idea: he wanted the precise joined
yvi'h the vague in ecstatic intoxication. (Which again
is a condition sought for by painters: it might lie held
that Rossetti deliberately drew untruly in order toaeeen-
tuate his artistic effect; and that, for the most parCthe
charges of bad drawing brought against him are made by
realists unfitted to comprehend his results.)
Some successors of Verlaine have also extolled the per-
fect beauty of faded things (Morris carpets and “ Patience”
and “ new spring suitings ” perpetuate the idea for En<>--
lish-speakers) ; but MaliarivnS, for example, extolled fn
faded language—which matched the idea and spoilt it.
For vou cannot drive a nail or a word farther than it will
go ; and onomatopoeia and similar devices are aids, not
essentials, in poem-manufacture. It is a fine thing to get
a husk to fit the kernel ; but the main thing is the kernel
If emotion goes when epithet comes, the result may be
“ precious,” but it is not Poetry.
(The cult of Faded Things and the glorification of their
supreme beauty have a natural home in Australia, the
Land of Faded Things—of delicate duns, delicious greys
and dull, dreamy olives and ochres. Iri such an environ-
ment, Bill and Jim (thimselves Faded —Tired deni-
zens of a Tired country true decadents, always fall-
ing down ; aslope like their gum-trees, and held up
only by the intervention of a post or fence) should
be naturally attuned to the tenderest chords of {esthetic
sensibility. Yet we have been content to let 'strangers
foist upon us the vulgar English ideals of glaring green
and staling red and orange, and denounce our grave har-
monies of rock and vegetation—shadow laid on beautiful
shadow, light on dusky light. And this though the lead-
ing English art-magazine, The Studio, passes by all
the English emeralds and flaunting autumn tints
to bind itself in dull Australian green ! This,
though intelligent Englishmen themsrlves revolt
against the traditions of their crudely - coloured
country, and declare, like returning Morley Roberts
that “there was one thing that struck me' in’
England as very strange, not to say painful, and that was
the vivid colour of the pastures. We are quite proud of
our perpetual verdure ; hut, to tell the truth, the tint, of
the grass after the soberer dull greys and greens and
browns of Australia was extremely unpleasant to my
eye. I thought the colour glaring, not to say inartistic;
it certainly was not unnatural, and yet it struck me as
being as nearly that as if someone had deliberately
painted the fields. It took me months to get reconciled
to it.” Precisely. And the typical English beauty looks
as painted as the fields, with her coarse contrast of red
and white ; yet we permit. Englishmen to come here and
decry the divine pallor of Australians tinted like a
capricious coral ! Englishmen have even been permitted
to denounce the gum-tree, the most picturesque tree that
grows on earth, always at ease, Bohemian, unconven-
tional. To see the cushioned tops of a gum-tree moving
in a breeze—that gum-tree shaped like a soaring para,
chute made of a score of minor parachutes which lift
and strain as if eager to be off and up ; - to watch the shift -
ing interspaces of sky through the cordage of branches,
and hear the birdlike murmur of the leaves, almost a
twittering;—this is to receive a litieral aesthetic educa-
tion. Yet Englishmen persist in, bringing hither those
dense, sombre trees of theirs which defy even an Austra-
lian sun-ray, whichalmost disdain to ruffle in an Alls' ralian
breeze; trees with the heavy magnificence of an English
dinner, and often as dull;-and call upon us to admire
these unnatural exotics I Englishman Marcus Clarke has
even called our gum-tree “ melancliolv," our forests
“funereal,” and won Australian praise for the ludicrous
misrepresentation 1 It is quite time for Bill and Jim to
take off the English spectacles and open their Australian
eyes to the beauties of this country as she are.)
3. Always provided that the poem, while gaining the
charm of dream, retains the force of reality. In weak
hands the effort to lift fact to imaginative heights re-
sults merely in incoherence and splashes. It does not
follow that incoherence is a poem, and splashes are a
picture, because they have been reached bv methods
which in strong hands would have led to the making
of a poem or a picture. Convinced of the validity of his
means, the Mallarmist, poet or the impressionist painter
is apt to take his result for granted. It isn’t. Only the
means are granted ; the result depends on his use of
them.
* * ♦ 9
Ogilvie stands just now at the head of our objec-
tive verse-writers ; Daley at the head of the sub-
jective.
1. The objective men deal with outsides, externals,
realities, and the sensations they awake ; the subjective,
if they take an external peg for thought,'.speedily merge
it in their dream and the sensations of their'dream.
There is no hard-a(id-last line; but essentially the following
classification holds. Objective : Ogilvie, Lawson, Paterson,
Gordon, et al. Subjective: Daley, Quinn, Stephens, Kendall,
etal. (The order here, as elsewhere on this page, is acci-
dental. To establish an order of merit would involve
reams of weighing and measuring, and then only a per-
sonal opinion would be established).
2. Kendall has held sway in poetic heads so long that
one almost, grudges the pre-eminence which Daley’s “At
Dawn and Dusk” has won for him. But the most
enthusiastic Kerulali-lover, judging fairly, should acknow-
ledge that the weight, the sustained accomplishment, of
Daley’s collected work places him first--, with this reserva-
tion : that Daley seems to have put forth the best that
was in him, and we have only hints gnd glimmerings of
Kendall’s better best. [ls it profitable so to compare and
criticise? It is more; it is necessary: for one cannot
rightly praise without.referenee to a standard of praise,
and a standard involves comparison between (not rivals
but) concurrent comrades in the fellowship of the Muse.]
But Ogilvie, like Gordon, is sliding into
the subjective and higher rank. “The City of
Gray Griefs,” (now first) printed on this page, is
a transition-poem, marking what may become an
essential change in his manner. It differs from a
subjective poem like Daley’s “Dreams” because
the reality is unabsorbed, there is a constant
metaphorical reference to the streets, corners,
domes and minarets of an actual city. This need
not detract one whit from the charm of the in-
dividual poem ; but a series of poems so charac-
terised would be likely on the whole to take lower
rank -.than a sfcries of purely subjective poems,
other things being equal. It is because other
things are not equal that Ogilvie, though essen-
tially objective, is poetically worth more than
many an essentially subjective writer.
* * * *
Ogilvie tried his pen at sonnets a little since,
and the best (and irregular) result (now first
printed) heads this page, It shows the vision, and
the power of picturing his vision in words, which
are poetically among his most fascinating attri-
butes. Of a thousand persons who saw the “lithe
young squatter ” probably five hundred failed to
note the anti-climax of rusty stirrups to gleaming
gear—so blind are many of us, though seeing.
And of the possible five hundred who rioter 1 , and
queried, and heard the story, possibly one hundred
appreciated the romance, and possibly a dozen
felt it fully. And there was just one who felt,
and whose brain instantaneously crystallised
emotion into words surely almost as vivid as
itself—
The lifeless form bound to the saddle-gear.
The blood-drops falling on the stirrup-bars.
Vision—emotion—and the phrase which leaps to
concentrate emotion: these are the Poet’s pre-
rogatives.
* • * *
It is a defect in Daley that he is usually hard
and cold ; his work is sculptured—sculptured in
coloured marble, indeed, but still hewn slowly,
and lacking the irresistible vehemence of passion.
In his earlier work— e.(j., “ A Sunset Fantasy,”
and “ The Old Wife and the New ” —there is the
desiderated heat, and these pieces will always bo
preferred by readers who wish to feel as well as to
admire—to receive a heart-throb as well as a bead-
thrill. In his later work— c.<j., “The River
Maiden” and “Night”—Daley is less occupied
with conveying an emotion than . with accumulat-
ing and elaborating beautiful images arid phrases ;
and the pleasure gained from these pieces is more
intellectual, less intimate and personal. The
later note may by some be held finer ; but it is
weaker. Ogilvie, more youthful, is more fiery ;
and while Daley writes slowly, quarrying out his
verses, Ogilvie’s come rushing almost uncalled.
With his head hot with emotion lie takes his pen,
muses a moment, there is a breath from the Muse,
and the thing is done. He says, and no doubt
truly, that most of his verses cost him no more
than half-an-hour in composition, and that if his
first attempt to convey a poetic impression fails he
can never convey it. Either a poem writes itself
or it cannot be written at all. This is not the way
to compose a “Paradise Lost”; but it is the way
in which lyrics are composed. And (once more)
the lyric remains the best fruit of poetry.
* * * *
From Ogilvie’s way of composition it results
that his verses have no marks of the file: they
are simplicity itself. And when he adds tender-
ness, the result is as fine as in “Leila”—•
That is a poem which Tom Hood would have been
proud to write. Ogilvie has all Tom Hood’s
limpid sweetness, though his lyre be not yet
tuned to the magic fibre which sings in “Fair
Inez.” (Ogilvie has indeed shared the emotion
which inspired “Fair Inez,” but Hood’s is trebly-
distilled. )
* * » *
Both Lawson and -Boake have a certain fullness
of vigour which is not in Ogilvie ; though Ogilvie
is vigorous. His poetic jet is flung as high, but it
is narrower. Yet Ogilvie’s better education has
given him a better sense and mastery of words
(approximating him to Gordon), and in a peculiar
intensity of phrase he is unrivalled. Eg.,
The spirit and the sweep of this are a.s good as
Browning at his best; and even Browning might
have envied the last stanza and the magnificent
last quatrain.
* * • *
It remains to say that Ogilvie has assimilated
and expressed the poetry of the Bush and of bush-
life better than any other writer (Boake, who
assimilated, died before he could fully express);
and that as Tie is young and ardent (his book re-
presents the gleaning of some five years ; Daley’s
of some fifteen) he is likely to heighten his claim on
Australian love and esteem. That book, indeed,
could already be made substantially better by the
inclusion of verses written since it went to the
printers; and a piece called “The Bush, My
Lover,” in particular (to appear in The Bulletin
Xmas number) is the finest poetic utterance which
the spirit of Australia has inspired.
BEN HALL'S STIRRUP-IRONS.
A lithe young squatter passes in the dust,
His buckle.rgleaming and his bars a-glance;
But laden with long years of old romance *
The slender stirrups covered with red rust.
The troops are scattered and the dark days dead
When robber-bands made wild the Lachlan Side;
No hunted outlaws to the mountains ride,
A thousand pounds of blood-fee on their head ;
And only these quaint stirrups hand us down
The thrilling story no one halts to hear
Of long, wild rides below the trusted stars,
And that last mournful journey to the town,
The lifeless form bound to the saddle-gear,
The blood-drops falling on the stirrup-bars.
W. H. O.
LEILA.
The nodding: plumes steal slowly by ;
Fair women turn, their heads aside ;
And yet the purest there must die
As poor Love-Leila died.
In town, a boy who never knew
Of better love than this
Is mourning' Leila’s eyes of blue,
And lone for Leila’s kiss.
A horseman on the burning plains
A hundred miles north-west
Bends gently o’er his bridle-reins
And prays for Leila, Rest!
A main who buried all his dreams
Of Love long years ago,
Has dropped one other tear where gleams
Love-Leila’s breast of snow.
All virtuous the world appears ;
But those who turn aside
May never win such honest tears
As fell when Leila died.
HABET!
Down ! And the World’s war-squadron splashes
Past, loose-reined, in the blood and the mire ;
Brown arms sweep and the hared steel flashes
On to the goal ot the World's Desire.
Down ! By the war-steeds hot hoofs cowering,
Broken the sword-arm, bent the sword,
And away to the front leap the sabres showering
Blows for the Hell-hearth, blows for the Lord !
Did he clutch at the moon for jewel
To hind on his bosom and wear?
Did he fight with a fate too cruel
Or follow a face too fair?
What does it matter the reason why t
He is down ; and it's little the World will care
As it sweeps in a foam-fret by.
Down ! Weeps the moon, and he never wore it.
Down ! Ariel the stars mourn into the mist.
Fate's ri d weal is across his forehead ;
Somebody’s face has never been kissed l
Flushes the dawn, and one vulture-speck
Spires and spins in a reeling sky ; f —
Down ! And it’s little the World will reck
As it rides red-rowelled by.
THE CITY OF GRAY GRIEFS.
Somewhere, hid in our hearts, a City stands
Gray-mossed with all the sorrow of the years,
And broken-arched with Love’s unclasping hands
And mortared stone to stone with bitter tears.
Here at each corner of the silent streets,
By every fountain in the empty squares,
Each one of us his stifled Sorrow meets
Beneath the mouldering'arches, unawares.
From dawn till day-death, white beneath the sun,
Hand in cold hand go past our sheeted dead,
Bale with regret for deeds of ours undone,
Weary with longing for our words unsaid.
Here the dim .Sin's held close in buried days,
With the loud sandals of Remembrance shod,
Make hollow echo on the grass-grown ways,
Calling the vengeance of an unknown God.
Here the lost chances of a ruined life
From shrivelled lips let loose a mocking tongue,
Or turn and stab with a relentless knife
The souls that scorned them when the world was
young. C y -
Here the hot kisses of a cruel love,
The lustful kisses, burn like heated brands:
Here is no rest; no Lethe to remove
The snowy fetters of the clinging hands.
Fades the red sun from minaret and dome
Night after weeping night; and still beneath
The gray-grown Griefs in long procession come,
Death’s messengers without the peace of Death.
W.H.O.

DESPAIRING
YOUNG- MEN.
S'tnrmtnmK of Dreaded Diseases which
. «/’ 1 destroy the peace of mind and hap-
piness of thousands every month.
The great number of men, young men, middle-
aged men and men of mature years, afflicted with
chronic ailments of a more or less serious nature
is little short of appalling. Drs. FREEMAN
& WALLACE have been gathering statistics
along this line, and the result is alarming.
The ghost of chronic affliction hovers about and
haunts two-thirds of the men you meet on the
street or in business. It is the dreaded nightmare
of thousands. When they would make merry and
be joyous it points its bony finger and says,
“ Stop—be sad ! ” It turns light into darkness.
It lowers a cloud of gloom and despondency over

Bards of the Backblocks.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Legion of the Lackers.
There is a mighty army, whose retreat long since
begun,
Goes westward, westward ever, with the progress
of the sun;
An army never marshalled, never numbered, never
led,
Whose wages are but scanty, and whose soldiers
poorly fed :
The legion of the lackers—by the force of want
bid range,
Whose prospects have been mangled by the Jugger-
naut of ’Change.
All through the land they wander, by an evil fate
dismayed—
Few are the chances Life affords to derelicts of
trade.
In vain is their endeavor into other ranks to
glide—
All labor is skilled labor to the men who have not
tried—
From post to pillar driven, they pursue their
weary quest
Till they find Life’s manumission in the Scylla of
the West.
Hail to the age of progress ! we are on an upward
way !
And the world goes leaping onward to the bourne
of perfect day ;
Soon will come the consummation of the theoristic
plan,
And the upward march of progress will evolve a
perfect man.
In the clank of mechanism sounds the tocsin of
the free,
And the curse that came with Adam ever exorcised
shall be.
What matters it that countless hearts for lives in
ruin bleed —
The good of unborn millions surely justifies the
deed.
The pathway of advancement was with hardships
ever paved,
And by present minor suff’ring future agony is
saved ;
The records of invention are with confirmation
thick,
From the cotton-spinners’ “jenny ” to the “ Mer-
genthaler’s ” click.
“All men are born to trouble,’’said the Israelitish
sage —
And there’s little room for doubting that they get
their heritage.
Though perfection may come floating on Inven-
tion’s flowing tides,
’Tis the lust for wealth and power—not philan-
thropy—that guides ;
And, thoiigh future good may follow, those who
now the hardships bear
In the clank of mechanism hear the tocsin of
despair.
N.S.W.
GEORGE POWICK BROWN.
The Song of the New-Found Field.
I sing to them that found me,
That sought me far and near ;
From the Maori’s trembling mountains
To the Arctic ice-fields drear.
To camps they pitched with doublings,
To duffer holes they digged ;
To strong hands rough with turning
The windlass roughly rigged.
They lack no room to roam in
That chase the gleam o’ gold ;
The ends of earth are open,
But needs must they be bold.
Will-o’-the-wisps of fortune
They follow, pole to pole ;
And jack-o’-lanterns fleeting—
The girth of earth their goal.
The glamour draws them seeking
The pay-streak in the drift ;
And deep the reef and lode-line
They prospect, shift by shift.
They lack no room to roam in,
The fields are wondrous wide.
And leads still lie deep buried,
And reefs still lie untried.
The dust shows plain their wand’rings
From soak to lake of salt;
In virgin tropic forest
The fever bids them halt;
They stake their claims in twilight
Beneath the Northern Lights,
Or with the Bushmen warriors
Their laagered vanguard fights.
By head-race round the ranges,
By flume across the span,
By sluice-box in the cutting,
By cradle and by pan,
By mill and hauling engine,
The thudding stamp-heads’ din,
By shaft and shift and furnace
The glist’ning gold they win.
To each the chance of fortune
That sifts the golden sands ;
And some return full-handed,
And some with empty hands.
To each the chance of fortune
With every claim they stake ;
But ’twixt the four pegged corners
Waits many a long heart-ache.
And some lie on the hill-side,
On plains all heaped and holed,
And some lie in the level,
Deep-buried with their gold.
Dry plain and frozen glacier,
And flood-swept river-bed,
Oh, men must fight for fortune,
And women weep their dead.
So, here’s to them that found me,
To luck and “ fortune’s frowns,”
To the huts in the far Sierras,
To the new-built iron towns ;
To claims worked-out in silence,
To the roaring stampers’ yield ;
It is sung to the hearts of heroes —
This song of the new-found field !

YANKEE DOODLE.

DIXSON’S TOBACCO HOARDINGS.
lO Anjerica.
lO Australia.
IT was in the summer of 1778, Wash-
ington, with his army ever active,
determined to oppose the British
General, Sir Henry Clinton, who, having
evacuated Philadelphia, was retreating
through Hew Jersey on Hew York. The
forces met on 28th June at Monmouth.
La Fayette conducted the attack. The
heat was so intense that some refused to
fight, and many fell dead from exposure
to the sun’s rays. Both sides fought
desperately. As the day waned, and the
j flagging spirits of La Fayette’s, soldiers
were at a low ebb, a strange sight
aroused them. Marching with drum
and fife was an old man, Jeremiah Wyn-
ton, and his son and grandson. The
old father, with silvery flowing locks,
sturdily strode through the field. The
son with bandaged head fifing lustily,
the youth, catching the spirit of his
grandsire, rolled out with him the soul-
inspiring rattle of the drum. Father, son,
and grandson had accompanied the
troops as volunteers, and were ever in
the forefront of the fight. The fierce
light of battle was in their eyes, they
knew no fear, and many a wild cheer
arose, as on that fateful day Yankee
Boodle was heard as never was heard
before or since.
YANKEE Doodle as a Trade Mark
is admitted by all to have no equal
on this side of the World, and, as
a Tobacco, Dixson and Sons determined
that it should enjoy an equal reputation,
consequently the resources of their great
establishment, and patient trials and ex-
periments (involving several visits to
America in search of suitable leaf with
which to produce the tobacco sought
after) resulted in a Dark and Bright
Tobacco, which to-day CHALLENGES
THE WHOLE WORLD IN COMPE-
TITION, and that it is appreciated may
be gathered from the fact that the sales
for New South Wales alone now reach
Tons pee Day.
The objection of many smokers to
Dark Tobacco is its strength. Dixson’s
have overcome this, and Dark Yankee
Doodle, while possessing a delightful
flavor, is not strong. Then again, Light
or Bright Tobacco, although mild, burns
the tongue. Bright Yankee Doodle is
equally mild, richer than other tobaccos
in aroma, and DOES NOT PARCH
THE TON'GL T E. Every smoker should
TRY EOR HIMSELF.

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘Correspondence" column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

applying to MS.

SATURDAY, DECEMBER 3, 1898.
On Behalf of the Widow.

under that Act according to the duties hereinafter men-
tioned.

2. Where under Part 111 of the Stamp Duties’Act
1898, duty is payable or chargeable in respect of the
estate of any person dying after the first day of December
1898, or is payable or chargeable on or after the death of
any person so dying in respect of any estate the subject
of any settlement, trust, disposition, conveyance, transfer,
vesting, purchase, investment, or gift, there shall be
levied, charged, and paid for the use of Her Majesty and
to form part of the Consolidated Revenue Fund, accord-
ing to the rates hereinafter mentioned, a duty upon any
property or any interest in the same which any person,
on or after the death of the person so dying, takes under
or by virtue of any probate of the will, or letters of
administration of the estate or order to collect the estate
of the person so dying, or any such settlement, trust,
disposition, conveyance, transfer, vesting, purchase, in-
vestment, or gift.
Lineal issue or lineal ancestors of the
deceased, or the husbands or wives of
any such persons 1 per cent.
Brothers and sisters of the deceased and
their descendants, or the husbands or
wives of any such persons 3 „

mother of the deceased and their de-

—Painfully elaborate resolution introduced recently in
N.S.W. Assembly.
The proposal to raise increased probate and
succession duties, which was affirmed by the
Assembly but rejected by the Legislative
Council of New South Wales a year or two
ago, is revived by the above resolution. And
The Bulletin again takes up the case on
behalf of the widow and the small orphan.
* • * •
Two or three things may be reasonably
allowed just here, before the widow comes
on the scene. The Reid Government has
always had a great difficulty in making ends
meet —in fact, it has never made ends meet;
and it is now driven to partially admit the
fact. It is difficult to see how anything very
large can be done by retrenchment; the one
distinctly good point about the Reid Minis-
try’s finance is that it hasn’t been extravagant
in its expenditure. It wants a great deal
more new revenue than it now demands—at
least £BOO,OOO a-year more—to enable it to
pay out of revenue the things that should be
charged thereto, and which are now put into
loans, and left as a burden and a dead-
horse to posterity. It is pledged not
to levy import duties —it does levy
them on a large scale all the same, still
there are limits in this direction. It is
distinctly pledged not to increase the land-
tax beyond the present penny in the £—not
that its pledges amount to much. There
isn’t very much revenue to be obtained by
abolishing the land-tax or income-tax ex-
emptions—besides, a special tax on the little
cocky who scrapes a scanty living out of 50
or 100 acres of ground, or on the man who
earns 30s. a week for the support of himself
and his family, would arouse a storm of
opposition, and it wouldn’t look well on the
part of a Government whose loudest boast
is that it has taken the burdens off the poor.
An absentee tax is contrary to the Foreign-
trade policy of booming the outside capi-
talist, and a graduated land-tax upon
specially large estates is shut out by the
promise above-mentioned that one penny in
the £ shall be the extreme limit. So many
things are prohibited by the Calico Jimmy
gospel that there is very little except in-
creased probate and succession duties to fall
backvtipon, And, so far as concerns the
duties on large estates and those proposed to
be levied on bequests, large or small, to
strangers in blood, there is nothing at all to
object to.
• * » •
But, as regards the small widow, and, in a
less degree, as regards the small orphan, the
case is different. In the first place, it seems
that the estate, if over £5OO and under
£lOOO in value, is chargeable at the rate of
1 per cent, probate-duty. Also, that the
widow is liable for 1 per cent, succession-
duty—payable no matter how small the
estate is. Therefore, if the estate runs to,
say, £520, and it is all left to the widow, it
appears that that unfortunate female is taxed
2 per cent. Below that she is still taxed,
but not so much. The Bulletin desires re-
spectfully to draw the attention of the
cornstalk Parliament to the fact that this
is, in very many cases, a special tax levied
on a very poor and distressed class at a time
when it is least able to stand that tax. The
average widow is elderly ; there are plenty
of exceptions, but in the bulk of cases she
has a habit of being elderly. Very often she
knows no trade, except the unremunerative
one of a housewife—learned by keeping her
own house; and that of a laundress and
plain needlewoman —acquired through the
necessity of doing her own washing and
sewing. A generation which jeers at the
female who wants to know any other occu-
pations, and calls her a New Woman,
and a Shrieking Sister, and uses language
about her snatching the livelihood of
men from them, can’t complain of this.
Very often she has three or four
children either below working age or
not sufficiently above it to earn anything
appreciable. A community which brags
about the increase of population as one of
the greatest blessings of a community, and
as a sure sign of its virility, and which puts
in prison for seven years the professional
gentleman who helps to prevent the increase
of population, can’t complain of this either.
And very often there are thousands of good
reasons why, after many years of hard work
and economy, the old man dies of rheumatism
or some other ailment caught through ex-
posure while repairing the public roads, and
only leaves £520, and is darned glad to leave
so much.
* * • •
Said amount may easily represent no
income at all. A six-roomed cottage with
the bit of ground attached and the furniture
can account for it all, and leave no ready
cash whatsoever (the old man’s illness often
absorbs all that), and no cash income to live
on or pay rates and taxes with. If the cot-
tage is sold (cottages don’t sell any too
readily just now) and realises, say, £350
(presumably the old woman still wants
the furniture), that means, with hard
economy, about four or five years’ support,
including the rent of the new house; and the
widow can’t positively rely on dying at the
end of four or five years. Nor can she rely
too securely on her children keeping her at
the end of that time—some children grow up
to be unlucky, or to loaf, or drink, or marry
and go away to remote regions, or go away
without marrying, or die. The widow may
be too old or feeble or incompetent to earn
her living by domestic service or washing, to
say nothing of the fact that her family may re-
quire her attendance. Also, she may live in
the bush, where people do their own washing.
She is generally too brown and battered to
be a success as a barmaid. There are very
few occupations open to an elderly widow
with no educational advantages—most widows
are of that uninteresting variety—and there
isn’t one which furnishes even a decent live-
lihood. And when this melancholy old per-
son loses her husband and her sole income,
Premier Reid (the gentleman who advocates
taking the burdens off the poor) proposes to
lay a special tax on her poverty and her loss
by way of celebrating the event.
* • * *
The Bulletin advisedly describes the fre-
quent variety of widow above set forth as
poor. The male person of 25 with no
encumbrances, with strength, a moderate
amount of education and intelligence, a very
moderate but reasonably reliable salary,
but with his life and the world—such as it
is—before him, may be comparatively rich,
though his whole visible assets are the
trunk of clothes in his boarding-house. The
woman of 45, with no breadwinner, no pro-
fession, who has forgotten in her married
life the art of hustling with the outside
world for . a living—such living as the world
affords to a woman at the best, who is bur-
dened with a family, and has lost her good
looks, and has the best part of her life
behind her iiistead of before, may have £5OO
or £6OO worth of assets and yet be miser-
ably, sordidly poor. When this same question
cropped up a year or two ago, and this
paper hung out a shingle with the same
legend “On Behalf of the Widow,” there
was a great addition to the postage reve-
nue through the letters it received from
unthinking scoffers to the effect that there
was no need to worry so much over the
widow with £5OO or so, when stacks of
widows would be glad to have one-tenth the
amount. Several Foreign-trade politicians
were among that horde of correspondents.
Yet if the widow with £5OO deserves no con-
sideration because other widows haven’t
£5OO, then the man with an income of 15s.
per week should pay income-tax because
other people have no income at all ; and it is
no good worrying about how the tea-duty
presses on the poor because there are plenty
of people still poorer who can’t get any tea
at all. In these days “Dr. MacjLaurin's
widow” was one of the brutal standing jokes
of the Foreign-trade press. But the widow
is as numerous and as deserving of considera-
tion as the shearer. The main difference is
that the latter has a vote, and the Govern-
ment and its organs, therefore, can’t afford
to treat him with disrespect.
* » • *
In The Bulletin’s opinion the widow
should be exempt from all probate and suc-
cession duties up to, say, £5OOO. She
occupies a position unlike that of any other
legatee. According to the law of man
and the alleged law of God, she and
her husband are one, and she is only
inheriting her own property. Where the
old man makes a small pile by industry and
economy the old woman, in most cases, is,
by all equity, his full partner. She has to
economise as much as he ; to deny herself as
much as he does ; in most cases, she has to
work as hard as he does, in order to save
domestic expenditure and give the pile its
first start. To her, as a rule, the alleged
inheritance of the little family fortune doesn’t
mean inheriting anything ; it simply means
that she loses her income, and, as a hollow
mockery of a per contra, that the deeds of the
house and the garden, where she has always
lived, have her name on them instead of
the old man’s. If the property goes
to an outsider, the tax means that he
gives to the State something of what he
has gained, and the rest is profit. In the
case of the widow it often means that she
has lost most of her assets (the old man and
his earning capacity being generally the big-
gest part of her estate), and the Government
proceeds to take part of what remains. It
taxes her for the use and luxury of her
domestic calamity, and makes her pay for
her loss. The amount isn’t large enough to
do the State much good, and it is quite
enough to do the relict, in many cases, a lot
of harm. And in most instances the whole
idea is foreign to the boasted principle of
relieving the poor and laying the burdens on
the rich.
* * • *
This paper isn’t a widow itself, and certain
reasons of sex make it eminently improbable
that it will ever be one. All the proprietors
will be dead before they leave any widows,
therefore the matter doesn’t concern them
personally. Any person who wants to heave
the obvious jibe that plenty of men leave
widows before they are dead (secretly, by
the back gate, or over the fence in many
cases, for fear of observation) is hereby in-
formed that this paper has thought of that
joke already. The point at issue is that by
exempting the widow up to, say, £5OOO
the Treasury won’t lose very much, and
a class which is, in the main, very
poor and very helpless, will gain some-
thing. If it doesn’t gain very much,
it will at least gain all that the State
can give it in this matter. And if it isn’t
the very poorest class in the community it
is at least poor enough to deserve considera-
tion. The argument that the old relict with
£5OO or £lOOO doesn’t deserve so much
advocacy when so many other relicts have a
great deal less, doesn’t come in anywhere. If
it is followed out to its legitimate conclusion
even the homeless cripple who tries to live
on offal deserves no consideration because
there are homeless cripples who haven’t the
luck to find any offal.
Threepenny Democracy.
Thebe has lately been printed a “Geography of
New South Wales,” by J. M. Taylor, M.A.,
LL.B., an amiable person who dwells in a rut.
It is a painstaking text-book, and on p. 96 it
declares that—
N.S.W. possesses abundant educational facilities. An
excellent system of primary education is furnished by the
Public Schools at a merely nominal cost, and in case of
poverty of the parents the fees are altogether remitted.
And so on, with the usual rut-remarks. A writer
on geography is not expected to discuss education;
and this rut-writer can’t afford to criticise it, even
if he has the capacity. His book is “ prescribed
for use in schools by the Department of Public
Instruction, N.5.W.,” and he must flatter the
fetishes. If he didn’t there would be no pre-
scription, no publication, no credit, no cash.
* * * «
New South Wales is governed under a pro-
fessedly Democratic constitution ; and among pro-
fessed democratic ideals are liberty, equality, and
fraternity. Or, if not equality of state, at least
equality of opportunity, so that noxious class-
divisions may be broken down, and the many may
achieve all the rewards and privileges reserved
under an oligarchical government for the few.
And New South Wales has a Parliament elected
by popular vote ; and a formidable section in that
Parliament is called the Labor-party—a body of
Nature’s noblemen, with lofty intelligences and
beautiful legs, who watch with sleepless and incor-
ruptible vigilance that democratic ideals shall not be
departed from by one jot or tittle. And the N.S.W.
system of education is made national, that poor as
well as rich may have the advantages of Knowledge;
and it is made compulsory, that careless or ignorant
parents may not defraud their children of those
advantages ; and it is made secular, in order that
the religious feuds bred by traditional creeds may
not canker the teaching ; and it is made free—free
as air—so that the child of the humblest may par-
ticipate in its blessings equally with the child of
the wealthiest, and at the outset of social life the
alert minds of children may realise that they live
under a Democratic constitution where each has
the same rights and privileges as another, and
there is One Class in One Country.
* * * *
Or, rather, that was the goal set for the framers
of the Public Instruction Act. But their hearts
fainted; and education in New South Wales,
though it may be held national' and compulsory
(with reservations due to natural difficulty or im-
perfect administration), is semi-religious and not
free. The religious instruction is in the national
schools rarely more than an empty form, and
causes little social friction. But the Paid-Educa-
tion, the compulsory fee dragged weekly from ill-
filled pockets, is a festering social sore. With em-
ployment irregular and wages small, 3d. a week,
or 10s. a year, is felt as a tax, When there are
three or four children of the compulsory school-
age, Is. a week, from a wage of 30s. or 405., is a
heavy burden. For the children have to be fed
and clothed, and when their school-fees must be
taken from their food or clothing—what a boon is
education! What a shabby, grudging, illiberal
boon !
* * * *
“ In case of poverty of the parents, the fees are
altogether remitted. ” They are ; by branding the
children and their parents as paupers in a com-
munity where, unluckily, pauperism is still a dis-
grace—almost a crime. The teacher collects the
fees ; and, no matter how he may try to shield the
feeless from the sharp eyes of others, he cannot.
And teachers are human, often overworked and
underpaid. You cannot get all learning and all
chivalry for £95 a-year. So there is often a Mon-
day morning crucifixion of a shrinking child. The
children with threepence pay unconcernedly or
proudly, according to temperament. The children
with no-threepence are asked to explain how they
justify themselves poor little wretches! for
having no threepence. “Please, sir, father’s
out of work; and mother says she hasn’t
it.” “Please, ma’am, mother has been sick,
and couldn’t go out washing, but she’ll send
it when she can.” And so they explain—
some callously, some with hot cheeks under the
glare of a score of interested eyes—the eyes of the
Children with Threepence. And behind them
goes a whisper: “’E don’t get nothing but dry
bread for his dinner.” “She an’ her sister have
only one pair of boots between ’em.” The teacher
notes the reasons, catechises, warns; and the
Children with No-Threepence sit down again—the
unclassed, the paupers, knowing themselves sepa-
rated from the others by the gulf of Threepence.
And this spectacle is one of the familiar products
of “our glorious ” Democracy and our “ excellent
system of national education ” in the 39th year of
Responsible Government, and the Bth year of the
N.S.W. Labor-party !
* * * *
The division between rich and poor, between
Threepence and No-Threepence, is often carried
into the playground, often bears fruit at home.
“Mother, Susy Jones had no threepence again
to-day ” —with the unspoken innuendo, “I’m three-
pence better than Susy Jones.” Meanwhile Susy
Jones is asking, “ Mother, why can’t I have three-
pence-like Henrietta Simpson ? ” So the iron
enters into the little souls—or the gold that pierces
more deeply than iron. In some schools there is
a weekly collection ; but as children ‘ 1 forget, ”
or “mother hasn’t got change,” the fees are
often collected twice daily—and much time is
wasted in rating careless children, or in correcting
mistakes. If any business man will consider what
it means to worry 3d. a week out of, say, 130,000
children, who often have to worry it out of their
jjarents, he will realise the immense loss of time to
children and teachers. Considered merely as a
method of taxation, no move cumbrous, no more
stupid system could be devised. And the waste
of time in school is only the beginning of waste.
Many children get into arrears ls., 2s. 9d.,
ss. 6d., and so on, and upon the teachers is saddled
the responsibility of collecting the arrears, with
the tremendous book-keeping and reporting in-
volved. Upon the country is saddled the cost of
a special staff of clerks to deal with arrears and
exemptions and check the teachers’ book-keeping
and reports. The system is so complex that it is
wonderful it does not die of its own weight. It


lives' only by the piling-up of tape and documents
to an incredible height—a height which only people
acquainted with Government departments can
imagine. And even then it fails to collect more
than a percentage of the debts.
• ¥ • •
Most children, most parents, postpone as long
as possible the application for a pauper-certificate
absolving from the payment of fees. In the mean-
time the teacher is acting as bum-bailiff, brought
into perpetual conflict with parents, rated by the
inspector if the arrears are large, continually dis-
tracted from his work or recreation. And the
humiliation involved in the efforts to enforce pay-
ment falls upon the poor, the hard-working, the
class already sufficiently humiliated— in the name
of glorious democracy. In a school of 150 to 200
pupils the teacher may have to make out 20 or
30 bills every month for school fees in arrear—
form A. Form B is a memorandum telling the
parent to send along the fees, or he will be reported
to the Department. Form Cis the report to
the Dept., under eight heads, and taking several
hours to prepare. Dates and amounts have to be
hunted up, and a copy kept. Form D is sent to
the Dept, with fees paid every month, and has a
host of interesting complications. Form Fis the
pauper’s form, in considerable detail, endorsed with
the teacher’s report. Form Fis a special report
on a case of arrears, and completes the batch.
And the Department has to keep a month-to-
month reoord of the thousands of children (id. in
arrear or more, as a check on the teacher ; to note
and check every individual case of exemption ;
and generally to oversee from Sydney the finances
of the remotest parent in the province.
* * * *
Report said that the N.S.W. Public, Service
Board examined into the working of the Educa-
tion Dept. Since the Dept, goes on as before—
surely report lied!
PLAIN ENGLISH.
“Support in Return for Concessions.”
Judging by the present political situation in
N.S.W., the policy of support in return for
concessions is producing very little in the way
of democratic legislation. The support is there
all right, but the concessions fail to come
along. The Council Reform Bill has again
dropped overboard to be a lost Jonah in the
vasty deep where Unfinished Legislation washes
around till the Day of Judgment. The long-
promised measure for consolidating the two
Savings Banks into a Slate Bank of Issue is
just where it was four years ago. The scheme
for the resumption of large estates for Closer
Settlement and the Local Government scheme
are never heard of nowadays. The Old Age
Pension system is promised on a small scale in
a year or two ; and a small instalment of the
State Land Bank principle is also promised not
quite so far ahead ; and a law providing for
arbitration in trade disputes is promised before
very long; and it is promised that the important
provisions of the Coal Mines Act, which
have been allowed to lie in abeyance for
two years, are to be enforced next February.
But while sundry valuable democratic schemes
are thus looming in front with the same old
loom, and a lot more have dropped hopelessly
behind, hardly any of them have actually
arrived. The things that are coming, and the
things that were once coming but which have
given up all attempt to get here, are very many,
but those that are actually here can almost be
counted on the thumb of one hand. The
establishment of a modified Foreign-trade
policy can’t be reckoned as a concession to the
Labor and Democratic party, for it professes to
have sunk the fiscal issue. So the list about
boils down to an income-tax which presses
equally on the man who earns a precarious in-
come by daily toil and the one who has a sure
income from investments, and which strikes
the man with £lOOO a year and the man with
£lO,OOO in practically the same degree. Also
a land-tax which doesn’t discriminate between
the man with a medium estate and the
man with half-a-county, or between tlie
absentee and the one who spends his
income in the country.. The concessions
are a mere speck, while the support is
enormous. Yet the situation isn’t so curious as
it looks. The relative cases of Lucknow and
Broken Hill about explain it. The Reid
Government and the late Dibbs Govern-
ment each had a big labor disturbance on
hand. In each case Capital was the aggressor,
and in each case Labor looked to the Govern-
ment for support. When the Lucknow gold
capitalists wanted the State to give them time
to crush their employes by granting a suspen-
sion of the labor-conditions, the Reid Govern-
ment declared it would oppose the concession,
and thereby gained the gratitude of Labor.
Then it didn’t oppose, and the owners got the
State assistance they asked for, and it thereby
gained the gratitude of Capital. When the
Dibbs Government had a somewhat similar
opportunity, it gained the gratitude of Capital
only by briefly and coarsely telling Labor it
would see it damned first, or words to that
effect. To all intents and purposes, Labor gob
left just as badly in each ease, but the way of
doing it was different. And now the Labor-
party chooses between the lying crowd which
promises everything and gives little or nothing,
and the brutally honest or honestly brutal one
which a few years ago promised nothing and
gave about the same as the other lot. Tlie
Democratic cause, unfortunately, makes very
little progress either way.
The Curious Case of Mr. Broadfoot.
Eleven months ago one Broadfoot, of a mani-
festly ill-regulated, if not irrational, turn of
mind, was sentenced, at Melb. Sessions, to 12
months’ “ hard,” for indecently assaulting
children, and, in order that his weak brain—
evidently incapable of controlling his abnormal
passions and desires—might have a chance to
recuperate in the cool seclusion of the prison-
cell, the Judge likewise awarded him four days’
“solitary” every four mouths. The wretch
became so peculiar in his behaviour that lie was
kept under observation (in itself a source of
irritation to the victim), but the gaol medical
officer thought there was not sufficient
cause to warrant his committal to Yarra
Bend Asylum. Presumably, also, there
was not sufficient cause to warrant the remis-
sion of the second ami third terms of solitary.
The result was that the other day the prison
gates opened to discharge a confirmed lunatic,
who walked along “ with a scared expression,
yelping and whimpering like a dog,” and be-
having so eccentrically that he was arrested,
pronounced insane by two doctors, and com-
mitted to the asylum. All of which is a
hideous commentary on the Vic. penal system.
The man was certainly more or less non compos
at the time of his sentence, and the lovely
“ system,” instead of trying to cure his disease
by rational, scientific treatment, endeavored to
“ deter ” him by a course of the silent, solitary,
masked folly of Pentridge. And bow many
more lunatics, or quasi-lunatics, are there at
present inside that stockade, being subjected to
“ solitary ” and the lash and other hideous and
disgraceful deterrents of alleged crime ?
Probate.
Premier Reid, on the proposed N.S.W. death-
duties (estates under ,£5OO to go free of probate
duties, but succession duty to be paid on all
bequests, however small, widows and children
being let off at 1 per cent) — (Telegraph report):
This schedule is exactly the same as the Victorian,
with the exception of the small estates of from £2OOO to
£5OOO. ... In all the other Australian provinces but
Victoria the exemptions were about £2OO or £3OO. Vic-
toria was the only case where such liberality was shown
as to grant exemption up to £lOOO. To follow the
example of the sister province would be to destroy the
revenue produced by the duties. ... It was more liberal
than any of the other provinces.
As usual, Premier Reid manufactures bis facts
as he goes along to suit his case. His liberal
proposal is to exempt estates below £5OO and
to charge the widow 1 per cent, duty all round.
Victoria not only grants exemption up to
£lOOO, but up to £50,000 it lets the widow and
children off at half-rates. Westralia charges
no duty on estates worth less than £l5OO, and,
if the estate is worth only £2500, it charges
duty on £lOOO only. Also, the widow or children
of deceased pay per cent, only on estates
under £2500, so, with the liberal exemption,
Westralia leLs the relict or orphan in humble
circumstances oil’ at about one-sixth of the
rates which Reid proposes t.o charge. When
the old man leaves only £l5OO, and the whole
property goes to the widow, W.A. law charges
her nothing, and for £2500 it exacts from her
only £5. Maori land, the most Democratic
province, and the one which goes in most
heavily for direct taxation, when the property
goes absolutely to the widow or widower iets him
or her off absolutely free, no matter bow large
the estate may be, on the fair and logical ground
that husband and wife are equal partners, and
that when one inhen\s from the other he, or
she, is only nominally inheriting what is really
his, or her, own property. But M. L. gets level
by charging high rates to outside inheritors —
the estate worth £21,000 pays 10 per cent, in
Maoriland, while Premier Reid proposes to
charge only 5 per cent. Therefore, when the
N.S.W. boss politician says that Victoria is
the only province which gives exemption up to
£lOOO, he spins the usual fiction ; and when
he alleges that his schedule of duties is more
liberal than that of any other province he is as
recklessly inaccurate as he generally is—which
is a pretty severe thing to say.
—
Salvation at tlie Other Man’s Expense.
The sweet unreasonableness of dogmatic reli-
gion asserted itself vigorously in the case of
Alfred Archer at Melbourne Gaol last week.
He had murdered his mate in cold blood for
the sake of £lO and a few perquisites ; his
efforts to conceal the crime and escape capture
were fruitless ; he was finally brought to the
condemned cell, where a zealous parson minis-
tered long and earnestly to his mortal funki-
ness, with the result that Alfred Archer
became very penitent. Anyway, he thowjht he
was penitent, and the good clergyman thought
likewise. Nobody can distinguish between the
workings of penitence, fear, or hysterical hope
in such cases, nor can anybody estimate the
value of “ true repentance ” in any sinner who
will be no further exposed to the temptations of
the world, the flesh, and the devil. The
death-sentence had cut this particular peni-
tent off from all risk of backsliding. His
ghostly comforter having persuaded him to
“ believe,” told him to consider himself
whiter than snow, and Archer did as he was
told. He believed with enthusiasm, and, al-
though he had no appetite for his last
breakfast, he certainly went to Glory
with a fair show of confidence in his luck.
Alfred Archer, of course, had exceptional
luck. He might have refrained from murder
and have died a natural death like any or-
dinary sinner. One never knows how and
when one may be sent for. Some years ago an
English judge died suddenly in a brothel, evi-
dently unredeemed. Accepting Church doctrine,
the Judge therefore died in sin and was lost
forever. How different the death of the mur-
derer at Melb. Gaol, as depicted in the local
’Eraed ! “ God so loved Alfred Archer,”
said the clergyman, reading the burial-service
on the gallon’s, “ that, he gave His only be-
gotten Son that through Him Alfred Archer
might he saved 1 ” . . . Archer, in a firm and
unbroken tone of voice, said, “ I wish to thank
all the officials here, and may God bless them
and keep them for that Heavenly Kingdom in
which I am now prepared to meet my God.”
The fortunate fellow associated his salvation
with the prison and all that therein is. He
was grateful to the warders who had kept him
in the place where salvation is procurable on
easy terms, and died practically thanking God
that he had “ found salvation ” through the
slaughter of his poor but irreligious male, who,
by all orthodox showing, was damned to save
AIF. Archer, Esq.
The Will of the People.
He asked that, if possible, they should stand with a com-
bined front with regard to the resolutions, so that the
other provinces might know clearly what this province
demanded before Federation could he accomplished.
When tlie Bill came before that House amendments, some
of which were no doubt of a drastic character, were
made. But he was sorry to say that when the Bill was
taken to the Convention the amendments of bills Chamber
were treated as so much dirt—(hear, hear)-and they
were sneeringly alluded to as only amendments of the
Upper House. Well, it must be a marvellous satisfaction
to hon. members who devoted a great deal of considera-
tion to those suggestions or amendments to find that the
greater portion had been adopted, and that the people of
the province had in a most unmistakable way stated their
approval of the work which the Council did when the Bill
was before it. (Hear, hear). Although he could not con-
scientiously say that he approved of the Bill, even with
the proposed safeguards, he would accept it —first of all
because he bowed to the will of the people, and, secondly,
because he was afraid that unless they took the Bill
altered and safeguarded as they proposed, the people of
the province might again be divided on the much-vexed
question, and perhaps be induced to accept a much more
dangerous Bill or fall back upon the old Bill, and accept
it without anvalteration at all. These were the considera-
tions which led him to make this compromise, provided
that the Bill was made acceptable to the people of New
South Wales, and workable and reasonable to all the pro-
vinces concerned. But he wished it to he clearly under-
stood that, so far as he was concerned, if the other pro-
vinces took the view that the alterations were not to be
accepted, and endeavored to force the Bill as it stood on
the Government, they would have to vet another Govern-
ment to carry out Federation. And if they hacked about
the resolutions and virtually destroyed them, if they vir-
tually wanted to return to the old Bill, it would be neces-
sary to get another Attorney-General in his place. He
did not say' this in any spirit of defiance, hut he did say
that when they knew the will of the people, declared at
the elections, etc., etc. — Attorney-General Want, intro-
ducing the Federal resolutions in fi.S. W. Leg. Council.
The Federal intentions of the present N.S. W.
Government are best judged by the unfederal
company it keeps. A year or more ago
Attorney-General Want declared himself in
the N.S. W. Council to be a provincialist, the
“ arch-destroying angel of Federation,” and an
opponent of Australian unity in every form.
He quitted, or pretended to quit, the Reid
Government, because of his bitter provincial-
ism, and he stumped the country with state-
ments, mostly untrue, about the alleged mi-
brotherly conduct of the ot her provinces, with
a view to inflaming the parochial spirit in Corn-
stalk land. His famous assertion that N.S. W.
should beware of alliance witii Victoria because
the latter province levied a duty (which existed
only in J. Want’s imagination) on N.S.W.
coal, was only one of a multitude of instances.
The Reid Government took Mr. Jack back
as a convincing proof of its insincerity,
and N.S.W. now finds that same old enemy,
unchanged in every particular, entrusted with
the management of the Federal movement in
the Upper House. He admits that he is still
hostile even to the new scheme drawn up by
bis own Ministry, and that he is only lending
the darkness of his perfunctory countenance to
it as a matter of form. He incites the Council
to make further alterations (of a strictly pro-
vincial tendency) in the scheme by telling it
that the people unmistakably approved of the
last lot of amendments from the same source —
which statement hasn’t one word of truth or
accuracy in it. The Convention rejected the last
batch of Council amendments, and the people
unmistakably approved of the Bill with these
amendments carefully left out. He claims to
be “ bowing to the will of the people ” in pre-
senting the new Bill, despite the fact that the
people accepted the old Bill by a majority of
over 5300. He is willing to go on with the
movement only “provided the Bill is made
acceptable to the people of N.S.W.”—the same
people who accepted the original measure by a
distinct majority, and were informed by the
truculent Want crowd that they couldn’t have
it. If the other provinces desire to go on
with the old Bill, which they and N.S.W. ac-
cepted, Want, at all events, will have none of
it. The people of N.S.YV. sent the Reid
Government back to office at last election on
the Freetrade and Council-reform ticket, and,
“ knowing the will of the people ” as thus ex-
pressed, A.-G. Want is not crying out for the
reform of the Upper House, but for the infuri-
ated chucking-out of the people’s Federal Con-
stitution as endorsed by them on June 3. In
every respect save one, the new scheme is vastly
less democratic than the one which the N.S.W.
community affirmed by over 71,000 votes five
months ago, and, by the time the unreformed
Council of N.S.W. is done with it, it will pro-
bably be even less democratic than it is now.
But the really interesting feature of the situa-
tion is Jack Want, the avowed provincialist, in
charge of the Federal movement, and calling on
the House which doesn’t represent the people
to destroy, in the name of the people, the Bill
which the people accepted, and saying that he
bows to the will of the people in doing so.
The Automatic Ikey Mo and the
Other One.
One Sievier, a rather artful, or exceedingly
foolish, friend of Ikey Mo, was complaining
the other week that The Bulletin’s way of
making a book on, say, Moouee Valley Cup is
much easier to accomplish on paper than in
practice. And the said Sievier did moreover
talk learnedly to The Bulletin re the policy
of laying a bit extra agin’ the favorite
and the chances of being caught napping,
and so forth, all of which was beside the
question of Tote v. Ikey Mo. Sievier and his
brother “ experts ” will please accept this para-
graph as an intimation that The Bulletin re-
quires no teaching how a “book” should he
made— on the linen of the tote. It was on those
lines thatour Moonee Valley Clipbook was made.
“ How accommodating your punters must have
been to allow you to lay exactly what you
wanted,” says the somewhat tricky or very
foolish Sievier. Well, the tote lays exactly
what it wants to iay against every starter. In
each case it lays the total receipts of the
machine (less 10 per cent.) against the total
amount invested on the winning horse. It
“ gets round ” every time, and its price against
the winner is the same for all backers. To make
a fair comparison between the bookmaker and
the tote we must assume that the hook-nosed
gentleman lays his full book against every
starter at “starting price.” And when we
work out a book for Ikey on the tote system we
find that of the two monopolists Ikey is much
the more expensive monopoly to the public.
The public, considered as one person, loses a
lot more than 10 per cent, of his average invest-
ments with theringmeii, even at starting prices.
The tote returns the same price to all backers
of the winner. That is a point for the average
holiday punter to remember, and it is on his
behalf chiefly that The Bulletin is talking.
Hundreds of men at a Melb. Cup meeting get
sinfully short odds for their money in the cases
of all except the heavily-backed horses. When
a medium favorite or an outsider wins, the
public, as a rule, find that the prices
which they accepted were less than “ start-
ing ” price. Ikey Mo’s odds against medium
favorites and outsiders are very elastic, and the
careless, happy-go-lucky punters don’t trouble
to stretch them to their fullest extent. They
take 7 to 1 from the first man they apply to,
and after the horse has won they find that it
started at-10 to 1. The tote has but one start-
ing price. It puts flats and sharps on equal
terms. It . relieves the public of all anxiety
concerning the “market,” and doesn’t keep
them standing round ’midst, the din of book-
makers’ bellowings, waiting to make up their
minds as to what price is the best price they
are likely to get.
Mr. Tiiomas 11. Bath, secretary of the YV.A.
Goldfields Electoral Reform Convention, writes:
1 have drawn my own attention to a par. appearing in.,
your issue of 29th Oct., and concentrated that attention
on the part which describes the Separation movement as
a fizzle. Wherever the information came from I can give
it an unqualified denial. For some time there has not
been much publicity given to the movement, but during
that period a committee appointed from the Convention
has been preparing leaflets and petition-forms. These
are now printed, and in the course of a week or two a
series of public meetings will be held throughout the
goldfields, and the residents will be given opportunity of
affixing their signatures to the petition. The question to
be answered by each signator is: “In the event of our
failing to obtain from W.A. Parliament Redistribution of
Seats on a population basis, are you in favor of
appealing to the Imperial Government for Separation
from the province of Westralia?” In regard to the
feeling in the different centres, Menzies, Broad Arrow,
Belong, Bardoc are all enthusiastic in favor of
the movement, and Kanowna, above all other places,
pledges the undivided support of the entire population..
Certainly, Coolgardie Municipal Council and Roads Board
is not as eager as we could desire, hut the rank and file
have promised the fullest support through one of their
delegates. Esperanc , of course, is for us to a man, and
Albany has declared its willingness to throw in its lot with
a goldfields province in the event of separation being
attained. The question of S.A. poverty and debt-per-
head does not have the least hearing on goldfields separa-
tion, and therefore the solidity of the standing of For-
restian admirers is very questionable.

“ Jay Way ” to The Bulletin :
Just one more try to get you to see the Esperance rail-
way through W.A. spectacles. Esperance is the nearest
port to the fields—certainly—if W.A. is going to continue
to import all that it requires from the other pro\ inces, as
nothing worth speaking about will grow at Esperance.
Native industries cannot prosper there for want of
materials. Now, W.A. can, and will before long, produce
everything almost that the fields require, but it is in that
corner shown on the map, inside the 14in. rainfall line —
from above Gerald ton by Southern Cross and down
to the sea-coast about half-way between Albany arid
Esperance; and of this district Perth is the natural
centre. Fremantle smelting works get their coal
from the Collie, their flux from Northampton,
and their coke direct from Europe. Would they have
the same chance at Esperance ? The greater part of the
chaff required on the Helds this year will be grown between
Katanning and Newcastle, and sent direct. Would a
single bag of that ever get there by way of Esperance?
The trouble is that the land good for anything costs from
£2 to £8 an acre to clear, and that has kept things
back a bit, but it would be folly to continue, as a per-
manency, an arrangement that would only benefit the
shipping oo’s and the Adelaide importers. Federation
will, of course, change all that —when it comes—but, till
then, every province must keep its own end up : or go
down. Ism quite with you in re the redistribution of
seats, hut the trouble is that the centres are shifting so
fast just now'. The Ashburton was going to he a big
place six years ago. Kanow'na to-day wants a member ;
tw r o years ago it was only two humpies and a dog.
The right men have hardly yet got hold of the log.
Vosper the unshorn is good—as a fly-blister—hut no one
who knows him would dream of giving him political
responsibility. The men will come, no doubt, and then
Forrest will have to take a back seat, hut the trouble
just now is that they are all in such a hurry making their
pile that Jack is the only man in sight to do the work —
and I know the crowd.
“Jay Way’s” explanation about the in-
equality of representation is a fool of a thing,
anyway. If the seats had been fixed according
to the number of electors when the last redis-
tribution took place there wouldn’t he much to
complain of. But that redistribution was a
shameful and deliberate swindle, and that’s the
whole trouble.
A useful little Act of Federal complexion was
recently passed by Vic. Parliament. It enables
the Acts and public documents of other pro-
vinces to be judicially noticed by Vic. Courts.
Formerly, if one wanted to quote a clause, say,
of N.S.W. Companies Act in a Vic. Court, a
N.S.W. lawyer of some sort had to be fee’d to
attend, swear he was an “expert,” and to
solemnly state that, in his opinion, the clause
in question was the law of N.S. W. on that sub-
ject. An even more roundabout procedure was
necessary in order to prove an official signature
to a marriage certificate, with the result that
prosecutions for bigamy were rendered abortive
by the merest technicality, and divorce suits
hung up for an indefinite time. No harm and
much good would be done by a simultaneous
adoption of this Act in all the provinces which
have not passed it already.

Brisbane Courier :
Payment of members is unanswerable in theory, but it
undeniably exercises a demoralising influence. It un-
doubtedly attracts art inferior class of candidates.
Most of us remember the gay old “ unde-
moralised ” representatives. They consisted
chiefly of squatters from the bakblox, corner-
allotment speculators, retired publicans, and,
occasionally, successful mining men. The same
spotless products of the non-payment system
were guilty of some of the most notorious
public jobs ever perpetrated in Australia. They
fostered (in Queensland) the kanaka trade,
palliated its outrages, ran ridiculous railways
through one another’s backyards, piled up pub-
lic debts mountains high, and last, but not
least, produced the political gang that ran the
Q.N. Bank down a steep place into its present
financial Sheol. A very fair percentage of them
could be seen drunk inside the House, outside
the House, and miscellaneously fuddled every-
where. The present House has its faults, but
it compares very favorably in morale with the
best of the old unsalaried Parliaments. But—
stay ! Isn’t the Upper House in Queensland
unpaid? Yes—and what a beautiful example
it provides of what the unremunerated legis-
lators are and can be I How they rush through
reforms and tear down abuses ! It’s beautiful
to watch them—isn’t it, Courier? Ditto the
unpaid N.S. VV. Council. Ditto Victoria’s gratis
House of Four Dozen Rich Landlords.
