In all its long Australian career old “ Har-
bour Lights ” never before got so brilliant an oppor-
tunity as the Firm is giving it this week at Her
Majesty’s, Sydney. Williamson doesn’t often
drop into old melodrama, and his only excuse for
resurrecting the aged lighthouse tale was his
desire to show what could be made of it with a
strong company, and perfect mechanism, and un-
limited scenic art. The result justifies the fresh
outbreak. “ Harbour Lights ”is built of the same
human materials as every other melodrama. There
is the horribly good young man who never does
anything wrong, and the bad one who hasn’t a
redeeming spot in the howling waste of slag and
ashes he calls his character. Also there is
the pure and beautiful girl; and the beauti-
ful girl who falls (in the usual long cloak
and hood) down that moral chasm which
has the small illicit infant at the bottom; and the
great suspicion, over ten feet high, which drops
on the wrong man when the squire is found dead
in his mortgaged halls of sin. All these things
are very ordinary, though in the “ Harbour
Lights ” the old materials are better nailed to-
gether than usual. But as a pictorial effort the
“Lights” are perfect. The battleship, with its
turrets and big guns and other details, is miles
ahead of any other thing of its kind which has
sailed on the wooden waters of any Sydney stage ;
and the light-house and the sea-pictures are be-
yond criticism. On its artistic merits, “Harbour
Lights ” is hereby recommended to everybody’s
notice without any reservation whatever, and as a
literary effort it is also recommended in modera-
tion.
* • • *
The company at Her Majesty’s, if it isn’t
the strongest “ Harbour Lights ” cast up to date,
at least lays over every other one that The Bulle-
tin can remember. Julius Knight is at his best
as David Kingley ; a plain character where
the cold wind doesn’t blow on his legs
appears to suit him. Cecil Ward, Richard
Stewart and George Majeroni are excellent. O,
Majeroni in particular is improving rapidly, and
every time this writer sees him lie gets favorably
impressed in a new spot. David Glassford is
eminently respectable, at all events, though lie
shines brightest as an Early Christian. Gaston
Mervale fits his sailor character just about as well
as if it had been thrown at him with a fork by a
very bad marksman. The bright and clever Lillian
Wheeler has the most prominent part as the nice
girl; and Ada Ferrar is the fallen maid witli the
long cloak ; while Harrie Ireland is wasted in the
small part of an old, gaunt, soured widow; and
Florence Terriss conies in casually somewhere. A
co. which can afford to use up good material by
putting two ladies of such ability in two casual
little parts is a pretty strong one.
** * *
“Brothei against Brother,” by Frank
Harvey, is probably put up at Sydney Lyceum as
an encouragement to virtue, but it is very difficult
to see where virtue comes in. The old man with
the bankrupt ironworks has two sons—the elder
one that he doesn’t know of, and the younger cub
in the belitopper and the supercilious tail-coat who
is really illegitimate, though lie isn’t aware of the
fact. When matters are explained the good elder
son agrees, for the sake of the cub in the bell-
topper, to say nothing about it, and his father, in
a burst of gratitude, makes him a partner in the
bankrupt establishment, and gives bin. half the
losses. Also, the generous elder son agrees to be
forbearing with tlie lout, and to bring him up
gently by hand, as it were—which, seeing that the
lopt is at least two stone heavier than he, is a
large contract. And he gets his reward. The
lout and his mother allude mostly to the new part-
ner as “ that menial.” Also, the lout biffs him on
the head, and in remembrance of his promise to be
forbearing lie doesn’t biff back again. His best girl
casts him off ; she says that she declines to marry
any man who can be spanked with impunity. The
loutish brother robs the safe, and puts the blame
on the good young .man who, remembering
Iris promise to forbear, stands it with the meek-
ness of the early spring lamb. When the girl who
fell per medium of the lout is publicly shaken by
her father and required to name her betrayer, that
illegitimate son fixes her with his eye to such pur-
pose that she publicly lays her infant at the door
of the legitimate brother, and he stands that also,
and doesn’t burst with the strain of liis wrath.
Even when his girl throws him over a second time
on account of this transaction, he takes it with a
holy calm. Later on the girl’s deceived father
locks him in a cellar at the works at night, and
burns the premises over his head, and thereby
definitely ruins the firm; and tlie good man
struggles out, much scorched and with his ear full
of hot ashes, and forgives the deceived father.
Then, when tilings are cleared up, he [juts all his
own money into the concern to start it again, and
publicly forgives the lout; and when lie finds that
all his best girl’s fortune has been embezzled to
keep the lout going he says that it doesn’t matter
in the least.
• • * *
Harry Diver gives a very able and artistic
rendering of the forgiving brother ; but, despite
all the excellent work he puts into the character,
that meek individual would look better if he
kicked occasionally. Cosgrove’s lout is also a fine
piece of work. Miss Helene Burdette is a good,
bright, flesh-and-blood heroine ; and Miss Wood-
ville is reasonably good as a blind girl who gets to
no definite destination ; and Miss Strathmore, as
the girl who fell, is wooden and perpendicular.
Frank Harcourt always fills the part of a hoarse
British workman to advantage ; and the rest is
mostly respectable tail. Another Harvey drama,
“ Judge Not,” will ensue next week.
* * * *
“ Robbery Under Arms ” continues to
hold its own at the Criterion up to time of writing,
and looks as if it were good for another week, any-
how.
* & * *
Sampson, the elephantine gentleman who
lifts 40001 b. at one heave, and breaks large iron
chains, and carries horses under his arms, and
does frivolities of that kind, is the newest addi-
tion to Rickards’ Tivoli company. The Strong
Man exhibition is apt to be rather a dreary kind
of wonder, but Sampson is proving an immense
draw. Rickards engaged him (so the advt. says) at
£250 a week, and a showman of his experience
would hardly scatter coin about so lavishly for an
exhibition which couldn’t be relied upon to knock
the unthinking multitude. And, on the other
hand, he would hardly make misrepresentations
about the salary of a gentleman who can lift
40001 b. at one go. A person of these dimensions
isn’t to be irritated with impunity.
* * * *
The Miss Maggie Moore and H. R.
Roberts close their season at Sydney Royal on
Saturday, the last four nights being devoted to
“Struck Oil” with Roberts as John Stofel.
After that the eo. will travel round Tasmania and
Maoriland, and finally drift to the United States
and probably to Europe. The date of its re-
turn is unknown. Roberts’ departure reduces
the number of good young Australian actors
loft in this country to a very few (it seems
that Australia grows clever actors mainly for ex-
port purposes), and in recognition of the loss
which this country will suffer the departing star
gets a benefit at the Royal on Tuesday afternoon.
* * #
Mr. John Bennett, once well-known in
theatrical circles, is to have a benefit at the
Royal on Monday evening. Mr. Bennett, in
the course of a long and stormy career, which
has been none too prosperous of recent years,
has done many things—the saving of 24 people
from death by drowning in various parts of Aus-
tralia being one portion of his record. A consider-
able quantity of unsaleable medal, conferred on
him by the Humane Society, is the result of these
achievements, but it is to be hoped that the 24
partially-drowned people will all take a few
tickets apiece for Monday night, and thus convert
their gratitude into solid cash.
* * * 5)5
~Dante has put on a new programme for
his last two weeks at Sydney Palace —at least the
programme is mostly new, though the old burnt
woman is retained in response to a strong public
demand. The principal tendency of the new bill
is towards ghosts and skeletons and things like
that, though the lady who is beheaded, and the one
who wanders about loose in mid-air, perhaps hardly
come into that category. A bony visitant who has
left his flesh in his dressing-room, and a quantity
of unattached 1 lands that answer questions by
flapping in the air, are interesting people to meet,
and would be worth a fortune to those spook-
raisers whose authenticity the late Judge Win-
deyer used to guarantee. Dante, by the time his
season closes, will have put up a record of 0(5 per-
formances in Sydney, and the curly-beaded gen-
tleman has kept the. Palace full all the time. When
he goes, the best idea would probably be to sell
the luckless building to the Salvation Army.
* * * *
Touring companies will not get a chance to
score in Melbourne presently, when the Opera
House is being rebuilt. Rickards has a lease of
the Bijou (likewise the little Gaiety Theatre below
it), Bland Holt gets the pick of the dates at the
Royal, and the Firm can always find its own co.
for the Princess’s. For long past the Bijou has
been the house of refuge for managers on short
visits, but soon Rickards will take possession, and
if Brough wants to play another season in Mel-
bourne next year there will apparently be only the
forlorn Alexandra at his disposal. Negotiations
between Richards and the Opera House pro-
prietary are off for the present, nor is it absolutely
certain that a new theatre will be built on the
projected basis of <£4o a week ground rent for
the same old site. A high ground rent is an
awful thing for the speculative builder to contem-
plate. Garner and Musgrove, when they put up
the Princess’s, had the Firm for a good fixed
tenant, as far as any tenant can be relied upon,
yet they were not very wise in incurring the
responsibility of £3O a-week rent to an absentee
ground-landlord. At any rate, Williamson didn’t
think it good enough to be their partner in the
building spec.. He isn’t tied to the Princess’s, ex-
cept by his connection with the Firm, whereas
Musgrove is tied to the bricks and mortar of the
theatre by the necessity for providing a half-share
of £3O a-week due to the owner of the earth whereon
those bricks are piled.
* * * #
Percy Abbott writes that the Hellers have
just finished a two weeks season at the Opera
House, Auckland. A feature of the show is an
illusion called the “Escape of Dreyfus.” One
editor refused to advertise the illusion on the
ground that Dreyfus had not escaped. However,
he got away at the theatre just the same every
night in a most surprising manner.
* * # *
“ Lord and Lady Algy,” the second of the
Brough co. productions at Melb. Bijou, is about
the most promising light comedy Australia has
seen since “Dandy Dick ” was new. As with
heavier work by R. C. Carton—instance “Liberty
Hall” and “Tree of Knowledge’’—this is very
convincing in places. A comedy that contains
dozens of smart lines, unforced or otherwise, and
is humorous in suggestions as well as in situations,
and has, moreover, points which touch an audi-
ence’s heart, is a thing to be grateful for. “ The
Liars ” was clever, but chilling. H. A. Jones
writes with a hard-nibbed pen. The piece under
notice is clever and naturally genial. Lord and
Lady Algy are a couple of amiable “sports ” liv-
ing apart by mutual consent owing to their
incompatibility of taste in cigarettes and racing
tips. Algy has a talent for backing losers, but
his Lady gets on to a “real dead-bird ” occasion-
ally, and when at last she wins =B9OOO at one fell
swoop she comes back to her husband like a good
old pal and re-establishes the firm on a basis of
mutual esteem and concession. Previously she
takes him by the arm at a fancy dress ball (where
he happens to be joyfully drunk in tight-fitting
big boots) and bears him safely out of mischief to
his bachelor home. The drunk episode is quite a
new gem of dramatic literature.
* • * •
Algy and his wife constitute the principal
charm of the tale, but there are other humanly
interesting circumstances in it. An affluent bone-
boiler suspects his wife of a secret liaison with the
impecunious lord, whereas Algy, in fact, warns
the soft-headed matron against bolting with bis
elder brother, a dyspeptic humbug of the Joseph
Surface type. Then there is their father, a
preachy, Low Church duke, who dotes upon the
contemptible first-born, and exhorts Algy to copy
bis respectable liver complaint and moral tone.
Mawley Jeinmett, a smileless jockey, achieves
several funny silent effects, also some casual
characters chip into the costume ball aforesaid,
and lend a good deal of operatic splendor to the
second act. Scenery and dresses should help to
put this up-to-date entertainment through a long
season, as seasons go nowadays.
* * * *
The acting, taken all round, is the least
excellent feature of the show. Without looking
closely into the details, one feels that some mem-
bers of the co. might be stronger in their parts.
Among the new people W. H. Rotherham and
Miss Italia Conti make successful first appear-
ances, especially the lady; but the success is not
brilliant even in her case. Dartrey, as the dreary
duke, is better than Lovell, as the elder son,
though the latter can take credit for being hardly
recognisable as the actor who played an ardent
lover in “ The Liars.” Three or four ladies in un-
important parts and handsome dresses make no
impression whatever, but Mario Majeroni cuts a
fine figure; and MTntyre, as the jockey, who
comes speechless to the ball in the dissipated
kilt and socks of the Young Pretender, is a
good joke. MTntyre and Brough (in his big
Marlbro’ boots) assisting oiie another downstairs
to the supper-room, are a memorable pair.
Brough’s Lord Algy is easy and likeable through-
out. He might put more breadth into his drunken
scene perhaps, but he couldn’t be more of a gentle-
man in trouble when he finds himself ordered out
of the house, and his wife comes to the rescue.
This is Mrs. Brough’s best effort; elsewhere she is
simply nice, as usual, with a certain want of
naturalness in her elocution. She doesn’t quite
realise the author’s bright creation of Lady Algy.
All the same, it would be hard to name any other
Australian actress who could do it equally well.

“The Mikado,” which was always a
wicked libel on modern Japan, is now more
libellous than ever, but the quaint charm of Gil-
bert and Sullivan’s opera gets there just the same.
Melbourne rolled up in force to the Princess’s on
Saturday, and the little maids from school will pro-
bably sing to big business for a week, anyway. They
don’t warble as sweetly as when they were headed
by Nellie Stewart, for Miss Parley's voice seems
to have lost a little of its color, and Miss Vane’s
vocalism is not her most valuable asset. How-
ever, the damsel puts some music-hallish “ go ”
into Pitti-Sing’s sassing of Katisha, and scores
the chief feminine success of the show, which isn’t
saying much. With Howard Vernon in his old
place, Chas. Ivenningham and Bathurst shaping
satisfactorily, and Lauri as the heathen potentate,
the male supports of “ The Mikado ” are more or
less equal to the occasion. “The Gondoliers”
will follow.

The netv ventriloquist (Lieut. Frank
Travis), at the Opera House, is a seemingly
genuine military person, who parts his hair in the
and puts a considerable polish on his
“turn.” pne of the Lieutenant’s dummy figures
is a life-sized ancient on two sticks, and the ar-
rangements in general show cause why the new-
comer should be billed as the Greatest English
Ventriloquist living. He is certainly a more
finished performer than several who have come
and gone before. Biondi will chase himself in a
succession of false noses four nights this week,
and Peggy Pryde is to patter back to the Opera
House on Saturday.

Amongst the items of fashionable intelli-
gence in last week’s Freeman’s Journal is an
announcement stating that Mr. J. Lazarus, of
Queanbeyan, is on a visit to Sydney. Mr. Lazarus
is—or, at any rate, used to be—the Queanbeyan
undertaker. Mr. E. W. O’Sullivan is M.P. for
Queanbeyan, and also editor of the Freeman’s
Journal.
The Massey-Harris Company seem to be
true to the traditions of the country from which
they hail. They believe in big things. On
Monday, the 21st inst., they loaded a complete
train consisting of 23 trucks of reapers and
binders for Orange. This is the first complete
train of reapers and binders that has ever been
loaded in New South Wales to one station, and it
certainly shows that they are not grumbling
about the failure of crops or bad harvest, but
seem to take things very comfortably, and to
judge by the daily appearance of their machines
at Darling Harbor station they are doing a very
large business right throughout the colony, and
their big warehouse at the Broadway, Glebe, is a
scene of activity all the time.*
“M‘G.’‘ gossips about certain hangmen :
The neiv Vie. hangman and his assistant have flown in
the face of all local precedent by appearing on the scaf-
fold in ordinary clothes and absolutely destitute of dis-
guise. The boss seragger, known as “Smith,” is a jolly-
looking, plump little man of about 40, with a jovial, round
face anil a spiky moustache. In appearance he is not unlike
a cross between Vie. barrister Duffy and Premier Keid of
N.S.W., and at Archer’s execution, attired in a well-cut
brown suit and wearing a gold watch-chain, he looked like
an affluent sharebroker or merchant of philanthropic ten-
dencies. He is a man who apparently takes a pride in his
profession, too, for after he had broken Archer's
neck he threw out his chest and smiled like a true
artist on hearing Dr. Shields say it was “ the most
perfect execution he had ever seen.” [Gaol-doctors nearly
always say that, of every execution.] After that, there
was no getting him off the platform ; he seemed to think
the remark was a call before the curtain, so to speak, and,
in the view of the spectators, he fussed round the hole
where the dead man swung, coiling up the slack end of
the rope, and obviously sunning himself in what he
imagined were the glances of admiration thrown from
below. He strikes one as a trifle fat for a flagellator,
looking as if 25 lashes would leave him puffing like a
grampus, but of course there is a rest of a minute between
every stroke of the cat, and, for the first blow, a fat man
is as good as a well-trained one. The hangman’s assistant
is quite a different person, being tall, gaunt, and spec-
tacled, besides wearing the moustache and short side-
whiskers which are the outward and visible sign of godli-
ness. ’flie assistant, in fact, must be a Sunday-school
teacher. He is a trifle younger than his superior, and
takes no ostentatious pride in his work, modestly remain-
ing hidden at the “ wings ” until the spectators file out.
An English correspondent gossips to The
Bulletin of the Crossley-Butt embroglio :
The Leeds triennial festival, the most important one in
England, has just taken place, and Miss Crossley has
again improved her position. In fact, the position of
principal contralto for oratorio and festival work in Eng-
land now lies between Miss Crossley and Miss Clara Butt,
and Lite rivalry between the two will be even keener since
tlie events of the recent festival with respect to which I
enclose three cuttings. Their position is this—Miss Butt
has an absolutely unparalleled voice, and no particular
intelligence, added to which she is musically very lazy
and learns slowly, hut before Miss Crossley’s arrival she
was without a competitor in such music as she chose to
learn. Then came Miss Crossley with a voice second only
to Miss Butt’s, and with far more intelligence ; tire
history of the last two seasons has been that Miss
Crossley by hard work has drawn close up to Miss Butt,
and the latter, though still recognised as the chief con-
tralto, is no longer unchallenged. The feeling between
the two is very bitter, Miss Butt being jealous of a very
formidable rival, arid Miss Crossley being incensed be-
cause, she declares, Miss Butt lias used her influence to
keep her (Miss C.) out of big engagements. Then came
the curious episode of this Leeds Festival. Miss Butt
was to sing the opening Elijah, and on the last night of
the Festival—the contralto parts in the new works were
given to a Miss Mackenzie—Miss Crosslev’s share (to
everyone’s amazement) was confined to a small part in
Handel’s Alexander’s Feast on the Friday night. She was
very indignant at this, and attributed it to Miss Butt’s in-
fluence with the committee. If this was so, it made
what followed all the more amusing—for ten minutes
before the opening of the Festival with the Elijah Miss
Butt was ascertained to be still in bed ! Miss Crossley
was at once appealed to, and sang the contralto part of
the first half with immense success. Miss Butt presently
arrived at the hall in a great state of excitement, and de-
manded to be allowed to go on at once. The stewards said
that Miss Crossley, having begun, should finish the first
part, and accordingly Miss Butt had to be content with
making her first appearance as Jezebel in the second
part, and, as may be imagimd, her state of mind helped
her to sing the vindictive music gloriously. Miss Cross-
ley, however, had secured her triumph. The event caused
much gossip, and finally came a letter from Miss Butt —
which 1 enclose—in which, it was remarked, there is not
even the common civility of thanking Miss Crossley for
coming to the rescue, and remedying her blunder. It was
observed also that the notices of the coming Winter and
Spring concerts, with which Leeds was placarded, con tained
the name of “ Miss /d i Crossley, principal contralto of
this year's Leeds Festival.” This has not unnaturally
added fuel to the already considerable flame ; and at (he
artists’ luncheon with ihe Mayor of Leeds the two ladies
were placed at opposite ends ot the table, whence (hey
exchanged (lie coldest of bows and tile hottest of glances.
In conclusion, “ Australia has adva> c-ed,” and in the
next two years, if Miss Crossiey’s voice improves, her
advantage in intelligence should give her the first place.
P.S.—l saw in your paper some months ago a statement
that Miss Crossley (at Madame Mel> a's advice) was taking
lessons in boxing. This may lie a necessary precaution.
No similar announcement with regard to Miss Bull has
yet appeared.

» * * •
The British Government lias at last decided that
the Australian mints may coin silver for circulation in
Australia only. There is no profit in coining gold—in
fact, there is a slight loss ; and so the British Govt, has
for years allowed Australia to do most of the gold coinage
of the empire. But seeing there is only about 7d. worth
of silver in the alleged shilling silver coinage pays hand-
somely, and Premier Turner already calculates on making
£6JOO a year out of Victoria’s share of the business. As
gold coinage doesn’t pay and silver does it is obviously to
the interest of Australia in future to encourage the use
of the latter as much as possible. One simple way to do
this is to cease coining the half-sovereign, which is an un-
satisfactory bit of metal anyhow. It is mostly thin, bat-
tered and ancient—why the Australian sovereign is
always new, while the Australian half-ditto varies from
25 to’ 70 years of age, is a mystery ; also, it is too
much like a sixpence in a dim light. If the
sovereign could be abolished and replaced by a
40s. gold piece it would also be a great idea, but
unfortunately that seems impracticable just now. At
present, there is far too much gold in circulation in Aus-
tralia. Under an ideal system, gold would only be used
for export. The local circulation, apart from silver, would
he State paper, and the public would be content to know
that it could get gold for the paper if it wanted it; under
such conditions it probably wouldn’t want it—not often,
anyhow. Then the gold coinage would suffer much less
from wear-and-tear, and from loss by fire, shipwreck,
vanishment down drains, and mysterious disappearance.
If the State was the sole issuer of notes, and it could
induce their general use in place of gold, it would probably
make almost as large a profit by the unaccountable vanish-
ment of its paper obligations as it will do by the profits on
silver coinage.

Tiie Bank of New South Wales (now in its
82nd year—-it and the gallows being the only really
am-ient institutions in Australia) issues its balance-sheet
for the half-year ended 30th Sept., 1893, showing a net
profit of £88,042. There is a great sameness about the
profits shown by the B.N.S.W. They keep such a
mechanical regularity—just enough to pay the regular 9
per cent, and leave anominal fraction over—that a reason-
able inference is that the institution just saws off its
gross profits the exact hunk required for this purpose,
with a little variation at the edge to prevent absolute
monotony, and puts the rest away to write off losses, or
provide for possible losses, or strengthen the “ inner
reserves,” or something of the kind. The following shows
how this curious accuracy in earning the exact 9 per cent,
pans out: —
9 per cent.
Profits. Dividend. Balance.
September,’94 .. £78,614 ..£78,497 .. £ll7
March, ’95 .. 81,714 .. 81,621 .. 93
September,’9s .. 84,737 .. 84,536 .. 201
March,’9B .. 86,480 .. 86,330 .. 80
September, ’96 .. 87,661 .. 87,453 .. 203
March, ’97 .. 87,898 .. 87,727 .. 171
September, ’97 .. 89,462 .. 87,750 .. 1712
March,’9B .. 88,128 .. 87,750 .. 378
September, ’9S .. 88,042 .. 87,750 .. 292
In spite of bad times, however, the B.N.S.W.
still keeps the lead. The profits shown (£88,042) are equal
to £5 11s. 9d. percent, per annum on the shareholders’
funds (capital £1,950,000, reserve £1,200,000). Its 9 per
cent, dividend is the largest in the Seven Provinces, the
West Australian Bank (174 per cent.) only excepted. Its
Stock of coin (£5,010,078) is the biggest in the Seven Pro-
vinces, so ate its reserve fund, its deposit business and its
gross profits: though the Commercial of Australia,
National and Bank of N.Z. have larger capitals. Also it
is the oldest survivor among the banks of Australasia, so
its record has many good points about it.

The Cornwall Fire and Marine Insurance Co.
(Launceston, Tasmania) finished a bad year on 30th Sep-
tember. The Melbourne fire and the wreck of the China
both struck this office heavily. The net premiums of the
little institution were £11,985, and the total receipts were
£15,709. On the other hand, losses (£17,618) and expenses
(£3940) made the total outgoings £21,564, and the result
is a disastrous loss of £5855. The last seven years’ record
of the Cornwall reads thus :
Profits. Dividends.
1892 .. £8,116 .. 6J per cent. ..£5,000
1893 .. 6,116 .. „ „ .. 5,000
1894 .. 5,556 .. „ „ .. 5,000
1895 .. 6,122 .. „ „ .. 5,000
1896 .. 5,951 .. „ „ .. 5,000
1897 .. 469 .. £3/2/6 „ .. 2,500
Loss.
1898 .. 5,855 .. Nil.
Profit £26,475 £27,500
The Cornwall is 57 years old, and it never established a
reserve-fun J to meet possible mishaps like those of ’97
and ’9B. Also, it never established a re-insurance fund
till this year—the year when it had no funds available to
do it with. The only way it could he done was by these
two remarkable entries in the balance-sheet:
Liabilities. Assets.
Reinsurance Fund .. £3596 Accumulated Loss .. £4031
The result is that the Cornwall is £435 to the bad, and
has no re-insurance fund at all. It is a very solid concern
with £BO,OOO capital—which seems to he quite intact,
barring the £435 loss above-mentioned—but unless it sets
about establishing a reserve a time may yet come when
its capital won’t be intact by a long way. In proportion
to the business done the Cornwall’s capital is enormous,
which makes it a very safe concern for policy-holders ;
but. on the other hand, the business is so small that it
can’t earn 6j per cent, on so much capital except in years
of exceptional good luck. The 6) per cent, doesn’t look
an exorbitant div., but it certainly is exorbitant to a com-
pany in the Cornwall's circumstances. Everything is rela-
tive in this world. A fruit-barrow or a coffee-stall may
earn splendid dividends on the £5 capital really required
to work them, but if they are expected to earn dividends
on £lOO the situation becomes verv different.
• • * •
The Mutual Fire Insurance Co. (Tasmania) is a
quaint little concern which never publishes any state-
ment of its receipts from premiums or interest on invest-
ments, its losses, or expenses. Once a year it puts out a
statement of assets and liabilities, and that is about all.
For a long series of years it paid 10 per cent. Then in
1896 and ’97 the dividend fell to eight, and now it makes
a further drop to 6 per cent. As far as can be gathered
from the scanty information supplied, however, it seems
as if the year’s profits were £2040, and as the 6 per cent,
dividend absorbs -2250, the Mutual Fire Co. is apparently
sailing pretty close to the wind. In 1895 the co.’s re-
serves consisted of £16,260 (reserve, £lO,OOO ; contin-
gency fund, £1240; premiums on insurances unexpired
otherwise re-insurance account—£3ooo ; profit and less
balance after payment of dividend, £2020). Now they
stand at £17,528, so the Mutual Fire has made some pro-
gress. Still, the advancement of this co. in its 25 years’
existence hasn’t been very great, and it seems to have
given up all ambition to grow into a large enterprise,
and is content to pay most of its profits in dividends as it
goes along. The size of its business is unknown. Also,
whether the investments are good or bad is a matter for
speculation ; interest-receipts give some clue to the state
of the assets, but the Mutual Fire of Tasmania keeps the
amount of these buried in obscurity. Still, the little
concern seems to hold its own reasonably well.

Another pathetic fragment which has seen
better days, the Colonial Permanent, Building and Invest-
ment Society (Melbourne), issues its balance-sheet to
decompose in the hot sun of inspection. The result for
the year ended 7th Sept., ’9B, is a loss of £lO,lll, which
compares with some previous years as follows :
Profit. Dividends.
1592 .. .. £4,817 10 per cent .. £4,800
1893 .. .. 2,522 4 „ „ ... 1,920
ISJ4 .. .. 104 Nil
Loss.
1895 .. .. 1,501 Nil
1896 .... 823 „
1897 .. .. 920 „
1898 .. .. 10,111
Tiiis institution is 30 years old, so it isn't one of the mush-
rooms of the boom. Too big dividends, and too little
reserve fund, and too much deposit at. high interest in the
good days were the cause of its downfall. In 1893 it owed
£69,00 ) to depositors, and as it now owes £49,500 the assets
are fading away a great deal faster than the liabilities,
Over £11,500 of the £48,000 nominal capital is admittedly
lost. Also two-thirds of the assets consist of “properties
in possession, £75,234,” and judging by present appear-
ances they will pan out for so much less than their
nominal value that the Colonial isn’t likely to do much
more than pay its outside creditors. Of the £lO,lll loss
for the past year, £9327 was made by the sale of securities
which didn’t realise their paper-value, and the other £284
was owing to the society’s inability to earn its deposit-
interest. The latter small discrepancy might probably
be male good in some way, but if the society’s securi-
ties go on turning out like this its latter end will be
lamentable.
* • • *
Melbourne Brewery and Distillery Co., a huge
tanglefoot enterprise whose headquarters are now in Lon-
don, publishes a belated balance-sheet for the year 1897.
So far as can he gathered from the co.’s rather hazy way
of putting things it would seem that it lost £20,408 in
1896 and £30,425 in 1897. It seems to be working on
£132,330 of its own capital and £228,468 of money bor-
rowed at 5 per cent, and upwards. Also, it appears to
have lost £57,222 of its capital, and it certainly looks as
if three years more at the present rate of progress would
lose the rest of it. The entire available cash is £64.
Beer is in an unhealthy condition in Melbourne.

* * * *
Cane-cutters on the N.S.W. Northern rivers
make on an average about 6d. per hour. The Eight
Hours day is practically unknown among them, 12, 14 and
16 hours being a common working stretch. Sixteen hours
gives them Bs. a day, but 8 hours would only give
them 4s.

• * * •
“ In all cases in which a miner contracts to get
minerals by weight, the mineral so gotten shall he
weighed.” These words are in the 38th section of N.S.W.
Coal Mines Regulation Act, passed two years ago. There
is nothing there about miners asking the mineral to be
weighed. The courts have decided that there is not.
“The mineral shall be weighed.” Yet the Law moves
not, and the Reid Govt., by saving the Law will only move
in February next, is actually breaking the law as passed
by Parliament and interpreted by the courts.

true, are easily construable as unspeakable libels on a

quite a lot of people—who bought Lake George shares at
£2 or £3 —who would dearly like t.o hear the Lake George
directors and their late manager say their very worst
about one another in open court'.

Ed. Bulletin. —Re “ Shaker’s ” endorsement
(B. 19/IX/*9B) of statement that “ the Palmer would carry a
white population to-day—but for these slant-eyed glean-
ers.” After 24 years’ experience of New England tin
fields, I can say most emphatically that almost every
patch that has been worked by Chinese would have been
worked by whites, if left; and thus allow the fields to
sustain a white population very much longer. Also, it has
been quite a familiar sight to see whites tackle a show
abandoned by Chinese. There are now whole tracts on
these tin fields entirely aban loned by Ch nese, but still
being fossicked by white families who run a garden and a
few head of cattle to supplement the sluice-box. The
white could do his own scavenging on a gold or tin held
and rear some more citizens during the process.
Stannum.

The sustained upward rush in the tin market
and the excellent prospects that there won’t be much of a
slump, if any, have stirred speculators to search out the
most promising of old N.S.VV. tin-shows. Some of the
long-deserted Euriowie claims —among them a few in
which J. S. Reid dropped a pot of his Broken Hill profits
—have been pegged. But it is just as well to remember
that, though there has been this big recent rise of over
£2O (from i 62 to £B2 odd) in 12 months, the market has
not anything like recovered the position it held when
most of these mines were tried before.
* * * *
It is not only in the new lands that the spurt in
the tin-market has pricked up prospecting. The cen-
turies-old Tincroft ami Carn Brea, in Cornwall, is raising
£50,000 to put a new shaft down to the deepest levels and
to do other big works —this on the recommendation of
the veteran boss of Cornish mining, Capt. Josiah Thomas.
It looks, therefore, as if, in the opinion of the best
’totherside judges, the tin recovery had come to stay.
* * * *
B. H. Junction North directors have made a
capital choice of a new manager: Capt. R. N. Thomas,
for 10 years mine-manager of Block 10, goes over. Block
10 has had creeps to right of it and creeps to left of it,
but has never had one of its own—it is the only Broken
Hill mine that has escaped. Thomas goes to a property
which all the time wants a man who can do just that sort
of work. He is the oldest “ boss” along the line : he was
underground manager at the B.H. Prop. 14 years ago—
that was in the Wilsons' time.
* * * *
B.H. Junction directors tell a rather more
cheerful tale this half-year. At the end of March there
was an excess of liabilities over assets of 118,141; at the
enrl of September the excess was down to £8143, so that
the profits on the six months work amount to about
£10,0.10. For the six weeks since then the mill has done
better work on better ore, and accounts should be square
early in the new year. The financial statements leave a
good deal to be desired. Though depreciation of mill and
ore-breaking plant is very rapid nothing has been written
off for depreciation since September ’97 —a circumstance
which seems to show that the directors are very anxious
to put a smiling face on the co.’s affairs in view of the
Camden Syndicate’s continued efforts to persuade share-
holders to transfer the management to London. There
are two or three ancient fables about wolves and spiders
and things that might be more or less appropriately re-
ferred to at this point; but they are crowded out. Which
is just what may happen to Australian shareholders also
if they go to sleep.

Opal-buyers .send a sheaf of assurances to The
Bulletin that there are no very big profits in the gem-
buying business any more, and that the stories of the
huge profits of the past are also traceable to M. Rouge-
naont Grien, during that person's apprenticeship. Further,
it is submitted, amidst this paper’s hear-hears, that, if a
witness in a recent case said that a parcel that was worth
£lOO9 was sold for £BO, the point of most importance is
whether, at the time he made the estimate, the witness
was a competent judge of gems. All that the opal-miner
for his part knows for sure is that there is an enormous
difference in the price he is paid and the price that is
asked of him when he goes to buy an opal-brooch for his
best girl ; and, since he conies into contact only with the
opal-buyer, he observes that that person’s name is mostly
M»ses, and thereupon naturally gives him the credit for
it all.
The White Cliffs opal-miner’s affections are not,
however, devoted entirely to the gentlemen named
Moses and Reuben, and so bn. The English co. shares it
with the latter—partly because the English co. has spread
a big fist over the pick of the field, and partly because the
co.’s chairman has taken the trouble to excuse the failure
of the concern to reach the giddy heights mentioned in
the prospectus by describing the workmen on the field
as wholesale robbers. Some of the sins laid at the co.’s
door have, however, been removed during the last year
or two ; notably trihutors (most of the ground is worked
on tribute) now have the selling of the gem they raise,
the co.’s manager simply putting a reserve on it.
* * * *
If it is not simply a Rongemontade about those
W.A. mines filling their stopes with cyanide tailings, the
statement deserves a little more public attention.
Cyanide of potassium gases are virtually poisonous. The
naturalist, for instance, uses a tiny piece at the bottom of
a stoppered bottle to kill specimens (large beetles, even)
without damaging their coloring, as spirits do. The
fumes, even in a well-aired store above ground, are very-
injurious to health ; and to vitiate the hot, badly-venti-
lated stopes in a deep mine with these reeking sands, is
nothing less than wholesale murder. The miners in such
places are worse off than the phosphorus or lead-workers,
and it will not take many months either to prove this
assertion. But no sane inspector of mines in a civilised
community would imagine such a practice possible out-
side of a nightmare.

Melb. Exchange is engaged upon a small and
shaky-looking revival of last year’s boom in Tasmanian
silver stocks. Crown Lyells had got back as far as 14s. at
time of writing, and such doubtful outside shows as the
Central Lyells and Queen Lyells were again fashionable
in “penny rise and ‘ bob ’ foolish ” circles. “Bound to
last until Xmas,” is the fascinating bait to catch minnows.
Twelvemonths back the boom certainly did last until
Xmas and for a week or two after the “House” re-
assembled. This year the rush to get out is likely to
happen before the holidays.

Dear Bulletin.— Re par., 12/11/’9B, on Tas.
Smelting Co. A director certainly was present at half-
yearly meeting of Hercules shareholders, but his “ strong”
argument to induce sale of ore was the then high market-
rate of metals. This the shareholders evidently thought
too strong, as it would take them months to arrange for
regular output; moreover, the Tas. smelters were not
prepared to take delivery then, nor can they start
operations before February or March next, despite
the fact that the “ experts ” assured the Zeehan pro-
ducers that they would be smelting in October last.
The assertion that “ the co. (Tas. Smelting) will soon be
interested in most of the going mining concerns on the
West Coast, and with unlimited capital at its back can
make its own terms,” is erroneous. It certainly won’t
pay the Hercules people to smelt at Zeehan, with the Tas.
Copper Co.’s works close by, and, in fact, if No. 5 level
pans out to expectations, they will probably treat their
own stuff if capital can be raised. They, like most Zeehan
Cousin Jacks, don’t seem partial to Sauer Kraut.
Again, smelters cannot touch the low-grade ores concen-
trated by the mines, of which there is naturally a greater
quantity than firsts, and the local buyers, who aren’t
asleep, run each other and an.yone else who comes along
for the product. Those in the know smile a smile of evil
omen when they hear of the large quantities of low-grade
ore available in mines that were abandoned years ago and
are now full of water; consequently the “experts” had to
rely a good deal on hearsay. As this sometimes diverges
slightly from fact, the denouement will be interesting.—
Badger.

* * * *
Clermont (Q.) is the alluvial field of Australia.
Last year only about 400 diggers were on the ground ;
latest returns for the district put the number at 5000. All
said to be making tucker, and some a bit over.
* • • •
A member of the late Q. Mining Commission
recently amused the House vastly with a description of
Durham on the Etheridge—a good old smellful wild-cat
property of previous days. Company sent out most
elaborate machinery including ten managers, salaries
ranging from £l5OO to £7OO. These gentlemen bossed 60
men, a number afterwards reduced to eight— though the
ten managers still flourished as before. Lovely offices
rose as if by magic, and a brick banqueting-hall lent an
air of palatial grandeur to the property. After a while
miners’ wages were reduced and contract let to six men to
sink at a rate which enabled these heroes to make at the
rate of £2llos. per week apiece, and all was joy and
beery felicity. And now— not a single foot of the Durham
lease can be found without the inevitable glass-bottle.

A novelty is reported from Bundaberg, Q.
Through the suction pipe of the river dredge several
pieces of opal have been drawn from the bed of the River
Burnett —one or two pieces being reported as of fine
quality. Captain Almond, the Port Master, has taken
samples to Brisbane.

To The Bulletin from Wandi gold-field,
Northern Territory :—Wandi gold-field, situated about : 0
miles east of Pine Creek, the railway terminus of Fort.
Darwin (N.T.) railway, is the only field in the N. Ter itory
with a European population, the Chinese being prohibited.
A large extent of country, plenty of reefs of a decent
size, but, generally low-grade (about loz.), and battery
charges 255. a ton. Since erection of lb-head battery, 18
months ago, 1610 tons stone have been crushed for the
puhlic ; yield, 180Coz. retorted gold, value £3 10s. per oz.
Climate healthy. A good opening for investment of
mining capital and energy.
© * * *
The new battery of 40 stamps lately started at
the Galleymont, gold-mines is one of the finest gold mill-
plants at work in N.S.W., and is well worth inspection by
parties contemplating erection of similar machinery. The
ore is fed automatically through rockbreakers and feedert
without shovelling, arid the whole is most substantially
built. The engines are a fine pair of horizontal compound
type, driving the battery with ropes. Mr. M. Eissler,
the managing engineer, speaks in the highest terms of
the construction and design of the plant, which was built
by the Austral Otis Engineering Company, of South
Melbourne.*
• • * •
South Australia has been pretty thoroughly
probed for copper ; and, though the present little boom
has not therefore induced much prospecting in new coun-
try, the old districts are being overhauled again. An
English co. has had a report made on the old New Corn-
wall, in Karlina district; the Moonta itself is doing more
prospecting, with results that come quite up to expecta-
tion ; and the Yelpa and Flinders, the latter in the long-
neglected Tumby district, have been revived.
* * * *
S. R. Wakefield, who died suddenly in Adelaide
lately, was the agent appointed by the Moonta Copper co.
to introduce competent miners to that province in order
to open up those mines. When they heard of this, the
few hands then employed promptly threw down their
tools and came to the surface ; and it took all the then-
newly-appointed Supt. Hancock’s eloquence to get them
to return. That was South Australia’s first mining strike.
• • • •
Now that Cap’n Hancock has left Moonta
mines, where he was manager for 31 years, probably
W. R. Williams, of Johnson’s No. 1, Bendigo, has been as
long at one property as any Australian manager ; his
period of service is 32 years.
• * * •
Mr. Robert Shand, sec. of the Australian Forge
and Engineering Co. Ltd, Williamstown, and attorney for
Mr. Mephan Ferguson, engineer, etc., of Melbourne,
states that neither of these establishments has ever been
so busy as at present. Nearly every department is work-
ing day and night. Firstly, there is ihe patent machinery
now being made ready for shipment to W.A. for manu-
facturing the new rivetless or locking bar pipes required
in the Coolgardie water scheme. The spiral riveiting
machines are working on an order for about 15 miles of
10 and 12 inch pipes for the Maryborough Water Trust.
Also, the firm has in hand an order for 300 iron goods
waggons for the Vic. railways.*

Last week a young man (supposedly very
well-off)—after courting a girl for five years, fur-
nishing a house, ordering the wedding-breakfast,
and bringing all his friends and the girl’s relatives
to a Sydney church to see the wedding—suddenly
•bolted in his nuptial garments, without luggage
and without notice, by s.s. Alameda, leaving the
poor bride-elect to faint and hysterieise in church.
“Inexplicable!” says his friends. But it isn’t!
It was probably one of those fits of overwhelming
bashfulness in which a man would rather go to
Slieol direct than stand up in front of a gaping
crowd and promise to be fruitful and multiply,
and go through all the dreadful circumstance of
“ getting spliced.”
* • * *
On the day that George Waldron (the late
Sydney City Solicitor) died he was at Rosehill
races in bicycle costume. He and an acquaint-
ance were talking about a mutual friend who had
died lately, and Waldron said: “I never could
understand what caused that man’s sudden
death.” “ I can tell you,” said the acquaintance
—“ it was taking hard rides on a bicycle in hot
weather without being fit for it. He used to ride
up hills and strain and struggle and blow, and it
affected his heart.” “ That’s a nice thing to say,”
said Waldron, “ when I’ve gone in for cycling.
I suppose you think it will kill me.” “ Just as
likely as not,” said the other. Waldron laughed,
shook hands, got on his bicycle, and—dropped
dead'off it within half-an-hour. The man who
gave him that warning is the writer of this para-
graph.
* * • *
A Bulletin corespondent ;
The death of Harvey Hay at Ooolangatta last week by
a gun accident was another instance of the malignity of
chance. Hay was 22 years of age, a fine, healthy young
fellow, strong as a horse, a good rider, oarsman and
athlete generally. He was fully accustomed to firearms,
and yet, cantering along upon a ryuet horse on good
going, he managed to shoot himself through the head.
On the morning of the accident he woke a friend who
was staying with him, and asked him to come out to shoot,
a scrub bull which had been giving trouble. They rode
all. the morning without finding the bull. Then
the friend wanted to return to Sydney, hut Hay
persuaded him to stay, ran in two fresh horses,
and t.hev went out again. Late in the day they
saw the bull a long way off, and Hay took a speculative
shot at him at very long range, without result. Then
they cantered steadily on round some swampy ground to
meet the beast. While they were riding along the friend
(who >vas about 50yds. ahead) heard the rifle go off, and,
looking round, saw Hay on the ground. The ball had
gone right through his head, but he was conscious and
lived several hours. No one knows how the accident
happened. The stirrup-leather of his saddle was broken,
and that, giving way unexpectedly, may have caused him
to have overbalanced, and in the fall he may have let-off
the rifle; hut even then it was hideously bad luck
that it should have exploded at the very moment
it was pointing at him. There is no law of
probabilities in connection with gun-accidents. It
was a thousand to one against the stirrup-leather
breaking, ten to one that a rider as good as Hay would
not fall off when it did break, a hundred to one that the
rifle would not explode when falling, and a thousand to
one against its hitting the man who was holding it. And
yet a malign fate safely landed all these odds, and so
ended the career of a fine young fellow with all his
chances before him—while any drunken fool with a
double-barrelled shot-gun can race and rip and yell and
swish it about and yet get home unscathed. The man
who mounts a horse with a loaded gun should remind
himself of Hay’s fate and of the death of young Lord
Ancratn and unload that gun. It may seem safe enough
in all human probability; but gun-accidents have a pro-
bability of their own—an inhuman probability that no
man can reckon with.
• » * •
A humorous execution is a thing unknown
to the present generation, but Pastor Elliott, who
“saw off” Archer, the Ulupna murderer, at Melb.
last week, nearly turned the strangulation of that
particularly cold-blooded person into a howling
farce. Substituting verses from the New Testa-
ment for the usual burial-service, this pioneer
parson even subjected them to “ managerial cuts
and emendations,” and, as Archer was led on the
drop, he astounded everybody by observing that
“God so loved Alfred Archer that he gave His
only begotten son that Alfred Archer might be
saved.” Which brought the whole ghastly affair
perilously near anti-climax, and it was only “ the
dull, sickening thud ” that put things straight
again.
* * * *
As a matter of precaution, it would,
appear safer to be a penitent R.C. than a
penitent Protestant, while awaiting the advent of
the hangman. At least, such seems to be the
lesson of the Archer case. Although the devoted
clergyman who attended Archer cannot be said to
have blabbed a sacred secret, it is extremely doubt-
ful if a R.C. priest worth his salt, the wide-world
over, would have said as much as he did, even to
ease the consciences of a Cabinet. Who knows
what the reside of the deputation of 5000 persons
to Gov. Brassey, which had been arranged for the
eve of the execution, would have been had the
Government not been armed with the parson’s
assurance that prisoner had said that the sentence
was a just one? The mention of this prevented
the deputation from going on with its mission,
and the sheriff was able to perform his duties un-
disturbed. By the way, had Archer known that
the. mention of this admission would have utterly
obliterated all chance of a reprieve, would he have
told the parson ? And, if he wouldn't have told
him under such circumstances, was the parson,
however well-intentioned, within his moral rights
in making it known ?
THE RIGHT WORDS AT THE RIGHT
MOMENT.
PARTICULAR OLD MAID : “ Are you sure that
these fish are quite fresh? ”
PATSY THE FISH-HAWKER : “ Yes, as fresh as yer
are yerself, Miss.”
Max O’Rell says that the criminal is a
canting hypocrite, and that his exit from life per
medium of the scaffold is always a pose. The
English one invariably calls upon his God and the.
French invokes the memory of his mother.
Archer, the recently-hanged Strathmerton mur-
derer, concludes his confession of a cruel and cold-
blooded crime with, “This, then, is the reward
for my actions—betrayed by the woman I loved,
condemned by man, and abandoned by God.”
Archer should never have been hanged. He
should have been spared to write blood-curdling,
“syndicated ” fiction for the daily newspapers.
The Age, moralising comfortably on the
hanging of Archer, asks “ what are we to do with
such criminals if we abolish hanging?” What)
cannot be cured must be immured.
JRe the case of James Broadfoot, who went
mad (perhaps “madder” would he more correct)
after undergoing “ solitary ” at Pentridge prison,
and is now located at Kevv asylum. The authori-
ties, anxious to demonstrate that his prison treat-
ment had nothing to do with his insanity, explain
that although he was kept apart and given no
work to do, he “had access to religious books
which the chaplain of the denomination to which
he belonged set aside for his use.” That satisfac-
torily accounts for everything. The prisoner had
no option. The official explanation deserves to
stand with that of the Grand Inquisitor in “The
Gondoliers,” who says that the old woman on the
rack in the torture-chamber will not feel lonely as
she has all the illustrated papers.
* * ♦ *
The Presbyterian General Assembly de-
scribes the lotteries held in connection with the
R.C. church as “ prostitution of charity.” Better
any sort of charity than none at all. Curiously
enough, while the Presb. Assembly was denounc-
ing the R.C. style of raising funds for charitable
purposes, the Anglican Congress at Ballarat was
laying down the dictum that “ Puritan theology
failed to permanently impress the masses of man-
kind.” There is no bane without its antidote.

* * * •
One of the most crying scandals con-
nected with the Polynesian labor - traffic is
the continual employment of “boys” at work
legally forbidden. To prevent this, Queensland
Govt, issued orders some years ago that the
Polynesian inspectors should visit the planta-
tions now and again to see that the Kanakas
*vere confined to field labor. This is how it
pans out in practice : Before making a visit,
the inspector always courteously wires the
planter, “ Intend to pay you a visit on Wed-
nesday next.” On Wednesday next the
boys are all duly cleared out of the mills
and off the drays into the cane, and the
official (who usually puts up at the planter’s
house) is able to report that all is right
with a clear conscience.
Another phase of the Polynesian
question. Tommy Tanna falls ill. By
rights, his employer should send him to
the Kanaka hospital (charge, 4s. 6d. a
day per head). But this is too expen-
sive. Wherefore employer (if he has a
number of “boys”) keeps a medicine-
man, often an ex-overseer, to doctor
them out of a drug-chest. Salts
and senna for all diseases.

‘ * Trebless ” : On recent Syd-
ney holiday dropped on unique
game of kiss-in-the-ring. Eight
buxom girls, a lad of about
twelve, and a young fellow on
crutches. Pathetic, such a
meagre supply of “he”!
Watched until every girl had
had a kiss from the lad and the
cripple. The girls were very
considerate, when running from the latter. They
could have shown even a pony-owner how artis-
tically to “run a bye.’ One girl— she was plain
—had to trip and fail to allow of capture.

Rumored that much-needed improvements
are to be made in the Anglo-Australian press
cable service. The personal reference to com-
parative nobodies is to be put down with a high
hand, so that when next Mr. Jones, of Sydney,
or Mr. MacSmiff, the Flinders-lane ragman,
visits England his doings will not be chronicled at
4s. lid. per word in Australian papers for the
mutual exasperation of a million profane male adult
citizens of the Commonwealth. The rock on
which the cable service chiefly seems to founder is
the practice of dispatching a uniform number of
words per diem irrespective of the importance of
the news. To the ordinary person the saner
arrangement would appear to be to limit the
length of the cables when news is unimportant,
and to draw on these verbal economies when a
“ sensation ” comes along.

Brisbane’s Mayoress, following Southern
example, gave her initial reception at the local
Exhibition last week. Sundry strong “ Society ”
hints as to “class” went for naught; and the
local female simply swarmed all over the hall and
wolfed the provisions, what time some of the
Nicest mournfully stood apart, and finally went
home dry in the interior and bedraggled in the
skirt.

WHOM GOD DOTH JOIN.
O Rite, whose holy tale is done,
O Ring, thou tender token,
O Priest whose lips pronounce us one,
O Church whose vows are spoken—
What thunders fall in Sinai-call
From ev’ry echoing groin—
“ Let no man put asunder them
Whom God doth join ! ”
O Life that laughs in ruddy health,
O sturdy forms that bound me,
O Race robust whose vital wealth
In vigor beats around me—
What voices rise in thunder-wise
From ev’ry crest and coign—
“ Whom God hath put asunder—them
No man shall join I ”
Q.
P. LUFTIG.

An unmarried woman gave birth to trip-
lets at Marriekville, Sydney, last week. On these
interesting occasions the “ Queen’s Bounty, ’ £3,
is generally paid by His Ex. the Governor, but as
this mother is outside the pale, the money will
presumably not be granted in her case, although
she is in desperate need.

To The Bulletin from the Far North :
The newly-arrived Jap. is working his little brown self
into the hearts of the ladies. At a Mossman (N.Q.)
recent “social” the Jap. interpreter (of “flesh of a,
female fowl ” fame) and several other cultivated
brownies were invited, and delighted the company
with Japanese dances in costume. After the local re-
citer had finished butchering “ Pardon, the Son of Re-
prieve,” the Jap. interpreter was asked how he liked it.
“ Really,” said brown man politely, “ I don’t thoroughly
understand what it was about.” By the way, a central
mill official, who is used to driving Fijian coolies, finding
some Japs, “spelling” in a cane-field jumped off his
horse and began flogging those nearest, but before he
could say Yokohama he had the little brown men dancing
round him with cane-knives. He took the hint promptly
and “ cleared ” However undesirable the Japs, are in Aus-
tralia, it must be admitted that, unlike kanakas and
coolies, they’re plucky and won’t stand knocking-about
from any one. The 30 or 40 white men who had three or
four months’ employment at firewood-cutting this year
for the mill will now' have to travel and cadge, because
the job is in future to be done by Japs. Where will this
Kanaka-Chow-Jap. business end?

Would give each constable the hump

“T.D.’\ Atßel-
more Markets, Syd-

The place is mostly used by

By the way, I have often wondered
whether the average Chinaman ever
washes himself. Have never observed a Chow

A parsonic letter in a Sydney daily reveals
the sad fact that while a man can lead his sister-
in-law to the altar, a woman may not under any
circumstances espouse her deceased husband’s
brother. Nobody quite knows the reason of this
distinction. One argument: let a woman marry
her brother-in-law and why not her father-in-law
also? The converse argument does not apply to
the other side, because no man ever does want to
marry his mother-in-law —except to the other fel-
low. The whole thing rests on a vague idea of
scriptural disapproval, which is funny enough,
seeing that in the days of the patriarchs a widow
was absolutely commanded to marry her brother-
in-law, and if he wouldn’t respond he was to be
smacked in the face with a shoe. The Bulletin
likewise is somewhat weary of the want of-delicacy
argument which is invariably brought up against
this sort of marriage, and against re-marriages,
and against various other marriages. As for that
matter, there is a certain want of delicacy in
getting married at all. [Barrister Ralston, who
should by now be a divorce-expert, avers that
marriage with a deceased husband’s brother is
legal in N.S.W.]
• • • *
At Newtown (Syd.) police-court recently a
man was gaoled with hard labor for having “ no
lawful occupation. ” But before a man could be
imprisoned it ought to be necessary to prove that
he has an unlawful occupation. Mere want of
exployment, according to this decision, is a statu-
tory offence.
Re the Parramatta girl whose purse was
snatched and herself knocked down and danced
upon, woman’s sartorial encouragements to the
art of thieving have been many and various.
First, the pocket at the back ; then the handbag,
generally gaping open ; now the long, leather
purse which won’t fit into her pocket. Present
writer saw in one cable-tram the other day seven-
teen women, thirteen of whom had each a purse in
her hand ; the other four, poor things, looked as
if they didn’t possess purses. Such is the force of
Fashion. And woman never learns by experi-
ence ; that Parramatta girl, as soon as she gets
rid of her black-eye, will reappear just as before,
smilingly holding out her money-bag for the
acceptance of the casual passer-by.

Professor Anderson Stuart before Sydney's
most fashionable ladies’ club :
Where a mother cannot suckle her baby I have little to
say, but where she will not, she is doing herself no good,
and she is possibly doing her offspring a deal of harm.
Part of the cycle of a mother’s life is to suckle her child ;
part of the cycle of the life of every individual is to be
suckled by the mother, and as surely as this order is
interfered with, so surely will Nemesis bringa penalty for
the crime against Nature—and the pity of it is that it is
not always the guilty party who suffers most.
Two touching instances of
maternal affection turned up in
Melb., the other day. A couple of
nine-year-old youngsters were ar-
rested as neglected children, and,
appearing respectively before the
(say) West and E. Melb. police-
courts, were remanded until the
same day pending enquiries. At the
West court, at the time fixed, the
lovin’ mother of one lad turned up,
and had a shock-headed urchin
shoved into her arms, with the
injunction to take him home, and
let him wander no more. The
woman gave a puzzled glance at
the boy, and then, turning to the
policeman at the door, whispered
timorously, “ But —sir—this isn’t
my little boy.” Incredulous and
busy, the constable had no time to
discuss such an idiotic remark.
“Nonsense, woman,” he said, with
a dramatic wave of his hand.
“ Clear out home. Don’t you know
your own child?” With feminine
respect for the divinity that hedges
a uniform the mother heaved a
sigh, and, trying hard to discover
the features of her offspring in the
discharged prisoner, she obeyed the
order—the word of a policeman was
something that could not be ques-
tioned. Certainly the boy seemed
to lack every characteristic of the
one she used to know, but she made
no demur until a friend arrived a
week later, and exclaimed “ But
that’s not your boy.” Delighted at
this unexpected testimony, the fond
mother replied, “ That’s what I
said; but the policeman said he
was.” The friend was of less com-
plaisant nature, and bullied the
mother into kicking up a row with
the authorities. The result was
that enquiries were started, and it
was found that while the first mother
had accepted the second’s son as her
own at West Melb., No. 2 had
taken the first’s without the slightest
question at East Melb., and had al-
most immediately handed over the
kid to the Scotchbyterian or some
other mission, which had given him
some clothes and sent him off to the
inaccessible selection of some cocky
in the wilds of the mallee. It
took three weeks to fetch him back,
sort the mixed youngsters out, and
hand them over to their proper mothers. It
was eventually found that they had been mixed
in the Department for Neglected Children during
the remand and sent to the wrong courts.

“Bargo”: Quite agree with suggestion
that Federal capital should be in some torrid
region. Untempered by the gentle influence of
beer, our laws would be drawn on Hardshell-Bap-
tist lines, and in a hot climate Bung is an essen-
tial.
* * • *
Some people are capable of forgetting any-
thing. The Victorian man whose little daughter
had her scalp torn off recently by some machinery
hurried her off to a doctor four miles away—but
quite disremembered to bring the scalp with
him. So the pathetic little sufferer had to wait
while somebody rushed back for the missing article
and brought it along.

* * * •
A very silent funeral occurred recently in
W. Tamar (Tas.) district. An old female resident
was interred without the help of a parson or
Scriptural word, and the only remark uttered was
that of the defunct lady’s son, who observed,
“ Well, that’s the first time I ever saw a person
buried like a dog ! ”

A Bulletin correspondent on the kilt
mania
Was recently in ’bus with two spectacled virgins when
a “ Sydney Scottish ” in uniform started to climb down
stairs from the top. One of the vestals was sitting in
the back corner when suddenly, in close proximity to her
cheek, appeared a muscular and hirsute calf. She
skipped back about a yard along the seat, but still kept
her eyes on the phenomenon, and when it was
duly joined by its fellow whipped off her
darkened glasses to get a better view. Then
when a kilt and a red head had duly accompanied
the legs, the pair found breath to ejaculate, “ Gracious
goodness I—how1 — how shook-ing ! ” All the same, I think the
local “ Highlanders ” are a bit shocking. There is no
offence to anyone’s eye in the spectacle of a pair of mus-
cular legs, tanned by the sun and wind. The tan is, as
it were, a guarantee of bona-fides—the man has evidently
been accustomed to wear kilts. But none of the Sydney
Scottish are brown-legged—their limbs, on the contrary,
are painfully pallid. The owners have evidently just
taken off their trousers in order that they may show
their legs to the public. It is downright indecent ex-
posure. Let the military authorities call tenders for a
coat of kalsotnine, or some dadoes, for Tonald’s shanks.

Hobart boasts a rabid teetotaller as Mayor.
The other day when the one-armed athlete, G.
M. Schilling, who is walking round the world for
an alleged wager of 5000d01., reached Jamville,
the Mayor asked him what he drank when on the
road. “ Water,” replied the Yank. Then His
Worship delivered a homily to the crowd on the
wonderful effects of cold water. “If our visitor
“IT IS THE CAUSE, IT IS THE CAUSE, MY SOUL!"
“ Adelina Footlytes, what makes you so hard? ”
“ Years of dealing with theatrical managers, dear hoy."
can walk all around the world on water, surely
other people can walk the streets of Hobart with-
out any stronger beverage,” he brogued. Then
the Yank, who really likes a glass of good brown
ale between walks, realised that he had fallen in.
As, to win his wager, he must neither beg, borrow
nor spend, the Mayor’s proclamation of the pedes-
trian’s teetotalism had the effect of cutting off
from him the flow of free beer.

A Sydney D.T. correspondent relates that
“ A young girl in her teens was engaged as cashier
and assistant at a city grocer’s. Her hours were
from 7.15 a.m. to 9.30 p.m. ; on Saturday till 11
and 11.15 p.m. She had no evenings off, and
was standing all that time, only a short interval
for dinner and tea ” 1 And the benevolent Miss
Rose Scott, speaking at N.S.W. National Council
of Women, told of a grocer’s assistant in a leading
city shop who started work at 8 a.m., and seldom
finished before 9.15 p.m., while on Fridays he
worked till 10.30 p.m., and on Saturdays till 11.15
p.m. That man got off two nights weekly—one
night at 6 o'clock and one at 7. Many
grocers worked 73J hours a week. A master-
butcher in the suburbs stated that he and his men
worked daily from 6.30 a.m. to 9 p.m.—on Satur-
day till 11 p.m. The lot of the men was hard ;
but the lot of the shop-girls was harder.

That ghastly institution, the male beauty
show, prospers in Melbourne. The other day
Brighton Branch of the A.N.A. gave a prize for
the nicest man at. a local flare-up, and the pink
Johnnie with the large, China-blue eye appropria-
ted the prize, which was a pot of rouge, or a packet
of pearl powder, or something. The Bulletin is
not sorry for the man pursued through life by the
reputation of having taken a prize in a beauty
show. It is a dreadful fate, but probably he
merits it. Writer here recalls the honest pride
with which a man introduced himself in a Cres-
wick bar. “ What, dontcher know me ? Good
! Why, I’m the feller what took first prize
fer ugliness at the ’ibernian sports.” “Glad to
meet you,” said the barman, “you deserve')
it ! ”
WHAT PROFITS?
What profits all the wealth and fame
The applauding world can give,
The light of an illustrious name
When the heart has ceased to live ?
The heart that once has ceased to glow
With love’s consuming fire
Has perished with the long ago
That fueled its desire.
O gracious eyes so blue, so blue ;
O lips I oft have pressed—
The burning heart that worshipped you
Is dead within this breast.
ARTHUR A. D. BAYLDON.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
, r _ Sydney, November 23, 1533.
Mr Dear Myee—
Everyone is telling everyone else the “ real ”
reason of the Hampdens’ sudden Hitting. I glean
y<nt the following few, carefully omitting several
which have malice and absurdity written on their
very face. Sudden scare that one of the charming
Misses Lrand was going to wed a "colonial”
nobody.
(Some such reason is trotted out when any
magnate leaves Australia, and was duly set down
as the cause of the Jerseys’ departure.) The
rude remarks of the press. A municipal bicker
that refuses to be damped. And—but why should
people go such a long, long way for the motives of
this very simple action? Surely anyone can
believe that His Ex. is tired of the billot, and
wants a rest from his labors.
Labors? I should think so. Stone-breaking
isn't in it with sitting from eight till eleven
in the front row at dreary charity concerts, and
never even the mitigating circumstances of an
interlude. T have an idea on this subject.
There’s a beautiful butler at Government House
with an architectural nose and quite an ancestral
backbone, and if the coining Gov. will only
begin from the very beginning and send this
beauteous being everywhere he doesn't want to
go himself, the butler will soon come to be equal
to His Ex. As long as advts. read " Government
House will be present ” the draw will proceed as
usual. Then, too, think of the ease with which
Potts Point will converse with vice-regality !
Captain Sloans Stanley, who went olf to a
family wedding a few mouths back, is not likely
now to return. Serious little Capt. Eiolden, a
popular aide, who has never given himself any
airs whatever, likes Sydney, and will stay on here
if he can. Some people say th it Lady Hampden
is altogether as popular as Lady Carington, but
the matter can't be definitely settled" till the
Hampdens are out on the briny. At present Her
Ex.’s kind, restful manner, and her daughters’
habit of rustling in and out of tlm crowd, and
talking to everyone about them, are doing much
havoc with the affections of the Beautiful Harbor.
Two fetes came along last week, both for
charities. The first was given for the Industrial
School at Manly, an institution which cares for
little girl waifs, and teaches them to earn an
honest brown. Catholics run the institution, and
I must say the hundred and twenty little girls all
look as if they like being there. I saw some mar-
vellous stockings knitted by these little hands,
and the specimens of needlework for sale at the
stalls might have raised the blush of envy on any
grandmother's cheek. Another sort of blush
came into well-regulated faces when certain gaudy
purple, orange, and canary-silk garments turned
out to be nightgowns. In fact, one uncommonly
well regulated lady observed loudly that she
thought it was ’ighiy immoral to let the children
make’em in sich colors. "Wc did not think of
that, ” said a sister, sweetly.
Lady Hampden and Miss Dolly Brand jour-
neyed down by special launch to set the
fete going. A drizzling rain fell, but society
turned out bravely ; and the Cardinal-
wonder what the Cardinal thinks of orunyc
nightgowns, if he ever thinks of them ? —Major
Bryan, and a few other men, endured the usual
gentle afternoon persecution. Nobody wore any-
thing in particular. Lady Hampden's dark-blue
silk was a familiar friend, and Miss Brand wore a
Cup muslin. The biggest swarm of women
hovered round an exquisite hand-made trousseau,
the undergarments all of soft white silk, which
was being rattled at sixpence a head. I heard that
the tickets for this rai* into quite a sensational
number of thousands.
Mrs. Oliver, of Neutral Bay. lent her waterside
grounds for the second fete of the week. This was
opened rather sumptuously by. Lord Hampden,
with the Admiral and Julius Knight thrown in,
but did not draw the public to any great extent.
It was ail extra-attractive fete too, with pretty
girls abounding. Miss Maud Gollin in a flowing
muslin robe, - pretty Miss Oliver in a pink apron,
Miss Una Russell in a yello.w polonaise and white
skirt, those two Gibsonesque maidens, Hope and
Ada Mayne in white muslin arid poke bonnets,
and a dark beauty in a daring green hat, sold
buttonholes and cigars unceasingly. Ernest Hus-
king performed skillfully in a side tent, little
comediettas went on at intervals, and yet biz was
dolefully dull, and depression looked out from
every stall.
The canker in the rose was a church grievance.
It seenn that the local church people didn't smile
on the fete because its proceeds were to go toother
churches and hospitals. Somebody took it upon
herself to write to Lord Hampden and the
Admiral and warn them not to lend their patron-
age to a false, deceptive fete that would take money
from the starving parish’s own collection-plates.
His Ex. declined to be upset, and wrote a neat
note to the fete owners saying that any maii
could do as he liked in his own bick garden, and
that he would bring along his patronage all right.
The mitch-advertised J ulius Kn igh t contribution
turned out to be the pseudo-poem about Mary
Queen of Scots, and how the "scene was changed.”
There was also a witty prologue from the pen of
Mr. Alex. Oliver, which Julius wanted to spout to
His Ex. in the middle of the lawn. Also, a cer-
tain young hero wore a big black frown all after-
noon—allegedly because nobody received him on
his arrival, and because the crowd wasn't as big
as it ought to have been considering Who was
coming.
Mr. Laurence Campbell’s third Annual Exhibi-
tion drew the usual crowded “invitation ” mob to
the Y.M.C.A. on Monday night. The hall was
filled from floor to ceiling with an audience that
sat patiently through a programme of IS items
without an interval, and listened patiently to the
different little or big girls tell the same tale of
woe in half-a-dozen voices, and with half as main-
gestures among them all. The' night was too hot
for so much death and bloodshed, and it was with
a big sigh of relief that one followed Miss Gladys
Pockley to a school examination, where there was
no greater tragedy than the bishop’s distress at
being asked “ how many legs has a caterpillar
got?” In the scene from King John, Hubert
de Burgh appeared in the short frock and black
stockings of a little girl of eight, and Arthur was
a younger sister in socles. These two little girls,
Miss Vera and Gladys Rich, carried off all the
honors and flowers in their baby hands. But the
flower of the Hook was the lady who recited “The
Passions ” to a piano accompaniment by Ernest
Truman. The stereotyped style was here deserted
for a cleverly conceived sketch of different—not
too unladylike—emotions.
An ingenue, Miss Shadforth Ilooper, gave a
bright little pianoforte recital during the week to
a crowded roomful of musical people. A romantic
elegance of style, characteristic of Signor do Beau-
ptiis s pupils, took the place of any tiery slapping
and hitting, and the Wagner-Liszt Tannhauser
was a particularly admirable performance. Mr.
Sydney Hoben’s concert was also a successful mat-
ter, the programme being systematically encored.
Since Carrington got to London, it seems that
Australia lias turned green in the manner peculiar
to all far fields. If the Baron's flashy speech Inis
any effect in England, and the large-footed dam-
sel with a nose, and a theory, and a red, red
cheek, really swarms these regions in search of
husbands, there 11 be a sad side to the now humor-
ous episode. For Sydney and Melbourne certainly
can t supply the necessary. Every man with over
three pounds a week is ring-barked already. In fact,
“eligible ” is now applied to those three-hundred a-
year-young-men who used to be as dragons to the
dowagers in the good old days. As for the back-
blocks, it takes oven a cast iron acclimatised Aus-
tralian woman a lifetime to recover from the wilt-
ing scorch of an out-back summer. Imagine nice,
clean Englishwomen in a land where a bath was
a wicked luxury, if not an utter impossibility.
And, from an entirely Australian point of view,
think bow mosquitoes would wax and multiply on
all that good red blood ! And we don't want to
owe a new breed of giant skeeters to the nation
to which we are already indebted for rabbits.
How’s this for high ! A little girl, dragging
along a miserable dog at the end of a string,
called recently at a suburban bouse and poured
forth a well rehearsed tale about a dead father,
sick mother and seventeen half-starved brothers
an 1 sisters,.finishing up with a request for cash.
When this was refused, she added, “ I’lease,
mother said would you be so kind as to give some-
thing towards the registering of our little dorg ! ”
I hose delightful Pieyel concerts wound up their
Winter season last week with a strong, short pro-
gramme. In this city of two-paged programmes
and double encores the concert that lets you out
before ten cannot be too hotly encouraged, and as
these little Chamber-music feasts are always ex-
cellent, I hope next year will bring the Committee
a bigger financial harvest. Signor do Beaupuis
(de 80-puss, a fat lady behind me, would call him)
played the piano part in the Scliumum quintette,
an 1 also put a few touches to the well-worn Men-
delssohn's Rondo Capriccioso. But I was sorry
this delightful pianist didn’t play something not
known by heart of everybody in the hall. It may
be an evil emotion, but I own to a feeling of irrita-
tion when an audience suddenly looks round
with a smirk, then settles importantly in its seat
and nods a vague, eccentric sort of time to show it
knew that piece of music all of yore.
