The Slavey question—how many ill-used mis-
tresses pray their Lares and Penates it could be
written Slave without the question!—is growing
into a prickle of such proportions that women are
woefully declaring all over the city they can’t
endure their lives because of their domestics.
This month and next the prickle always gets its
nastiest point out, as the staidest Janes and
Eml.ilys begin to give themselves airs in anticipa-
tion of Xmas, when the stuffed goose and the
plum - pudding can’t do without them. The
registry-offices are swamped with “ladies,”, while
the servants’ rooms are, for the most part, empty.
A suburban bookseller sends me word that a
“general ” advertised through his shop last week,
and received 07 replies, many of them telegrams,
while a mere man h is been wasting his substance
for weeks in ad vertising for work, without a single
response. Ibis doesn t look so bad for poor,
oppressed woman.
Moreover, Embly Jane has her own decided
system of cataloguing the good and bad things of
life. Approaching her would-be mistress, she
starts off like a game little racer with, “ ’ow many
in faultily ?’’ And if the miscreant missus owns
up-to anything over three, up goes Embly’s nose
ami “ this place won't suit me,” she tells the
registry-keeper scornfully. What pangs of shame
must pierce the mothers of ten and twelve when
brought face to face with these pitiless inquisitors.
Iwo in fambly is Embly’s allowance, anything
over that having to be paid for by the inch. Then,
when that little matter is quite understander!, on
she goes with the slavey catechism. “ What’s the
hootin'? Uo yez keep much company ? Is there
many stairs?” and so on. After all, though,
there’ no ’arm in Embly; she only wants to have
a good time for her money, and, when you come to
think of it, wherdoesir t ?
Just now the servant-girl famine is being
availed over in public and private. Brisbane
missuses have determined to bring out domestics
made in Germany and Scandinavia, as the blue-
eyed Teuton maid holds a record for respectful
manners and honesty. Up North they are used
to all sorts of foreign and alien labor. And on a
certain small station in those low latitudes the
neighbors claim the missus thereof as the cham-
pion mean woman. She counts the oranges grow-
ing on the trees, and can tell to a single fruit how
many are cribbed. One day she interviewed her
Chinese gardener about five: missing oranges—her
piercing eye had noted five short in the daily
count. Of course, John didn’t savee anything,
but, after prying round, she found peelings outside
John’s hut. And, on owning up, the missus had
him booked for the fruit at the rate of a shilling a
dozen. If you think very hard you will find that
-the .above, storyotte—has a bearing on . the great
Servant-girl Trouble.
The barmaid is quite a prominent person to-day.
She is threatened with a sudden dissolution. A
Bill, on her own account only, may set Parliament
scuffling any day, and the blue ribbon of the
female suffragist is already unrolling itself for her
funeral. Any infant can tell you that men know
more about this subject than women. The
shielded feminine woman’s mental paint-brush
always daubs the same picture on her retina at
the mention of this particular human animal.
Wicked gold hair at so much a. bottle, dressed in
the height of a fashion just gone by, a pinched
an 1 steely waist that anyone’s arm could surround,
color heaped upon color in her raiment, and a
tinge that never varies in her cheeks —this is the
barmaid of every housewife’s dreams. This is the
minx with the oily tongue that calls everybody s
husband Charlie and Willie and Reggie. In a
word, this is the Woman’s Barmaid.
The Man’s Barmaid, who is the real thing, is
sometimes like, but a thousand times more often
is as unlike, this picture as the painters of it.
Several barmaids in this city teach Sunday-school
as religiously as if they were milkmaids, and even
if there’s a little diffidence at “Look not on the
wine when it is red,” well —where’s the Sunday-
school teacher who does not shy internally at some
text or other ? And another Australian barmaid
completely floored the editor of the but no
matter. He misquoted poetry, said Campbell
wrote “ The Pleasures of Hope,” and she cor-
rected him and said it was Rogers, and lie bet her
a new hat that she was wrong, and even brought
along a book of quotes to prove bis case. Where-
upon he pointed out to her that the book said
Campbell’s “ Pleasures of Memory,” and on the
next page mentioned the “Pleasures of Hope”
without naming the author, from which he de-
duced that they were both by the same. So she
called upon a veteran journalist to decide, an 1
won her bonnet. Of course no one supposes that
barmaids always win their bonnets that way, but
if mankind and hotels were reformed the objection
to barm aids would soon fizzle oat.
If the time ever comes when no fresh maid shall
be admitted to the bar, a curious vision may be.
realised. All the old bar belles will stick to their
posts till they curl up from sheer age. Then some
day will be seen the spectacle of the one remaining
Flossie serving long beers with a skinny claw,
while two teeth mock each other from opposite
sides of her mouth, and a wig that hasn’t even the
strength to keep straight flops over her withered
oar. And if that awful vision doesn't do some-
thing for the rights of barmaids, nothin ;/ will.

“Besides her grown-up daughter, Janet Lady
Clarke has two younger girls. A dark-haired up-
standing school-miss, who seems to possess some
of her mother’s energy and vim, and a fragile nine-
year-old, the baby of the family. The whole
Cliveden clan start for Cairo almost immediately
to spend the rest of the northern Winter and
Spring there before going on to Europe.

at home on the eventful morning instead of pick-

with chairs iu the baekblocks.

are declaring they never saw such a beautiful
woman as this daughter-of-tho-Cfesars-lookmg
Caraudini scion. Some ascribe her charm to her
mouth, others to her neck. Maybe it’s that
culinly-iiiditfereiit ‘like me or leave me, don’t care
tuppence ’ expression. Up in Brisbane, ’tis said,
the very potentates in the seats of the mighty have
sighed and looked, but sighed in vain. Ages ago,
in her girlish days, the then Miss Palmer sold
Chelsea' buns at a church ‘Okie Englyshe Fayro’
in Melb. Town Hall. Gilded youths besieged the
bun stall, and mince-pies went like wild-fire.

no one but herself has had a hand in the cultiva-
tion of her daughter’s effective voice. Mrs. Wilson,
one hears, regards teachers’ terms and prospects
as far less rosy in Melb. than in the Northern
capital.

meeting on Saturday—such a rainbow squad of
skimmers cleaving the sunshine! The smash tn
r inte, and the two ambulance loads carried off the
field, kept up the excitement, though dressy

“ Appearances are deceptive. The loveliest
pair of legs on the ground pedalled their owner
nowhere near the front.
“There were more Napoleon toques than Tre-
lawneys at the wheel-races. The former are like
the Little Corporal's cocked hat with the up-tilt
worn to the front. It’s hard to find novel devices
for these applique trimmings. Some frocks are
scrolled all over with fantastic versions of the
wearers’ initials in narrow white satin ribbon, &c.
Corn-flower blue is the favorite color for cloth
‘tailor-mades,’ and pockets at present are situated
in the placket-hole, the latter buttoning under-
neath so as to save loss of ’kerchief, &c.

wonder that the cap-and-pinny brigade sneer at
those outside ex-enthusiasts, who, after-all their
collecting and fuss, seem to have completely for-
gotten the projected Infectious-Diseases Hospital
“The Georgia Magnet says she has been at close
quarters with any number of Harem beauties. In
India she did her ‘force-resistance’ deeds in the
private theatres of various Maharajahs, where
bevies of morganatic Flossies viewed the perform-
ance from curtained cages. This didn’t quite
satisfy them, so the Magnet was brought into their
midst (by favor of the dusky rooster) that they
might pinch her arm;, and test her bona-fides to
the best of their limited intelligence. They didn't
try any muscular conclusion with her ‘force,’ for
harem charmers are not built that way. The
Magnet's' most notable opponent in a cue struggle
was the late Czar of Russia, who gave her an
audience at his bit. Petersburg palace and per-
spired freely in many attempts to lift the tricky
little woman from the floor against her will.
Previously she had allowed several picked athletes,
all of them smaller than their Sam soman despot,
to pick up her eight-stone figure with case, but
when the Czar came along she brought the trick
into play and forced his Nibs to relinquish her in
despair. Only a scientific ‘ fake,’apparently. All
the same, it isn't every woman who can retain her
presence of mind whilst an emperor is trying to
hoist her by her elbows.
“ Latter-day acquaintances describe Lady Hope-
ton n as ‘ reserved ’ re her Australian martyrdom—
unlike hubby, who winks the other eye compla-
cently while harping on ‘dear old Victoria.’ One
of his yarns tells of a walking-tour in the Western
district. The then Governor and chums, who had
lured a stray ‘ knock-about ’ on the'road to help
with the ‘ blueys,’ bolted for a day’s shooting at a
small homestead, placed at their disposal by its
absent owner. The rough-hewn factotum in
charge, mistaking the slim, chirpy, carelessly-
clad Ear! for the unwelcome ‘tramp,’ bullied
the surprised aristocrat from the start,
and kow-towed all he could, which wasn’t
much, to the most viceregal-looking of the party.
As soon as Lord H. scented the blunder he de-
cided to keep up the fun, though ‘bounced,’
1 bossed,’ and called to heel with the guns all day.
To flavor the joke, the impostor blundered through-
out like ‘Simple Simon’ or ‘Handy Andy,’and
was ostentatiously snubbed by those in the secret
for his ‘dunderheadedness,’ and brutally slang-
whanged and sworn at by the old ’un for his
‘ blanky cheek.’ At the bachelor homestead that
night, the supposed Governor and his friends
begged their host to make ‘ that fellow ’ useful, so
that his masquerading Ex., skipping around
with plates and things, had a rough time.
In retaliation, lie violently nudged the diners to
ask what they’d take, in defiance of the slave-
driver’s menacing glare, answered back when
reproved, fell down with the fried rabbit and
bacon, and finally was pelted out o£ the room with
potatoes by the infuriated Master of Ceremonies.
Next day, when the visitors had left, their dupe
heard the truth along with a friendly message from
his late victim, congratulating him on the wonders
of his vocabulary.

has given J 6300 for a bull-pup.

Isidor Lissner, of Queensland, is in stature
the smallest man in any Australian Legislature.
His recorded height is sft. lin.
“ G. 8.” : Anybody know what became of
the Baron von S , anointed scoundrel and
Bismarck’s nephew ? Last heard of, he was
in Pentridge, where he had been “sent to
Coventry” by the other prisoners as a spy
and informer. When at liberty, this
nobleman used to live by faking tele-
grams from ’Ome announcing despatch
of colossal remittances. With these he
would work on simple folk who were
glad to accommodate a swell of such
high degree. Had a cabman in this
way for some hundreds of hoarded
earnings. When he was haled be-
fore Justice, some officers of a Ger-
man man-o’-war, then in Hobson’s
Bay, deposed that he really was
made in Germany and related to
the man of blood and iron, but that,
all the same, he was an infernal
rascal. Wonder has he benefited
by the decease of his great kinsman ?
Premier Seddon, of M.L., when
excited, has a difficulty with his “ aitches.”
On one occasion he was making an impas-
sioned appeal in Committee of Supply for more
’igh-schools on the West Coast, when Vincent
Pyke, with an air of the very deepest sympathy,
arose and said: “ I had no idea until the hon.
member spoke that things were so bad ; will the
hon. member kindly tell the committee how many
of these ophthalmic institutions will be required.”
Dr. Broyer, R.C. Bishop of Samoa, now
visiting Sydney, fought on the French side in the
Franco-German war of 30 years ago.

1879.

James Stewart Grant, of Manly (N.S. W.),
dow in his 94th year, took part in a champion
game of draughts one day lately. He played the
“ Herd Laddie,” a champion player, in Belfast,
Ireland, in 1840, and it is claimed that he plays
now as clearly and rapidly as then.
Vic. Legislative Council always opens with
the Lord’s Prayer. President Zeal has been say-
ing this prayer every sitting-day for years past,
but he doesn’t know it yet. Whenever he tries
to say it without the aid of the inorocco-bound
copy in front of him he comes a cropper.
The only time on record when Austra-
lia’s champion swearing skipper was unequal to
the occasion, was once, when, as he was impa-
tiently pacing a Melb. wharf, wishing to get away
with his ship, an ill-favored Chow tapped him
familiarly on the shoulder, and asked, “ What
time you start, old man '! ” The skipper’s silence
was truly eloquent.
Mr. L. J. Harnett, the N.S.W. Sergeant-at-
Arms, is collecting subscriptions for the pur-
pose of placing a marble slab over the remains of
the Maoriland poet, Thomas Bracken, whose
“ Not Understood ’’has justly attained world-wide
fame. Its broad humanity and clear-eyed philo-
sophy, indeed, make it a poem that anyone, any-
where, might well be proud to write over his
name.

There are some queer and more or less
authentic stories (for “the apparatus can’t lie ”)
about “ Dr.” Towle, who died the other day in

One cause of Alfred Deakin’s comparative
reticence in politics is a chronic weakness, which
makes it painful for him to remain on his feet to
deliver a long speech. Ex-Premier Shiels is simi-
larly affected, and so was ex-Gov. Hopetoun.
Dear Bulletin.—Curious, with regard to
the word “ Sufyerd ” —reversed spelling of
Dreyfus—that if you double the F ; make the Y
into I and change the R into L (because he had
such “ an L of a time ”) you.get “ Suffield.”

A Melb. vendor of Malthusian medicines
heads his advt. in one of the local papers with an
alleged portrait of himself. It is in reality a
picture of Lord Byron. This is probably the
worst indignity that has ever been put upon any
poet dead or alive.

One or two of Mortimer Menpes’ first
drawings are still preserved bv a fellow employe
of the painter in his early Adelaide days. Menpes
was office-boy in an Adelaide warehouse, and
finally lost his billet for doing an audacious cari-
cature of the head of the firm.
They were talking about the Draconian
impartiality of a Cabinet Minister, and the en-
thusiast said, “Why, he is so fair and impartial
that I believe, if it came within his duty, he would
hang his brother Jack!” “Humph,” growled
the pessimist, “ so he ought to ! ”
“ Banjo ” Paterson lately returned to
Sydney after a great time in the Northern Terri-
tory, buffalo-shooting. He brought with him
many spoils of the chase. Buffalo-hunting is quite
an industry in the Territory: the steamer by
which Mr. Paterson returned brought down 1000
hides.
Rowland Hassall, of Braidwood, old-time
squatter and still enthusiastic horse-breeder, was
one of the original founders of the Australian
Jockey Club. He is a member of the largely-
diffused Rouse family, but, unlike other members,
has all through his life maintained a profound
contempt for politic*. He is now nearing his 80th
year.
Lord Monkswell (never heard of him
before) is guessed for the N.S.W. Governorship—
apparently on the ground of his having been
nominated for S. Australia and only displaced by
Buxton at the last minute. Monkswell, it
appears, has been a Lord in Waiting to the
Queen and a general hand about the Treasury.
His family name is Collier, and he himself is a
barrister.
Superstitious people will grimly rejoice to
learn that a fortnight before her unexpected and
sudden death Mrs. T. P. Hudson (Miss May
Habgood) deliberately sat last at a table at which
attention had been drawn to the fact of the pre-
sence of 13 persons. By the way, a Bulletin
man made one of a festive dinner-party of 13 fully
three years ago. Scot Inglis was the first of that
party to go under.

Miss Catherine Hay Spence, of Adelaide,
whose name is carefully not alluded to in all Vio.
discussions on proportional representation, is the
daughter of the town clerk of Adelaide, who acted
as returning officer when Rowland Hill’s system
of quota-voting was put in practice in Adelaide, in
1840, in a municipal election. It was the first
official application of the principle, and Miss
Spence dates her interest in it from that.
The oldest identity of the Barwon and
Castlereagh died last week, aged 98. George
Hedger was one of the first whites on the Barwon,
and first settled on the Castlereagh 60 years ago.
He had many encounters with the blacks and was
speared several times. He was one of the first to
take advantage of the free-selection clauses of the
Robertson Land Act on the Castlereagh, and, un-
like most selectors, retained his holding till his
death.
At Watson’s Bay (N.S.W.), last Saturday,
as the steamer containing the military-Parlia-
ment party neared the Govt, wharf the launch
Premier was passed, with Premier Reid aboard
assiduously angling. At once there was an out-
burst of yells: “ Angling for a fresh policy,
hey ? ” “ (Daught a new tax yet ? ” “ What are
you fishing for, George ? ” To which taunts
George curtly and not inaptly replied, “ Flat-
head ! ”
The salary of the manager of a leading
Sydney hotel has been for years past £lO4l 13s.
4d. per annum, while his commission amounted on
an average to £350 per annum. Why, Dickson,
Kingston, Forrest, Braddon and Reid manage
each a whole province for less than the
total of the sums mentioned. A hotel-manager
enjoys another inestimable privilege. He can
always get from himself all the drinks he wants
on credit.
Old Jack Robertson and Parkes were
K.C.M.G.’d simultaneously. At the time they
were not on good terms—in fact, for long they had
not spoken —but Jack wanted to make friends
again. So, seeing the rival leader leaving the
House, he contrived to meet him behind the
Speaker’s chair. “ Ah, Parkes,” snuffled old Jack,
pulling out of his breast pocket the Imperial noti-
fication of bebaublement, “ I s’pose you’ve got one
of these blanky things too ? ’’

Baron Yon Mueller’s share in the dis-
covery of the virtues of eucalyptus oil has been
mostly overlooked in the obituary notices of Jos.
Bosisto. Mueller it was who persuaded the other
to take up the extraction as a commercial matter ;
his own business, he said, ended when he found
out that the oil was there and was worth extract-
ing. Bosisto had been imported for an Adelaide
chemist’s business.
The late Joe Bosisto, the eucalyptus-oil
man, made one memorable speech in Vic. As-
sembly. He had just come back from the Colonial
and Indian Exhibition with a C.M.G. label on
him, and he told the House of his great joy when
he saw with his own eyes Our Gracious Queen,
and heard with his own ears “her silvery voice.”
At that time Joe was the most profoundly loyal
chemist under the sun.
Hermes : Newspaper pictures of murderer
Archer represent him as a rather good-looking
young man with a luxuriant moustache. Apropos,
“ Owen Blayney,” in the Humanitarian, de-
clares that though he has often read of handsome
criminals, in the course of 12 years’ intimate
experience of Courts of Justice, he has never seen
one. Of course, it all depends on what “O.B.”
would consider “good-looking.”
English Socialist Hyndman visited Aus-
tralia in the late sixties, and wrote leaders for the
Argus on free education, when the battle for the
Act was being fought. He was formerly a good
cricketer, and played with Sussex against Aus-
tralia. His father spent ,£150,000 on the erection
of churches in the East End of London. Hynd-
man himself is 56, and at latest was very dis-
satisfied at the slow progress of Socialism.
The Outlook on poor-devil Grien, Tich-
borne the Second :
St. James’s Hall was crowded on Monday night. The
much-advertised Re Rougeniont’s lecture had filled the
place with a curious assembly. The body of the hall was
crammed, and on the raised platform, before a skeleton
map of the Australian continent, were set out Mr. De
Rougemont and his backers. These last had evidently
spent the days preceding the lecture in taming their wild
man. For no more broken-spirited savage was ever
butchered to make a Heaven-knows-what than the op-
pressed and automatic individual who struggled through
his task under the eyes of his keepers. Mr. De
Rougemont’s lecture was painfully like the performance
of a carefully-trained animal. We mean no disrespect to
Mr. De Rougemont. Such greatness as was his on Mon-
day night was pa'pahly thrust upon him. It was evidently
so set forth in. the bond, .and the bondsmen were there
to claim their pound of flesh. We felt sorry for Mr.
De Rougemont. He floundered along ; his heart was
not in the task. But he had signed the bond.
The word “civilisation” was much in the air on Mon-
day night. Nor did the irony of its usage under such
circumstances seem to strike the audience. The meeting
was of course clol lied in a thin veneer of decency. There
was a chairman and a table with a decanter of water and
a glass. But beneath this veneer luxuriated a positive
carnival of savagery. We need not dwell on Mr. De
Rougemont’s performance. It was paid for, and there’s
an end to it. He mentioned the Wide World Magazine
as often as was required. He was rude to his critics, as
per instructions. He retold the adventures of M. Louis
De Rougemont as far as they have appeared, and hinted
at the concluding chapters. These were to be found in
future issues of the Wide World Magazine. He men-
tioned that he was to be paid by results, and that
therefore his policy was such and such. He alluded to
a New Testament which had been the prop of his exiled
years and to various shadowy wives and families who
had served a like purpose. It was all very depressing.
When the end was reached a person in the audience
evinced a desire to inspect Mr. De Rougemont’s arm.
The request was granted. The “lecturer” had so far
gone through his performance without remonstrance. He
had signed the bond. This crowning indignity made him
tremble from head to foot, but he came to heel and
obeyed his proprietors. A more degrading spectacle than
the one that ensued we have rarely witnessed. De
Rougemont was helped out of his coat and waistcoat.
The chairman seized one arm and rolled up the shirt
sleeve to the shoulder. “It’s just above the
shoulder,” he said, in allusion to a convict brand which
he seemed to entertain some hopes of finding. The mark
was absent. The crowd round De Rougemont at this
period resembled nothing so much as a company of evil
vermin. They pounced on the poor wretch with a cal-
lous indecency that would have shamed a costermonger.
They bared his arm, they would have stripped him to the
bone without overmuch hesitation. Modern journalism
has reached some strange depths, but nothing more en-
tirely sordid and offensive than the conduct of last
Monday’s “ lecture ” has been swallowed by the British
public. The Syndicate have had their pound of flesh,
De Rougemont has looked to his bond. Let. this be the
end of it.

“ 0.K.” : Bourke district beats “ P.G.’s ”
W. A. heat-record. Two travellers towards Bourke,
one very hot day during ’BB drought, drove up to
a station. No sign of life ; no answer to repeated
oooees. At last one of them spotted man camped
under tree, so walked over and asked if the boss
was at home. No answer. Next enquired where
drinking-water was to be found. Man simply
pointed towards tank with toe. Mao (loq.) : “By
, do that again and I’ll give you five bob.”
Man took no notice—too hot !

“Yarrum”: Last night found an old
fellow eel-fishing in the Minamurra. Sat down to
discuss Federation, and noticed that the “lone
fisherman ” didn’t articulate as distinctly as he
mostly did, but chopped his words considerably.
THE GRAFTER'S HEAD.—A sketch from. life.
Asked the old man what he had in his mouth, and
he. mumbled—“Nothin’, only a few worms for
bait. Yer see,” he explained, “if yer puts ’em in
a tin they dries up ter nothin’, and if yer puts ’em
on the ground they makes tracks.”
Also, t’other night dropped across another
“lone fisherman” on the Minamurra. He also
was after eels, and was using for bait live frog,
than which, he said, there was “nothing better.”
After yarning with him for almost an hour, he
complained about getting no bites couldn’t
understand it. Then he struck a match to light
his pipe, and discovered froggy sittinq on his knee.
Slung him out again and waited half-an-hour
without a bite. Got another light and found the
bait back on its old perch. Then he wound up,
cursed much, and made a bee-line for his humpy.
Eel-fishing, in the dark, is an interesting pastime.

The Bulletin haa nad this argument
before, but a good sermon can hardly be preached
too often :
Dear Bulletin. — These prayer-for-rain people are posi-
tively sickening to a man who, like me, has passed the
last 4 >4 years in a district wherein stock have been dying-
in thousands from starvation. Their sufferings extend
over months, and their last moments are indescribably
horrible. Think : lambs, with entrails plucked from their
yet-breathing bodies, and their moaning mothers, with
eyes torn from their yet-blinking sockets ! Our old pet
milkers, too, and favorite hacks all alike smitten, and
their last throes intensified by tortures inflicted by those
other of “ God’s creatures,” the accursed crows. And if
all this, beneath His all-seeing eye, fails to awaken His
compassion, how shall the prayers of humans who are
only troubled about the money' ioss avail ?—L.M.
• * * *
White grubs as man’s food ? Rather !
Nolan, one of the brothers who found Mt.
Browne (may Heaven forgive them !), told the
writer that, during the worst of their awful time
—they struck that droughty part at its droughtiest
—their best tucker was running hog-weed and
white grubs. Both can be got on those sand-
patches where in season melons grow so luxuri-
antly. They stewed the hog-weed ; writer forgets
how the grubs were served. But, anyhow, Nolan
said they always, when eaten together, reminded
him of rhubarb and cream.
* * ♦ •
Dear Bulletin. — Be tree-grub eating.
When I was at school at Ross (Tas.) nearly every
boy had his tomahawk and grub-tin, and, on holi-
days, used to go out after wattle-grubs. Those
that were cut while being chopped out of the true
were eaten on the spot. The others were taken
home, cooked in hot ashes or on a piece of tin,
and eaten with much gusto. Some preferred them
raw though, and would always take care to bite
the head first, as, otherwise, the grub would nip
the tongue. These grubs are pink when young,
and ivory-white when old, but there is also a
blackish kind, which we wouldn’t eat.—G.S.
* * * *
“C.F.”: Be grub-eating by white men.
Have myself eaten the sago-palm grub, which is
considered such a delicacy by the Muruts of west
Borneo. The crawlers, about ljin. long and thick
as a man’s thumb, are spitted on a skewer and
toasted. Taste like high-quality sausages. Have
never seen even natives eat ’em raw, and seldom
heard of a white man tackling them at all. Only
did so myself first time when pushed for food, bub
afterwards ate them from choice—and enjoyed
them.
• • • •
“ Bargo ” : Re tree-grubs. Went to school
with orphan boy who was despatched daily with-
out any dinner—always dined on large, white
grubs. Preferred them even when bread-and-
treacle was offered by his mates. May have been
pride, though.

“A.E.G.” : No man could make me be-
lieve he ever saw one jackass kill a snake bigger
than a blind-worm. I have seen half-a-dozen
“johnnies” worry to death a sft. black snake.
They tossed him about in mid-air from one to the
other; and, when he reached ground, he was as
dead as Csesar. Was talking, the other day, to
one of the oldest bushmen in Australia. He, too,
has seen many jackasses kill a snake, but never
one. Kept a tame goburra for three years. One
day a tiger snake came along, and I put Jack
down to “knock him out,” which he didn’t.
There was no fight, although the snake was
willing.
• * * •
“T.H.T.” : Re what cows will eat. Once
saw one munching a shin of beef that she had
nosed out of the open window of a butcher’s shop.
Struck me as very extraordinary, but I have since
learnt that the Faroe Islanders (midway ’tween
Scotland and Iceland) feed their cattle on boiled
whale-blubber. Have known an old cart-horse
that could chew tobacco like a shellback. Gave
him a fig one day for experiment and he scoffed it
in a jiffy. Then offered him a newspaper for
dessert, and he swallowed that.
* * * *
“Jinda”: Assertion (B. 12/11/’9B) that
cows won’t eat each other’s feed : have often seen
one moo turn from her own feed to tackle another’s,
and have had to separate two milkers because one,
being a quick feeder, would, after finishing her
own lot, drive other away and take remainder.
On other hand, have noticed that a horse is very
chary of touching another’s feed, especially if it
has been at all nosed about. Of course, am re-
ferring to stall-fed, city animals. Out back, where
they have to hustle for their tucker, they’ll eat
pretty well anything. That cattle will chew bones
of defunct relatives is true. Due to an abnormal
appetite, caused by the absence of natural salts in
the pasture. Rock-salt is the remedy. Have lost
more than one cow through choking with a bone.

load of wool from Q. station, 80 miles away.

Dear B, —At Warranga station, on the
Moonie, Q., in ’5O, then owned by the Crowders, the
term “brumby ” was applied to wild horses
by the blacks, denoting astonishment at their
wild starts. Also, the Condamine and
Balonne country was then spoken of as
the “Never Never.” Live authority
for this.—Steele Rudd.

“C.H.S.” : Be rat exodus (8., 5/11/’9B).
Similar hegiras are known in Sweden and Norway.
Armies of bush rodents, mostly bucks, start on the
wallaby at irregular intervals, a couple of years
sometimes intervening between the rat-march.
No explanation has been discovered of the where-
fore of this journey, though the suggestion has
been ventured that —as the birth-rate of bucks
exceeds that of does, and as the “ hes ” in early
life absorb the “ shes ” by the simple process of
“ scoffing ” them—the consequent paucity of wives
drives the bachelors out in mobs to seek for matri-
mony elsewhere.
“E.S.S. : The common bush phea-
sant is the best imitator of the “swish-bump ” of
the pit-saw. Its cry is a slow “she-e-yew.”
Sometimes it’s plain “swish,” but you get the
bump all right after if you happen to be walking
along looking at it and don’t see the intervening
log till you’re spread-eagled (a common experience
with bush naturalists). Then there’s some
blanky after the bump, which doesn’t sound
like a pit-saw at all.
The other day, in Budda Lake
(N.S. W.), a monster cod (1451 b.),
about as ancient as the very lake
itself, was shot with a rifle-bullet.
This Noah’s-Ark fish was seen from
time to time, years ago, by the local
blacks, who were more inclined to
believe it a bunyip than a cod.
About the same time as this monster
was shot, another bunyip-cod weigh-
ing over 1001 b. was caught in Budda
with hook and line.

A Riverine man trucked a lot
of sheep to Melbourne, and when they
arrived in the early morning several
were, as usual, down, and smoth-
ered. For these a local butcher
offered a price, and when asked what
he wanted them for said, “Oh, they
make great sausages.” The sausage-
eater should be able to move moun-
tains, his faith is so abounding !
“ Tassy ’’ : Be pelicans’ nests. Have seen
plenty both in Westralia and on the B. coast of
Tasmania. They were built of reeds in every
case. Saw on Tas. E. coast a rug entirely of
felicans’ breast-skins (of course, with the feathers).
t was made by a fisherman, and sold by him to_a
Vic. tourist for .£20.

“R.H.D.”: When “ J. Jingle” states
that Henry Lawson means “dust” when he
speaks of “ svvaggie’s ” bed of ashes, he only
shows, the limit of his experience. “ The ash-
heap ” in these parts (Q.) is as much an institu-
tion as the bush-shanty, and equally well patron-
ised. Every pub. hereabouts has its ash heap,
and when the “ topers ” are “ doing in their bit ”
at the bar they take their rest in ash-bed. Have
often seen 10 or 12 drunks more or less covered by
burnt “ gidgee,” and apparently quite com-
fortable.

Coolabah to Powell’s, on the last day we missed
four, and sent Busy back for them. Next morn-
ing she brought along two of the missing beasts,
and when the boss and myself went back for the
others, we found that Busy had had two
pups, and that they were
bringing the rest of
the bullocks along.

“Good morning, Mr. Jones.”
FARMER JONRS: “Good morning, is it? If you
had a kid with the hellers all night, and got
up and found three cows gone, you wouldn’t

Ed. B.—ln answer to “H.S.R.” Kanga-
roos, at all events, don’t breed three times a year;
rarely indeed more than once. The young, though
carried only five or six weeks “in womb,’’are
very small and undeveloped when born, and grow
very slowly. They are suckled for a considerable
time after leaving the pouch, and always use the
teat they were reared on ; the new joey being
attached to another. Have often taken twins
from pouch, and on two occasions two joeys of
different ages.

* ‘ Trebless ” : Re fires started by broken
bottles. During the “big N.S.W. strike’’ out
West, many devastating fires on big runs were
deliberately so caused. At sundown, a bit of
clear glass would be fixed on top of a small heap
of dry grass ; then the incendiary would travel all
night, and be 20 or 30 miles away by noon of next
day, when fire w’as pretty certain to start.

Re swaggie and dead dog (12/11/98). Writer
met a shepherd in Carcoar once, with his dog
across saddle, dead. The animal had picked up a
poisoned bait in the street, “ and,” said its master,
almost in tears, “ I wouldn’t bury a dog in such a
—— hole. I’ll take him into the bush and bury
him.” And he did.

“Raspberry”: A recent par. recalls a
Launceston (Tas.) incident. A friend of mine,
while working a moulding-machine in a timber-
mill, got his hand caught and chopped off at the
wrist. He came walking down street, and I, see-
ing him with his apron wrapped round his hand,
asked him what was up. “ Just cut off my hand.”
After having a brandy with me, he walked to the
hospital, two miles away.
» * * •
The Lutheran missioners to the Centralian
blacks have a hard row to hoe. One brother,
down south now from Kopperamanna, says they
have 1000 square miles of country on the Cooper
and 100 blacks to look after. The stock, apart
from the natives, consists of 10,000 sheep, 1000
cattle and 500 horses. Year before last they lost
20,000 sheep through drought, and this year three-
fourths of the lambs died. The blacks are dying,
too—some of consumption, but more of another
wasting disease.

Editor Bulletin. “ Maroogaline ” (8.,
5/11/’9B) makes “ braxie ” mean the mutton of
sheep “dead from diseased liver,” but the term
really applies to flesh of sheep smothered in the
snow on the Scotch hills in winter and recovered
during spring thaws. Have often as a boy eaten
braxie, from which fact I presume that it was not
regarded as “ diseased.” —W.H.
* ♦ * *
Into old Gills’s pub. at Croajingalong
came recently three strangers and ordered three
long beers ; but, when the liquor was planked, a
friendly argument ensued as to who was to pay.
No. 1 wanted to, because he had asked the others
in ; No. 2 because No. 1 had shouted last; and
No. 3 just to settle the matter. Decided at last
that old Gills be blindfolded, and the first man he
touched was to pay. G. agreed ; and while one
man blindfolded him, the other two silently
mopped up their swankey, and then, remarking
that the bandage was not tied pro erly, went
over and fumbled with it till their pal had also
sunk his beer, whereupon the trio quietly
“ cleared.” The old man meandered all over the
place, bumping himself grievously in his efforts to
clutch some of them, till at last he collided with
the door-screen and nearly stove in his jaw.
When he pulled off the handkerchief and found
the strangers gone and the glasses empty, his
blasphemy could be heard for chains around. A
week afterwards, however. Gills sighted one of
the strangers and bailed him up, when the man
explained that the whole thing was a joke ; but, as
he hadn’t the 18d. on him just then, would Gills
come with him and get it fixed up ? Took Gills
accordingly to the local news-agent (a great tem-
perance advocate), left G. outside, went in and
spun yarn about having “man waiting to sign the
pledge.” The cold-tea man brought Gills in,
stranger saying he would wait outside ; and then
the temperance-apostle got out his pledge-book
“ Surely,” said Gills, “ you are not going to write
a blanky cheque for it?” “Why, your friend
told me you wished to sign the pledge.” Of course
when Gills rushed out his friend was gone. And
now the old man keeps a loaded gun in the bar in
case anyone should propose another game of blind-
man’s buff.

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*

Related that a flock of emus——apparently
dying of thirst —recently came right into a N.S. W.
country town. Interesting to know how the un-
lucky birds, were served. Were they hospitably
entertained, or were they knocked on the head as
a reward for their pathetic trustfulness?
A Melb. paper has a story of a local artist
who painted a landscape with a calf and an adult
bovine in the foreground, and called the picture
“ The Mother.” An expert rural critic dis-
covered that the adult bovine was a bullock, and
the artist was overwhelmed. But when the pic-
ture was once more made visible, Dauber was
happy again. He had re-christened it “The
Father!”
• • • *
At Shepparton (Vic.), in the early fifties,
the then few inhabitants (amongst them present
Melb. P.M. Panton) were aroused one night by a
loud bellowing, and next morning saw a round,
black head bobbing up in the river at intervals.
Presently, in getting over the punt-rope, the crea-
ture showed itself entirely, and was pronounced
by an old blackfellow to be the veritable bunyip.
As sketched by a young artist present, however,
it so strongly resembled a seal that there are
grounds for supposing that it really was a phoca,
which had mistaken its way at Adelaide, got into
the Murray, and become completely bushed. [Here
endeth the bunyip-question.j

“ Alcheringa ” : Some Vic. western dis-
trict ancients aver that when the summer-fly
perches in myriads on the sides of the grass spears
rain is only a few hours off and will come from the
direction opposite to that on which the flies are
clinging. Have we really “ barometer flies,” and
who told them all about it ?

“Tin-Lip”: More scrub-furkey. After
first part of nidification is complete, the mother
watches nest, containing the eggs, continually
scratching further leaves and brush and earth
over it till the time of hatching arrives. Then she
hovers around till she hears sounds indicating that
the young have broken through the shell, when
she scratch 1 s the way clear for them. Iguanas
being on the increase through the present-day
blackfellow not being so much dependent on hunt-
ing for food, the turkeys are on the decrease. The
iguana burrows into the nest for the eggs.

The other day 862 store bullocks were
trucked from Dubbo to Wodonga. That was all
that was left of 2600 head which left Morney
Plains, in the Gulf country, over two years ago.
The rest had dropped along the devious ways
which were taken in the perplexing quest for feed
and water. The cost of the trip is put at <£2ooo.
* * • •
This season’s dust-storms out in the Never-
Never, like every other season’s, about put up a
record. Latest stories, certified by the backbloek
journalist, who at this time of year is too tired
even to lie, are that during the fury of one gale at
midday all was so dark that the time could not be
told by a watch held in the hand of a person in a
house whose builder had guaranteed it dust-proof,
and that this condition lasted for five solid hours
with breaks of only a few minutes ; that at Mt.
Sturt, where the homestead was partly wrecked, a
lady could not see her terrified children, though
she held them about her knees ; and that near
Cobham the horses had to be taken out of the
coach, not only because the track could not be
seen, but because the coach was in imminent
danger of being overturned—the wheels actually
left the ground two or three times. At the
Broken Hill mines most work at the surface has
been suspended several shifts ; and for the greater
part of many days every telegraph line to the
town has been broken ; while special gangs have
been kept on the railway lines shovelling off sand
drifts in order to allow trains to get through.

It is well known to every intelligent visitor to
India (comments London Sketch), and to every-
one here outside of the “ flocks ’’ who subscribe to
missionary societies, that no one would care to
employ a converted native, his conversion being
generally only a subterfuge for cheating his master.
The “ heathen in his blindness ” is indifferently
honest, but the “ lamp of life ” only serves to light
him to theretofore undreamt-of rascalities.

“N. 8.”: Out west, 25 years ago, when
there was little money circulating, a kangaroo
scalp was worth Is., and a wallaby’s 6d. Have
often seen a scalp-hunter paying for liquor with
scalps ; would call for a beer, plank a kangaroo
scalp, and the publican, out of a box, would give
him a wallaby’s ditto in change.
* * * *
“ A.A.G.” : A unique fish haunts that
part of Admiralty Bay, M.L., where steamers
from Picton slack down before going through the
French Pass, a narrow channel across which you
can throw the proverbial biscuit. This fish is
about lift, long and of a greyish color. People
call it the Wainui fish, a white porpoise, an
albino. Naturalist Hector has given it a deca-
syllabic Latin name. Its most striking peculiarity
is that it is “soft” on steamers. When it sees
one coming it “goes” for it, gambols round it,
caresses its bow, rubs itself against it from stem
to ’midships, first on the port side and then on the
starboard. Maoris call it a white taniwha,
straight. But if a pakeha, who is sane and has
seen it, may make a suggestion, I would throw
out the belief that it is a “killer” that suffers
from a parasitic disease. Old Bay of Islands
whalers tell of a narrow rocky gut where whales
used to come for the purpose of rubbing the bar-
nacles off their sides, and be killed in the opera-
tion by said ancient mariners.
Mrs. MAESMORE MORRIS.

Fruit in season. In summer she provides
her meat; in winter, finds her store complete.
This is the position the thrifty housewife finds
herself when she takes advantage of the bountiful
supply of fruit we are going to have this season.
M‘Lean Bros, and Rigg, of Sydney and Mel-
bourne, have anticipated the season and are now
landing their stock of specially-manufactured
fruit-jars. Lightning, Mason, Chicago, Perfec-
tion, &c., &c., all guaranteed and air-tight. Jam-
jars all sizes and kinds.*

THOS. SUFFIELD.
Photo., Kerry & Co.
The Bulletin had never seen Thomas
Suffield, or even his photograph, until he called at
its office the other day after his release. He
proves to be a rather good-looking, thickset fellow
of very Saxon appearance and of the naval-officer
type—you couldn’t pick him as a civilian out of
any group of navy-men with clipped beards. His
manner and appearance are certainly not those
usually ascribed to a brutal assailant of women.
And in case there is anybody still unacquained
with Suffield’s story, The Bulletin repeats that
he was convicted at Maitland, N.S.W., in 1891,
of an attempt to assault a woman named Ann
Chambers. The notorious Judge Windeyer
conducted the trial, and sentenced Suffield
to ten years’ penal servitude. His sen-
tence was shortened by good-conduct remis-
sions to about seven and a-half years, and he
was released last October, having several times
refused offers of earlier release made by N.S. VV.
Justice Dept, if he would sue for mercy. The
woman on whose sole and uncorroborated and self-
contradicted evidence Suffield was convicted swore
to one story at the police-court enquiry and to
another story at the trial. She at first gave her
employer and his family and the doctor to under-
stand that she had been attacked by a bull, and
only next day declared she had been assailed
by a man, and later still alleged that the
man was Suffield. There was no conclusive medi-
cal testimony that a man had ever been near her,
and in Suffield’s favor were his best of characters,
all the circumstantial evidence (including the fact
that he was absolutely unscratched, after having,
as the woman alleged, gone through a long and
desperate struggle with her), and an alibi sworn
by two persons. Yet Judge Windeyer acted as
prosecutor instead of Judge, frowned down the
defence, and secured a verdict in the teeth of the
evidence and the probabilities. Enquiry into the
case has hitherto been persistently refused. The
Bulletin’s pamphlet on this subject may be had
by post to any address for seven stamps.
Zola at latest advices was in London, pre-
paring an even bigger bombshell than ‘‘ J’Accuse! ”
(the “open letter ” which raised the whole storm).
Sirdar Kitchener is alleged to be aeon-
firmed woman-hater, and this without any known
“ history ”of “ blighted hopes.” Possibly a mere
case of professional absorption. Kitchener’s pro-
motion, very slow at first, has of late years
galloped in a way that could only mean “ one
man, one idea.”
“Black Maria ” broke down in Melb., the
other morning, and 21 prisoner passengers were
violently upset and shaken. “The exceptionally
large load evidently was responsible for the mis-
hap,” said the Argus, but it didn’t go on to re-
mark that the 21 victims of departmental reck-
lessness are entitled to sue for damages. Prisoners
charged with theft or “drunk and dis.” deserve
quite as careful treatment as a criminal under sen-
tence of death.
A few years ago the Aucklanders built
themselves a huge dock (the Calliope), and when
it was completed scarcely knew what to do with
it. Mr. J. H. Witheford, of the Harbor Board,
saw a future for the dock if the Admiralty could
be persuaded to use it as an auxiliary repairing-
station to Garden Island. He proceeded to
London and did persuade the Admiralty. The
scheme is of much value to Australia. That
eminent military expert, Gov. Jervois, regarded
Maoriland as an advance shield over Australia—
it would probably bear the brunt of a descent on
these regions, and with its coal and other re-
sources in the hands of an enemy it would become
a formidable menace to us. Up to now this out-
post has been without means to repair any of the
local squadron damaged in repelling an invader.
If such a contingency arose, the injured vessel, if
she were able, would have to make for Sydney to
repair. Mr. Witheford has been instrumental in
remedying this, and the Admiralty has de-
cided to suitably equip the dock with repairing
machinery.

The Solicitor-General: ‘-The Council, it neemetC was in favor of the Bill,
Trat desired to impose restrictions. House had abandoned two proposals brought
forward to amend the Bfll. 'and now the matter of proportional representation was
sought to be imposed Even if the system of proportional representation were per-
fect, was it opportune to-iuehide it in this Bill and to dictate to the Assembly the
terms under which the Bill would be passed 1 His colleagues in another place were
against this amendment, and would advise their supporters to vote against it."
Sir Henry Wrixon replied to some of the objections raised by the Solicitor-
General, and pointed out that all through he had been actuated by a desire to see the
One-man-one-vote made an accomplished principle of legislation ; but, at the same
time, he reminded ht>n. members that, without some such scheme as that put forward
in his amendments, the One-man-one- vote would mean the permanent disfranchise-
ment of a large number of voters. All that he desired was the adoption of some
method that would give to each vote its effective value. They did not wish to dictate
to the Assembly. The other House could alter the proposal in any way ; it was only
the principle they desired to see affirmed not with the view of repealing the Bill,
but of accepting it. it .

The N.S.W. Lager Bier Brewing Co., Ltd.,
of Moore Park, Sydney, has placed upon the mar-
ket a splendid Lager Bier called Sydney Lager
Bier. This bier is the choicest thing ever offered
to the people of N.S.W. Analysts praise it most
highly ; doctors recommend it to convalescents,
and connoisseurs at the last Sydney Agricultural
Show gave it first prize against all competitors.
\Vhat has long troubled the rational people of
N.S.W. is “what to drink.” There is no fun
in summer weather in drinking fiery alcoholic
liquors that break up the nerves, affect the diges-
tion, heart, liver and kidneys; and on the other
hand there is only an intense vagueness and total
dissatisfaction in drinking wish-wash. Sydney
Lager Bier is the happy mean between these ex-
tremes, and people in the country who desire to
have the newest and best drink going should com-
municate with the N.S.W. Lager Bier Brewing
Co., Moore Park, Sydney, for particulars. Al-
though the new lager is so much superior to all
others, it is the most reasonable in cost upon this
market. *

“Blue” Law.
The “blue law ” question will apparently be dis-
cussed in the near future as vigorously in Aus-
tralia as it has been in the States. There, a
“blue law” is one which, while in full vigor in
the statute-book, receives the cut direct from all
administrators of the law in actual life. The
principal samples there are laws connected with
licensing and prohibition in States, or parts of
States, where public opinion, though weak enough
to allow such laws to be passed, is strong enough
to successfully defy them. The coal-weighing
law has been up to date a “ blue ” law in N.S. W.,
and, if all the rumors one hears are true, intends
to continue wearing its azure robes.
» * • •
There are, of course, “blue laws” and “blue
laws.” Some can’t help themselves, their blue-
ness being the inevitable senile dementia that is
the fate of all things mundane. In this class may
be included the vast host of laws transported with
the early inhabitants to N.S. W., and confirmed by
an Act of George IV. Very many of them have
become, in the course of time, utterly unsuited to
their new surroundings, and are, as a rule, ignored
altogether, except when, as in the case of the Sun-
day Act, or the sorcery law, someone powerful
enough has an ecclesiastical axe to grind. The
bulk of them are marked “obsolete” in the
dictionary of law, and their bluemouldiness does
little harm. They only serve the useful pur-
pose of being a perpetual and powerful argument
for a regular codification of at least the statute
law of the country. With a code they and their
power for evil would disappear. But the other
class of “ blue law ” is a very different kind of cen-
tipede. No self-respecting Government can toler-
ate for long the existence of laws which are
not obeyed. This kind of “ blue law ”is a defiance
of Government, an insult to Democracy. It indi-
cates the existence of a class in the community
more powerful than the Government, and is thus
the symptom of a dry-rot which, in time, will in-
volve both Government and community. It is
somewhat singular that an English law directed
against “time-bargains” on the Stock Exchange
has been successfully defied for a number of years
by, perhaps, the most powerful class of men in
England, the stock-brokers. And it can hardly be
surprising that the coal-barons of Australia should
make similar attempts to prove their superiority to
an Australian Government. Whether they should
be allowed to succeed is, of course, another ques-
tion. Whatever may be said of obsolete laws,
there can be no question that, in a community
ruled under the principles contained in the Bill of
Rights, there should be no “ blue laws ” of the
coal-weighing type. A live Act should either be
enforced or repealed.
• » • •
It may not be amiss to remind those concerned
that there is an unrepealed principle of the British
Constitution in full force in this country, namely,
that no one, not even the Sovereign, has the right
or the power to suspend the operation of an Act of
Parliament; and, further, that the remedy of im-
peachment is open against Ministers guilty of
winking at the suspension of laws which they have
sworn to enforce.
Two Shots for the Prosecution.
“ Lex,” to The Bulletin
The point which has arisen in Victoria over the con-
viction of an accused person for perjured evidence
given at a previous trial for another offence on
which he was acquitted is most important. In the
old state of the law, when accused could not give evi-
dence on oath, such a state of things was impossible.
That, under the new law, such prosecutions for perjury
are possible after acquittal is, to say the least of it,
dangerous. In the present case, the accused was
charged with an offence at Carrurn. Evidence was
given by some witnesses tending to prove an alibi, and
accused on oath said that be was not at Carrurn at the
tune the offence was committed. The jury disagreed. A
second trial look place, exactly the same evidence was
given, and he was acquitted. Then the Crown proceeded
against him for perjury for saying he was not at Carrurn,
and he was convicted. In the first place, the possibility
of this sort of thing must tend to make the exercise of
the right to give evidence on his own behalf either a
dangerous one or a useless one to an accused. If he
asserts the right, then whether pronounced innocent or
guilty he may find himself up for perjury ; if he doesn’t
assert the right, most jurymen will assume that he is
afraid on account of his knowledge of his guilt. In
the second place, such a prosecution is really, though
perhaps not technically, a breach of the admirable
and ancient British legal rule that a man shall not be
prosecuted twice for the same offence or on the same
evidence when he has once been found innocent. This
rule serves the double purpose of preventing harassing
persecution, and of providing for finality in criminal pro-
ceedings ; and the introduction of accused’s right to give
evidence was certainly never intended to either directly
or indirectly abrogate this rule. And, finally, such a
prosecution really puts in the hands of the Crown in
every case in which accused gives evidence, and in which
there is an acquittal, a method of appeal from the decision
of the jury, which is denied the accused if he should be
convicted. It is monstrous that one may appeal to the
highest courts in trivial questions of property, and that
appeal should be denied, except on law points, in cases
involving liberty and even life. But it is still more mon-
strous that not only shall a prisoner be denied right of
appeal, but that the Crown should be given the right of
appeal if prisoner should be found innocent. A prosecu-
tion for perjury after an acquittal really amounts to an
appeal from one jury to another jury. Mr. Justice Wills,
m a recent address to an English grand-jury, said plainly
that such a prosecution was a breach of the doctrine of
autrefois acquit. I can only hope that Vic. Full' Court
will, when the point conies before it, take the same view
and preserve acquitted prisoners from the dangers which
any other decision will ensure for them at the hands of
perhaps chagrined policemen and detectives, the instiga-
tion of private enemies, or the relentlessness, or worse,
of Crown Law authorities.
More About the Betting Evil.
The Vic. Totalisator Bill being practically lost
for the present, Murray, M.L. A., has invited
the Assembly to appoint a Royal Commission
to take evidence concerning the “ social, moral,
and economic effect of horse-racing on the com-
munity, and to recommend what, in its opinion,
are the best legislative steps to be taken,” if
.State interference seems advisable. Sancti-
monious opponents of a legalised gambling
machine cannot raise objections to this proposal,
seeing that the term “ betting evil ” is con-
stantly on their lips. A Royal Commission
with the avowed object of diminishing the evil
must command the Church Party’s earnest
support, unless the Church Party has pinned
its faith to Ikey Mo, and is prepared to resist
any attempt to injure his betting business.
The question, then, for consideration is—what
witnesses should be called by this Board of
Moral Health ? Horse-owners, bookmakers, or
any other habitual “patrons of the turf” are
not the right people to consult in this matter.
They find no fault with the effect of
horse-racing on the community in general.
They are the sharps at the game, and the wel-
fare of the flats is not their concern. They
don’t ask for a measure of protection against
their own natural gambling tendencies, which is
exactly what the general community wishes to
obtain, somehow. Horse-racingseemsinevitable,
betting seems inevitable ; and any arrangement
whereby the craving for betting can be
satisfied with the least possible harm to the
young and foolish is a consummation devoutly
to be wished. We may safely say that every
man at 35 is a doctor or a hopeless fool as re-
gards the betting ailment. He cures himself if
he be not incurable. But for the perennial
army of boys and young men a partial delivery
from temptation is needed. An honestly-con-
ducted commission of enquiry into the betting
evil is bound to find it rooted in the book-
maker, therefore, unless the pietist who prays
to be led not into temptation on his own account
is utterly unconcerned about the temptations
of his “ deah young friends ” he will hence-
forth and for ever cease to work in the interests
of Ikey Mo against the totalisator. The tote
is the only present substitute for the human
bookmaker. It doesn’t wipe him out alto-
gether, but it abolishes him for general public
purposes. He ceases to exist in tobacco shops,
and at street corners. He is the temptation
which melts into air under the comparatively
benignant rays of the tote. It is argued that
the machine creates a taste for betting in
women and boys who didn’t deal with book-
makers. The absurdity of this parsouic fiction
is obvious. Boys cannot bet directly with the
machine, whereas the smallest urchin can
hand his coin to Ikey Mo. All women at
the races find a means of gambling if they
want to gamble. The tote doesn’t attract a
larger number of women or men to the course.
It merely takes the place of the bookmaker on
the course, and pays back to the general public
after each race a larger amount than the book-
maker paid, and exterminates the small town
betting man who was an accursed evil.
The population of Victoria increased 3219
for the quarter ended 30th Sept., ’9B. This makes
it one of the four best quarters Victoria has ex-
perienced since ’9l.

Arthur Snowden, the onetime Mayor of
Melbourne who was howled down bv a public
meeting in his own Town Hall, popped up lately
as the Restless Spirit of Vic. Leg. Council.
Snowden is tired of rejecting progressive
measures, he says. It is time that the Assembly
ceased to send ’em along. The People are now
enjoying all the good they are entitled to, and
more, and the Assembly’s habit of directing new
legislation against Property is most unfair. To
put a stop to further scandals of this kind the
Council must, in its turn, become aggressive.
Snowden raised the aggression cry a fortnight
back, and last week he was at it again. The
Assembly has had a monopoly of aggression, and
Snowden will not sit quiet any longer. He’pro-
poses to retaliate presently in some vague and
awful manner. Meanwhile he mourns the fact
that the Council didn’t take a “ firm stand ” on
the Dual-Vote question, instead of condescending
to fool the country with Wrixon’s proportional-
voting amendment. —

BILE BEANS AGAIN VICTORIOUS.
AN ADELAIDE LADY SPEAKS IN HIGH PRAISE OF THEM.
CURED OF LOSS OF APPETITE AND IRRITABILITY.
Mrs. Margaret Nelson, who lives in Phillip Street, Wolseley (near the Croydon Rail way Station),
Adelaide, is one of the latest beneficiaries through the curative properties of Bile Beans for Bilious-
ness. Hearing of the cure, a reporter from the Adelaide Advertiser went to Croydon on 1* riday
afternoon to inquire personally into the case. Mrs. Nelson at once expressed perfect willingness to tell
her story of suffering, which continued for thirteen long and
weary years, until arrested by the use of only three boxes of
the boon to thousands in Australia —Bile Beans.
“Tell me,” said the reporter, “ the facts connected with
your illness.”
“ Certainly,” said Mrs. Nelson, ‘ I will do so with
pleasure. Bile Beans have wrought a wonderful change in me.
Thirteen years ago 1 took ill with biliousness and indigestion.
I had pains in the head, a bad taste in the mouth ; a sickly
feeling would come over me, and if I did not pick my food I
would suffer great pain after eating. The doctors prescribed
all sorts of physic, but it did me no good.”
“ When did you first hear of Bile Beans ? ”
“About ten weeks ago. Although at first, like many
others, sceptical, I decided to give them a trial.”
“And with what result!’ . -
“ Well, look at me. I look healthy enough, don 11 ? And
I assure you 1 never felt better in my life than Ido now. My
husband knows how ill I was before I took Bile Beans.’
Mr. Nelson, who was listening to his wife’s^ narrative,
humorously assured the reporter that he “ just did.”
“ My temper,” continued Mrs. Nelson, “ was shorthand I
worried a great deal over trifles, and was also very irritable.
You know,” she said confidentially to the interviewer, that
the ordinary housewife has a lot to worry her, and when her
health is bad it does not make matters any the better.’
“ Bile Beans have been a blessing to you, then ? . T „ wJfh
“ Aye, that they have, and I shall always sing their praises. I now go about my
spirit. I never knew during my long illness what it was to be without pain, but during: the' J 6 ®
weeks I have been a new woman. The doctors told me that I would never be cured andnaturallyl
believed them. My appetite used to be wretched before I commenced using the Beans, but now I can
eat almost anything with relish, and do not dread to take my food. . tt
Mrs.Nels y on has resided in the Croydon district for 12 years, and » W widely known. Her
testimony was given with perfect candor and spontaneity, and her marvellous cure is y yp
case of the merits of Bile Beans.

A pleasant hour to spend,

None beats “ White Heather” Blend.

POLITICAL
POINTS
An estimate gives 83 per cent, of candidates
for seats in next Q. Parliament as editors, owners,
or mortgagees of newspapers.
In Yic. Assembly. Staughton, M.P. :
“Dead men sometimes vote.” Hancock, “Yes,
I cannot imagine any other kind voting for you.”
N.S. Wales Executive is going to hang
(on Dec. 13) Wong Ming, the Chinaman, sen-
tenced at Dubbo for slaying another Chinaman.
By the way, it is always safe in Australia to hang
a Chinaman.
S.A. Opposition has, by hard stonewalling,
practically scotched Kingston’s State fire-insur-
ance Bill and other leading items of his policy for
this session. But State fire-insurance, and life-
insurance, will come for all that.
At latest advices the protests of the various
Australian Governments against the stupendous
Papuan land-steal haven’t even evoked an answer
from Chamberlain, of Birmingham where
the bogus jewellery and the gas-pipe guns
come from.
Premierßeid’s sliding probate-tax, running
from 1 per cent, to 10 per cent., concurrent with
an ungraduated land and ungraduated income-tax,
is as good an illustration as any country can give
of quack as distinguished from scientific political
economy.
“ Sugar ” Perry, Opposition whip in N.S.
Wales Assembly, who voted with the Reid Govt,
on last censure-motion, affords the first instance
in any responsible Parliament in the British empire
of an Opposition or Govt, whip voting with the
opposite party on a censure-motion.
Kingston’s Law Reform Bill, despite per-
sistent Tory obstruction, passed its third reading
in S.A. Assembly by 27 to 13. The measure is
one of the few now before the Upper House with
any chance of adoption, as several members on
the Tory side have unexpectedly decided to vote
for it.
The Reid Government has again nominated
alleged Oppositionist “ Tom ” Ewing for the
Works Committee, and he has voted for it on
every division since. As Ewing has deserted the
Opposition it will try to knock him out of said
committee, and is running W. H. B. Pidding-
con, a Bartonian who stuck to his leader through-
out.
The idea of seizing upon Hyde Park,
Sydney, for a new central railway station has
been abandoned by the Reid Government, which
is one large sample of wisdom to the Reid Govern-
ment’s credit, anyhow. But the announcement
that the Ministry is “making inquiries” about
some other site looks as if. any definite move was
still a long way off.
The “disastrous effects
of Protection.” Bradstreets
recently collected statistics of
the U.S. tin-plate industry.
Up till about '9l Great Britain
held practically a monopoly
of tin-plate supply to the
U.S., which took annually
about 700,000,0001 b. none
being made locally. As soon
as the protective duty of a
little over 2 cents a lb. be-
came law, the Yankee began
to manufacture it for him-
self, turning out about
14,000,0001 b. the first year—
’92. Since then there has been
a steady increase in the local
output of about 50 per cent,
per annum, bringing the pre-
sent year’s production to
640,000,0001 b., and every year,
as Uncle Sam .makes more
himself, he buys less from
Britain, whose sales to him
now are hardly worth men-
tioning. So, by the institu-
tion of a protective duty, the
U.S. has found employment
for its own men working up
its own iron and tin ; has kept
a vast sum annually in its
own country ; and on top of
all has brought the price to
the consumer down 37 per
cent. The “ disastrous effects
of Protection on a country ”
are obvious—the other fel-
low’s country.
Westralia—which owns
in its south-west portion some
of the most fertile and most
regularly watered land in this
continent—is proud to report
that it had 133,000 acres un-
der crop in ’9B as compared
with 111,000 in ’97, an<l 98,000
in ’96. It is a notable im-
provement—for Westralia—
and is mainly owing to the
fact that the t’othersider, who
isn’t quite so aggressively
dead as the sandgroping agri-
culturist, has begun to go on the land. S.W.
Westralia, with its natural advantages, should
be a great agricultural country, when its weary
population rises from the grave and gets to work.
Sugar-members Ewing and M‘Pariane con-
veniently “ratted” to the N.S.W. Ministerial
fold on the eve of the last vote of censure. The
very next week, by a queer coincidence, sugar-
members Ewing and M‘Farlane were both Minis-
terially nominated to the Parliamentary Public
Works Committee. Are the sugar-constituencies
entitled to 50 per cent, of the Assembly’s share of
that committee ?
Freetraders in N.S.W. Assembly, seeking
to justify the tea-tax, found themselves in a hole
over the other proposed duties. Storey, M.L. A.,
said that the tea-tax would receive his support
because, he argued, it would raise the standard of
tea ; but he was altogether against the rice-duty.
“ But, ’’ objected Crick, “ why wouldn’t it get us
better rice ? ” “ Because it wouldn’t,” was Storey’s
best answer, and he gave it.
Among the measures left unfinished at the
end of the Maoriland session were the State acci-
dent insurance scheme, the compulsory eight
hours, the measure providing for a second ballot
where no candidate has received a majority of the
votes polled, and the municipal one-man-one-vote
idea. Still, the Old Age Pension law was passed
despite tremendous opposition, and that is a
good session’s work in itself.
When Premier Reid was laboriously ex-
plaining away the failure of the Jolly-and-Fat
as shown by his Budget, his supporters, who
could not believe their ears, kept interjecting all
through the speech. One of them interrupted his
peroration with: “Why are the duties neces-
sary ? ” Whereupon the enraged Premier, turn-
ing right round and facing his followers, de-
mand : “Am I addressing a timber-yard! ” The
rest was silence.
Melbourne Corporation is struggling hard
to get a Bill through Parliament this session
authorising it to raise .£250,000 and therewith
buy up the local electric-lighting companies and
illuminate the city “on its own.” The gas-ring,
and the Tory party which objects to municipal
Socialism because such things disturb it in its
sleep, will make a hard fight, however, and old
Dunky Gillies will say that the proposal is “too
thin,” so the chances of success this year are not
brilliant.
The recent victory of the Tory candidate
at Tuapeka (M.L.), whereby Seddon’s majority
was reduced to nine, was a very limited success
after all. The new Tory member was returned by
1118 votes, while the four Govt, supporters who
split the Democratic vote and wrecked the show
mustered 1658. The Absolute Majority Bill, pro-
viding for a second ballot in such cases, unfor-
tunately went overboard among the unfinished
business at the end of the session, but it will pro-
bably be revived next year.
The N.S.W. soap and candle industry is
starting a demand that it shall be included in
Premier Reid’s new protective tariff. Since the
duties began to be taken off N.S.W. soap and
candle factories have diminished from 50t040 ; pro-
duct of soap has declined from 299,000 to 187,000
cwt. per annum; product of candles from 4 632,000
lb. to 2,632,0001 b. ; number of employees from 476
to 310 ; wages have gone down 10 percent., and the
public pays the same for its soap and candles as
before. And the drop in the trade is even bigger
than it looks, for in the protective days a soap or
candle factory as reckoned in the official statistics
was a place employing 5 or more hands—all
smaller establishments were left out. Now places
employing 4 or more hands are included and their
product is reckoned in the total.
WHAT ARE WE TO DO?
“It is feared that the bubonic plague will be introduced here
through rats.” —DAILY PAPER.
For ten solid years Gippsland (Vic.) pro-
vince allowed Charles Sargeant and his three col-
leagues to stroll back to the House of Rich Land-
lords unopposed. Now, Sargeant has gone broke
and become a poor landlord and lost his seat, and,
as two candidates have arisen (God knows where
they got the second one), there is actually going
to be an election. But the machinery has got
rusty. The big constituency has quite forgotten
how to elect anybody, and a wail comes from
Yackandandah that the inhabitants can’t even re-
member where the regular polling-places are.
What a beautiful commentary on the alleged re-
presentative character of the 48 Earth-stealers !
The N.S.W. Labor-party is a sugar-party,
but not a tea-party.
POETRY.
NEW NURSE (with rather rough complexion):
“ What pretty dimples you have got, my
dear!"
TERRIBLE INFANT : “Yes, and what ugly pimples
you’ve got- and that's poetry.”
“Increases are provided for every civil
servant down to telegraph boys.” —Premier Reid,
in Assembly, 23 Nov. Yet on the printed esti-
mates there is hardly a single increase anywhere.
A lump sum of £15,000, however, is allowed,
which may or may not be spent. If it is spent it
has to be spread over the salaries of nearly 16,000
public servants, so if every one gets an increase
it will be about 4d. a week all round. What a
magnificent gift of inaccuracy is possessed by the
pachydermatous Premier Reid!
When Premier Reid retired Saul Samuel
from the Agency-General over a year ago he pub-
licly stated that the only allowance the Govt, had
granted the aged official was a full year’s salary
under the cover of twelve months’ leave of
absence. On the Estimates for ’9B-’99, to be dis-
cussed shortly in Parliament, .£2365 is set down
as a gratuity to the ex-Agent General, on the
basis of one month’s pay for each year of service.
Why on earth couldn’t the Reid Ministry tell the
whole truth about a small matter like that ?.
N.S.W. Lands Minister Carruthers curtly
declines to produce the official report on Norfolk
Island matters “unless moved for in the usual
way.” As the “usual way ”is for the Govt, whip
to “ object ” every time a formal motion to pro-
duce papers is made, this session wil} probably go
by without the report reaching the table. Minis-
ters therefore cannot complain if rumor hath it
that the report has been “burked” because it
advances charges of peculation in comparatively
high places, and that a well-known society leader
is mentioned as having expressly solicited the
burking.
There are people who wonder why the
N.S.W. Labor-party doesn’t jump at Crick’s
suggestion to ease-off food duties by collecting
postage on newspapers. But the fact is that
the bosses who run a good many of the Labor
members run also allegedly Labor papers, which
depend for their circulation on free postage. A
postal regulation has it that newspapers shall not
be delivered in the town in which they are pub-
lished ; but that little difficulty is got over by
carting a parcel out to a suburban office, and
dumping the lot in the box there.
Members of N.S.W. Labor-party complain
bitterly of being expected to “keep a House”
while highly-respectable Govt, supporters go home
to bed. It is a tradition among the ranks of the
M‘Gowenites to “see the thing out,” and the
attorneys and business men who make the rank
and file of the Reid crowd take advantage of this
amiable weakness of theirs by clearing out early
in order to be on duty fresh for “ important en-
gagements ” in the morning. Hot words were
exchanged about it between members of the allied
hosts on the evening of the last vote of censure.
One of these nights the same old George will find
himself suddenly and entirely dependent on his
own early-closing barrackers after eleven.
“ This House has never opposed the will of
the people, when constitutionally expressed,” was
the beautiful remark of Fitzgerald, of Yic. House
of Rich Landlords, when that body sent back the
One-Man-One-Vote Bill to the Assembly, the
other day. The House of Landlords has three
times rejected the Bill providing for one-man-one-
vote at Assembly elections after the People’s
Chamber had passed it by huge majorities. This
time it has passed it, after four months’ delay, and
after tagging on a scheme by which eight Mel-
bourne constituencies are to be combined in one
and put under the Hare system of “ proportional
voting.” There is no visible gain for Conservatism
in this idea, and it appears to be put in only to
show that the Council won’t take the plain,
straight,. clearly-expressed will of the people at
any price.
The rising wall of European Continental
Protection. Russia has ukased that, from the
beginning of 1900, A.D.,
all goods forwarded between
Russian ports, European
and Asiatic, shall be car-
ried in Russian ships.
Also, that no British are
to be employed in ships
under the Russian flag.
At present most of the
Russian coastal trade
is done by English-
owned ships, worked
by Russian crews,
commanded by a Brit-
ish captain and a
Scotch engineer—all
marine engineers are
Scotch registered
as Russian and called
by Russian names.
The British own-
ers will now have
to choose bet ween
giving up the
Russian trade,
or trusting their
ships entirely to
Russian officers
and agents.
Premier Reid’s
half-promise that
if N.S. W. Assembly
passed the tea-tax
now he would estab-
lish an Old Age Pen-
sion system with the
proceeds next year
was a miserably impro-
bable affair. The one
thing Reid never keeps
by any possible chance is
nis promise. The tea-
duty money is otherwise
required—we have Reid’s
own word for it. It wouldn’t
yield near enough for pension
purposes, anyhow. Also, the
only sure and business-like way
to get the Old Age Pensions is to
bring in a Bill formally establish-
ing the system and providing the
needful source' - of revenue for the purpose, said
revenue to be paid into a special fund and kept
entirely apart from the hungry Treasurer’s cash.
Unless Q. Parlt. sits right up to the day of
its dissolution, ll'th April, ’99, there does not
seem a hope of getting' through the proposed
measures. These include the Mining Bill of 246
clauses, 50 of which have been fought over line by
line; ten other Govt, measures and numerous
private Bills ; also, three-fourths of the Estimates-
in-Chief, to say nothing of the truly dreadful
Loan and Supplementary dittoes.
Quite a sufficient reason exists tor Premier
Reid to place himself in communication with the
Colonial Office about the appointment of the new
IN.S.W. Governor. If he believes Federation to
be as near as he says, he should inform the
Colonial Office that only a pro tem. Governor is
required. The Home authorities may otherwise
appoint a man for five years, and when so ap-
pointed N.'S.W. might have to foot his,bill for the
whole term.
The committee to which Victoria’s House
of 48 Rich Landlords has remitted the Exported
Products Bill isn’t getting by any means the sort
of evidence it expected. The idea is to extend to
other exports the same State supervision now
exercised over exported butter, and the Council
regards the idea with a baleful eye as an objec-
tionable interference with the liberty of the sub-
ject. But a whole multitude of solid business
men have rolled up to approve of the interference.
They say that the Govt, brand put on good pro-
duce, and the rigid rejection of inferior stuff, have
raised prices, improved the demand for Vic. goods,
and prevented the trade being damaged by small,
mean, ignorant people who would otherwise ex-
port rancid cow-fat, and bring down prices all
round. Most of these people howl for more State
interference, and the 48 Landlords don’t know
what to make of it.
The financial policy of N.S.W. Treasurer
Reid wobbled last week in a frightful manner. Its
various phases were : (1) He absolutely required
the .£175,000 a year estimated to be produced by
the proposed tea, rice, confectionery, coffee,
chicory, cocoa and chocolate duties. (2) He was
prepared to drop the duties on coffee, chicory,
cocoa and chocolate. (3) He was prepared to limit
the tax on tea to a period of 18 months or two
years. (4) He was prepared after this’year to set
aside the tea revenue, or most of it, as a provision
for Old Age Pensions. (5) He was prepared to-
sacrifice two-thirds of the tea duty, which con-
sequently stands at Id. per lb. instead of the pro-
posed 3d.—the Assembly looked after that matter-
and threw out the 3d. duty by a majority of 12.
(6) He was prepared to abandon the rice duty
altogether—the Assembly looked after that also,
and bounced the rice duty out by 46 majority.
(7) He was prepared to abandon anything, except
office.
“Violent Fains in, the Stomach
after eating, and pains under the shoulder-blades
for several years,” writes the pictorial and adver-

after the use of a few bottles I was quite restored
to health.”

Curious turn of the wheel ! The daughter
cf Lomhroso—a roll-priest of decadence, and boss
-criminologist—is to be tried for complicity in the
