“Molb.”: The burning of a woman in a
lighter on the Yarn gives a climax to some
romantic ideas 1 have put together about such
habitations. In past years Melbourne had a large
fleet of lighters, with women and families aboard.
Life was spent on the water, alternating between
river and bay. Some had pretty little flowerpot
gardens.
A very important point re the rights of
persons found not guilty of offences has been
raised in Kngland by Justice Wills. A man in-
dicted gave evidence in bis own behalf and was
acquitted. He was subsequently prosecuted for
perjury, and the Judge, in addressing the grand
jury,said that the proceedings practically amounted
to trying the man twice for the same offence. _ It
would certainly seem that such a prosecution is an
indirect means of interfering with the finality of
a jury’s verdict, and thus gives the Crown a right
to.appeal against the result of a criminal case,
which is denied to the accused person.

Bayswater, Sydney, October, 1898.
W. H. Malyon, Esq., Man. Chemist, Ballarat.
Dear Sir. —For years past (about ten), I have been
trouble.with “Acne” in its worst form, and have sought
advice from the best Australian and London doctors, with
only fair results. A-Victorian friend of mine, some four
or five weeks ago, sent me a tin of your “ Ligmolliene ”
and speaking very highly of its wonderful curative powers.
I naturally took same Cum Grano Salis " and was in-
clined not to use it; eventually I tried your ointment
-with truly marvellons results,.and .ani now entirely cured ;
and think people who suffer from skin diseases are greatly
indebted to you for your wonderful remedy. I shail
advise all my friends with skin troubles to use same.
•Wishing “Ligmolliene- 1 ’ all the success it richly deserves,
Yours faithfully, GEORGE J. O’REGAN.
WHAT THE BOCTOHS SAY:—
...... Doveton -street, Ballarat,
My dear Mr. Malyon,— August 21st, 1898.
With great pleasure I now sit down to record pro-
gress with regard to my old enemy “ Eczema Capitas,”
from which I suffered-; notwithstanding constant treat-
ment for some years under the prescriptions of Pro-
fessor M'Oall Anderson and other eminent derma-
tologists,. with only partial and temporary relief.
When I met you I commenced somewhat sceptically
your ointment Ligmolliene, and after about two months’
treatment I may honestly say my enemy is defeated,
and lam perfectly well. I will only add that since I
commenced the treatment of myself I have prescribed it
to others, and am still doing so with brilliant results.
Please accept ray hearty thanks, and remain yours sin-
cerely, TIIOS. LE GAY HOLTHOUSE, L.
Discovered by the Proprietor,
William Henry Malyon,

Truth, it is said, is sometimes stranger than
fiction, and in no way has this phrase been better
exemplified than in the plain, unvarnished state-
ment given a reporter by Mrs. E. Morey, a lady
who resides at 125 Jones-street, Ultimo, N.S.W.
The story she related is here given in her own
words. She said: “I had not been well for
several weeks, suffering terribly from severe pains
in my right side, in fact, the pains at times vvere
so severe I could neither sleep or rest on mv right
side, and drawing my breath was accompanied by
a sharp pain in the side and back. My liver was
in a very bad condition, food I could' take but
very little of, because of the distress it caused after
eating. I tried many treatments and medicines,
but all failed, and my condition was so bad I fully
made up my mind death was the only release from
my illness. A few weel<s ago I heard of the re-
markable cures being effected by the use of Dr.
Morse’s Indian Root Pills. I was persuaded to
try them. The first few doses gave me immediate
relief, the pains leaving like magic. I continued
using them for a time, and to-day am entirely
cured. I firmly believe Dr. Morse’s Indian Root
Pills have been the means of saving my life. I
have recommended them to many of my friends,
and it gives me great pleasure to make this state
ment public, as my faith in Dr. Morse’s Indian
Root Pills is very strong. If persons reading my
words are sufferers, a remedy is at hand that will
cure them, viz., Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills.

A Brisbane Marvel.
WELL KNOWN LADY SUFFERED
TORTURES FOR 15 YEARS, APPAR-
ENTLY NIGH UNTO DEATH, BUT
DR. MORSE’S INDIAN ROOTS PILLS
EFFECT A COMPLETE CURE.
Mrs Elizabeth Smith, whose home is in Surrey-
street, Red Hill, Brisbane, Q., has every reason
to be a thankful woman, as she has been actually
rescued at the brink of the grave. Having heard
such reports, it was decided to fully investigate, so
a reporter was sent to obtain the facts, which are
given in Mrs. Smith’s words : “ For the past 14
or 15 years I have suffered severely from my liver
and indigestion, and two months ago I had a very
bad turn ; the sensation was as though there had
been an iron band around my stomach, and I had
great trouble in breathing. In fact, I thought I
was going to die, and was about-to make my will.
It was about this time that I read about Dr.
Morse’s Indian Root Pills, but having tried so
many medicines without any good result, I hardly
thought they were worth trying, but thinking . if
they did not cure they would do no harm, I decided
to give the Pills a trial. I was too ill to go for them
myself, but sent one of my neighbors to Mr.
Vaughan, the chemist, where they were purchased,
and I can truthfully say that they have made a
new woman of me, and I feel better than I have
been for years. I never miss an opportunity to
recommend them to everyone suffering from any
complaint, for they have done more for me than
all the medicines I have ever tried. Since I have
been cured, my daughter-and-son-in=law have dis-
carded all other medicines, and swear by Dr.
Morse’s .Indian Root Pills. While the ..reporter
was interviewing Mrs. Smith, her married daugh-
ter, Mrs. Brierly, came in, and she not only fully
corroborated her mother’s statement, but spoke
in the highest praise of the Pills. She said that
after seeing the marvellous result in her mother’s
case, both she and her husband feel quite certain
that Dr. Morse's Indian Root .Pills have been the
means of saving her mother’s life.”

Scratched for Ten Years.
TERRIBLE ECZEMA CURED.
Job had patience, we are told, but there is no
need to go back four thousand years to exemplify
patience, for there is a storekeeper named Mr. W,
J, May, at Tabulam, N.S.W., who has had eczema
for ten years, and who has probably out-Jobed Job
for patience. He writesit is with great
pleasure that I testify to the excellent quality of
Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills. I have suffered
for ten years from a skin disease called eczema,
and have tried doctors, herbalists, and chemists,
without deriving the least benefit. I was under
the treatment of one herbalist for twenty months,
and another for eighteen months, I tried every
remedy that I could hear of, but only met with
disappointment. I can assure you my complaint
was a most troublesome one, so troublesome chat
at night I bound the parts up to prevent scratch-
ing when asleep, and I used to take the bandages
off in my sleep, and would scratch until it was
quite raw. I saw several testimonials of cures
effected by Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills, and
though there were none that would apply to my
case, I decided to give the Pills atrial. After
half-a-dozen doses all the itch and irritation had
gone, and though after taking about a bottle of
the Pills my skin was just the same, I persevered
with the remedy, and after a few more doses could
plainly see an improvement; and now, after taking
five bottles, I am glad to say that I am completely
cured, and my skin whole. You are at liberty to
publish this letter. It is the truth, and the most
respectable and reliable people in this district can
certify to the same.”

Dear Bulletin. Re paragraph (26/11/’9S)
concerning Warwick (Q.) electorate. There is
quite 25 per cent, of fiction in the alleged fact
that “ exactly two-thirds of the voters are R.C. ’s ”
and just 100 per cent, of fiction in the further
statement that “Arthur Morgan (present mem-
ber) boasts that he can beat any man in Queens-
land for Warwick seat.” Permit me to say that
no such boast has ever been made by—Yours
Arthur Morgan. ’
Australia s oldest attorney, the venerable
Mr. B. C. Rodd, died the other day, not a month
short of his i-Oth birthday. He was for 05 years
on the roll of N.S. W. solicitors, and was im-
mensely proud of his record. A hale, lively old
gentleman, he told a Bulletin man some time
since that he started life with no education worth
mentioning, and came to Australia in 1822, when
13 years old. He was at once en i> -a fr ed as
overseer to proceed to Patrick’s Plants in
charge of six convicts. He learned to reap
drive bullocks, and build huts; and in '2s’
for a change of occupation, went to Parra-
matta .And apprenticed himself to Dr. Montgomery
Martin, who had some local celebrity as an author
of books on Australia. From Dr. Martin he learned
how to blister and bleed, and draw teeth—at 10s
Gd. per wrench. When Martin left for India
young Rodd went to Sydney, and articled
himself to E. J. Keith, a well - known
attorney who represented George Smith Hall in
certain famous Govt, prosecutions. In three
years Rodd was at the head of the office • and
shortly afterwards he left Keith and started busi-
ness for himself. . In his first year he made £93,
and paid £52 for living expenses. In his second
year he took in lodgers—Ranclolph Want was one
of them ; and Want and Rodd, eager for fame
and fortune, studied regularly from 7 to 10 every
e Y J ' l,n «- By ’37 Rodd was making over
=61200 a year. In ’39 he married Miss Janet
Robertson (sister to “old Jack.”) In ’O2 he re-
tired from business ,£BOOO in debt (“or rather ”
said the old man, “ business retired from me ”) •
but he paid his debts and made money by the
sale of accumulated land. The old gentleman
had been living at Five Dock since 1850, and
leaves many children and grandchildren to per-
petuate the memory of a fine old fellow with a
line old record.

Hugh Hart Lusk’s “ School History ” has
now undergone the scrutiny of four N.S.W.
Ministers of Education and the erasures and
amplifications of a score of revisers—with the
very natural result that, whatever it was worth at
the beginning, it is worth little or nothing now.
-Lusk got <£4so for the MS., and <£545 extra has
been spent on putting it into its present state.
When the MS was originally accented, Mr. Lusk
professed hnnself willing to bring certain figures
up to date, and otherwise tit the work for publica-
tion, but the Education Department took so long
to make up its mind that he had to leave the book
to its fate. Now the £1)05 will almost certainly be

T- P. O’Connor, in liis journal, “ M.A.P.,”
publishes what he describes as a “ startling story ”
regarding the estrangement between Hori Grey
ami his wife. O’Connor says that Grey’s sus-
picions concerning his wife were, aroused bv the
discovery of a compromising letier written to
D une Grey by the captain of the war ship which
was conveying the Pro-Consul and his fortunes to
the Cape. Grey, it is alleged, subsequently dis-
covered that his wife knew nothing of the letter,
and this paved the way to a reconciliation. This
* startling story "’ is, in all probability, an inven-
tion. Some years ago Lord Stamford, Grey’s
relative, who brought ab mb the reconciliation,
minutely detailed to a friend of The BulT-ETIN
(who was also deeply in Grey’s confidence) the
facts of the painful estrangement, and there was
no reference to the alleged letter.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The best-look in" youngster seen nt Rand wick
on Saturday was Cameron, Tom Pay ten's big black
Locltiel colt, which had his first public run in the Nursery
Stakes. Although unplaced, he finished well up, and
will probably turn out well. Pavten has a l>i" striiv in
hand—somethin" over 20-and as they include such as
Cocos, Clarion, Sweet Marie, War God, Phillips, and Miss
West, besides a dozen or so youngsters by Locltiel and
Carbine, his stable should be worth watching. Co»*os is
none the worse for his Melbourne ifruellimr but War God
is very sore.
* * • •
Tim attendance at Rand wick on Saturday was
not what might be expected at. the province’s premier
course on a tine afternoon. Auditor was made favorite
for the Hurdle Race, but could not get belter than a bad
second to Aboriginal, the previous Saturday’s winner at
Warwick Farm. Lady Trident ami Sunflower started
equal favorites for the Course Handicap, but both failed
to run into a place, and Telegraph, well backed by the
public at 7to 1, won from Wakawatea. Nineteen young-
sters started for the Nursery Stakes, Nightlight being
most fancied. Lowland Chief, a chestnut son of Anticus,
at Bto 1, won comfortably from Cornquist. Corrie Roy
was picked upon by the crowd for the Club Handicap,
but again they pulled the wrong pea, as Linda, still
another Bto 1 show, won from Shaughraun. The High-
weight Handicap was a good betting race, lint the winner,
Satanelte, a brown mare by St. Swithan, Merlooloo's sire,
was the only one of the heavily-backed division who
showed prominently.

For jumping from his mount to pick up his
whip. Trainor, rider of a winner at Jloonee Valiev, was

The law. having tried all it knew to escalade the
famous Co! ling wood tote-shop, and been beaten in the
attempt, is going to try again in a very low-down fashion
Part of the success of the “defence” hitherto has been
the alleged fact that neighbors permit of escape through
then premises, which, not being gaming-houses, cannot
he either watched or entered hv the police. To th rart
this, Govt, is submitting a Bill to Amend the Police
Offences Act, enabling warrants to he issued to enter
neighboring houses, and to apprehend persons and seize
moneys and gaming paraphernalia found there. Some of
the phraseology of the Bill and the warrant under it
savors of watch house justice and it will be a matter of
surprise and regret if even the indolent Vic. public allow
it to get on the statute-book. Grave infringements of
the rights of liberty are possible under a provision giving
power to a policeman to enter a house “ reported to be a
common gaming-house,” and arrest persons and seize
moneys found therein. In fact, it is only the cool cheek
of impunity that could have tempted the authorities to
even suggest such a law in a free community. The things
that the most harmless people and harmless hou-es are
reported ” to be by the average constable would astound
even an afternoon-tea society-woman.

Alfred Detikin states that a Melb, Cup gather-
ing, apart from the horrors of the odds, is “simply
frivolous,” and although he doesn’t clamor for the
abolition of the national carnival, he urges the necessity
for “moralising anti rationalising” it. If Premier
Turner appoints a royal commission to report on the
“social, moral, and economic effects of horse-racing on
the community ” (vide Jack Murray’s proposal), he may
as well get Deakin to draw up a rational Cup scheme.
Mould a Sturdy Cob race, with clergymen riders, he
Rational, or does Alfred desire to devote the V.R.C meet-
ing to prayer ?

Editor Bulletin.—Re unclaimed prize-money
held by “Tattersall” since his arrival in Tasmania
Some years ago it was stated that “Tatt.” held in trust
upwards of i 50,000 unclaimed prize-money. Whatever
the actual amount, it would be interesting to know how
it was disposed of and what has become, or is to become
of subsequent accumulations—for the money cannot pos-
sibly belong to “Tattersall,” who, I am sure, would be
the last to claim it. Also, re distribution of result-slips,
I have more than once had occasion to send a second
stamped envelope before I got my slip, and, considerin''
how promptly holders of tickets which draw a horse are
notified, it should be just as easy to notify holders of
tickets which draw a money-prize only. “Tail..” has the
address of every subscriber, so there can be no difficulty
in doing this. State audit has shown the large sums of
unclaimed prize-money left in “Tatt.’s” possession for
a half-year s operations during what is practically the
“off-season” for racing, and the public are justified in
demanding some such concession as that the winners of
money-prizes should he apprised of their luck in the same
way as are drawers of horses, it being well known that
from a variety of causes tnanv subscribers never see a
result-slip. “Tatt.” can easily afford to hunt up nil
prize-winners, as hitherto-unciaimed prize-money must
be the source of a verv large income to him in the shape
of interest.—A.J.B., Westralia. 1
The story told of Forrester’s first Molb. Cup
win ought to commend him to womankind in general.
Things were looking blue for the much-disappointed
sport when he took Gaulus and The Grafter down to Vic-
toria for the Spring Carnival, leaving his anxious wife in
Sydney. If he didn’t win the Cup the outlook would be
much bluer. When the eventful moment arrived, and
the two nays that carried his hopes came tearing down
the straight beyond all possibility of defeat, Forrester ran
to the telegraph-office and wired the ylad tidinys home—
“All’s well, ’ or words to that effect.

Re the Corbett-Sharkey set-to, last week which
ended in a wrangle over a foul, all bets being declared

betts second was bribed to go into the ring, thereby
causing a breach of the rules which turned to Sharkey’s
account. Writer will not be surprised to hear that Cor-
bett was looking for a soft board to fall on when the
wrangle began. In his fight with Fitzsimmons Corbett
seemed to nave had enough rounds before he received
the alleged knock-out.

The large attendance and enthusiasm shown at
East Sydney Swimming Club’s Carnival, on Satur-
urday last, is another instance of the hold this
fine sport is faking upon the public. Lane won the
220yds. N.S.VV. amateur championship in 2.40 1-5, and
“Tunis Cavill won the 100yds. prof, championship in
1.8 ; the pity being in each case that the field could not
extend them.
* * • ,
“Sid.” Cavill returned to Sydney last Sunday
from the land of the wooden ham, a sadly disappointed
man. All the Yankee swimmers adopted a'“ ware-hawk”
attitude, and whatever matches he got on, by dint of
wheedling or coaxing, were for paltry wagers or o- a te
only. Cavill departed an amateur and has returned a
pro., a state of affairs which he much deplores, for while
little or nothing has been gained by the change in status
the opportunity to compete in Australia and win further
laurels has been irretrievably lost. He holds a poor
opinion of “God’s own country” and reckons that
Australia is good enough for him in future. The
cute \ ankce, says Cavill, looks askance at any
Australian athlete hearing the hall-mark of genuine-
ness, and straightway buttons up under the impres-
sion that the visitor will take him down unless he
is extremely careful. Cavill went to Boston, to New
York and to Chicago, but beyond making a little out of
exhibitions did nothing worth mentioning. He created a
sensation in 'Frisco by swimming to Seal Rocks and
landing among hundreds of the bellowing amphibians,
while the spectators ashore held their breaths expectin''
to see the foolhardy Australian torn to hits; but rat HI
found Hie monsters as harmless as doves and cowardly as
dingoes.
* * * *
The Austral wheel-battle begins this Saturday
on Melb. Cricket-ground. The races last Saturday re-
sulted in one or two good spins and a splendid show of
oronk running by the crowd of riders who didn’t want to
be penalised for the big event. But for the heavy rain in
the morning there would have been many smashes in-
stead of only two-bolh in the 25-mile event. The only
man seriously hurt was the plucky Alf. Middleton, who
broke his collar-bone, and so rubbed himself out of the
Austral. However, before doing this, he won the Vic.
Mile (£5O) in great style from Tairie and Berry.
The mile scratch proved deadly-dull. Walne
was beaten by Mutton in the sprint of his heat, the
champion being apparently unable to gain aii inch on the
Sydneyite, yet in the final Mutton was dead meat to the
other starters. In his heat Beauchamp ran a bad third,
and was awarded a disqualification for the day, which de-
barred him from the final of the Vic. Mile—wherein New-
haven Jackson and L. Barker rode head to head for the
last 100yds., with Don Walker, Forbes and Mutton
battling in that order in the rear.
The 25-mile race showed up Forbes in the light
of a splendid stayer. The big-little man stayed in the
middle of the Held throughout, dodged the falls, waited
until the right time, and then whisked past Parsons—
who expected to win and who had public sympathy—as
if he were a novice. Forbes won by 20yds., Carpenter
and L. M. Jackson second and third.
Apart from the actual obvious cronlc running,
several men, not suspected, performed so erratically as to
make it a very tough job to pick the best of the Aus-
tralists. Corbett, on his St. Kilda trim, seemed to hold
'Value safe, yet he was a bad failure on Saturday, and
Walne himself went down before a man considered im-
measurably his inferior. Beauchamps is, or was, an Austral
“good thing”; but, if Saturday’s form be true, he can’tsee
the front crowd in the big race. Little Gordon escaped
notice the whole afternoon, Gargurevich faded
to nothing, nobody saw M‘Gibbon ride, and the onlv
dangerous Austral men to do anything were Berry, New-
haven Jackson, his namesake, and the Barkers and
Sharp. Sharp, however, fell in his race, and his form
could not be gauged. Still, he is in decent nick, can
sprint and stay, and possesses a head worth those of
hundreds of smart young men who can turn off far faster
miles. Sharp looks a sweet thing for the Austral, but
amid the multitude of Saturday’s crocks, the spectator is
likely to regard any average rider who tries as a cham-
pion.

both times bein<£ world’s records for a t*rass track.

A Engaged for, the Country.
Melb. Evenin' 'Erald points out that it
“ said as much ” a week before Salisbury delivered
his Mansion House speech. Good thing to have
an editor at once a warrior, diplomat, prophet, and
first-class humorist. Any paper ought to hum
under such direction.
“Economy” of some kinds is a crime.
At an Adelaide inquest, Dr. Cleland, of Parkside
Lunatic Asylum, deposed that he was the only
medical officer attached to the institution —which
contained 700 patients. In addition to the over-
sight of all these, he was S.A. Chief Medical
Officer, necessitating the inspection of country
hospitals and attendance at North Terrace Luna-
tic Asylum ! Now, they hanged a man at Mel-
bourne the other day for murder ; but what is one
straightforward murder to the countless murders
caused by this unspeakably uncivilised neglect!
If only for the mere sake of having in the com-
munity experts in mental disease, there should be
attached to a 700-inmate lunatic asylum at least
four doctors.
Harry Stockdale to The Bulletin ?
“E.F.” (B. 12/11/’9B), in his Rougeniont comments,
says:—“Why dir] Carr-Boyd not point out that Grien
could not possibly go overland from Cambridge Gulf to
Carpentaria without blundering on the telegraph line or
coming across cattle stations nearly all the way ? ” Now,
Alex. Forrest traversed in 1879 about 400 miles of this
country, and never came across a single station. I also
traversed it (much upon route Rougeniont would pro-
bably have taken) in 1884, and only met with three
stations between the telegraph-line and the Gulf, a dis-
tance, allowing for having to head big-waters, of 400
miles ; two of these stations had only been formed a few
months, and at the time Rougeniont writes of,
before the existence of the telegraph-line, there
wasn’t one station to a thousand miles. “E.F.” also
takes Hogan, M.P., to task for saying that the
N.W. corner (scene of the alleged adventures) is the
one large tract of the Great Southern Continent that is
still, in the main, a terra incognita to the white man ;
and adds that, though the N. VV. corner may not be as
well known as the S.E., it is no more a terra incognita to
white men than Hyde Park, Sydney. Upon which my
comment is, like “E.F.’s” own—“ Rot! ” There is at
present an enormous extent of N.W. country practically
unknown to white men, to which I have been nearest
(always excepting Rougeniont), and should know. “ E.F.”
writes from hearsay, and does therefore exactly' what he
rates Hogan for doing—writes about places he was never
n>a : ith the difference that Hogan is right and “E.F.”
quite wrong.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
27 of this issue.*

Nanango, a little sun-baked Q. village, has
put itself in running for the Federal capital, on
the ground of the salubrity of its climate. In
proof, it points to one John Toohey, born in 1814,
who has lived at Nanango most of his life, and is
still hale and fit for work. But who wants poli-
ticians, Federal or provincial, to live to 85 ?
realistic battle-pictures, a man shot
dead ihstahterinvariably falls oh his face. In the
huinah there is such a slight projection behind,
that a man naturally throws his weight forward to
avoid going over backwards; and thus, when
death is sudden and the support of the foot is
instantaneously withdrawn, the body must fall
forwards. Also, a wounded man generally turns
on his face to die.
In a Sydney photographer's
window recently saw a mouse dart out of ambush,
catch a fly, eat it; therj hide again and repeat
performance. Mentioned this tb shopman; who
said, “Oh, he’s tame; we keep him tb catch
flies ! ” Wouldn't have believed this if I hadn’t
myself, when a boy, ,tamed a common rhouse.
Caught him young, and had him so that he would
sct-amble all over me, sit on my head, and take
refuge up my sleeve.

“ Yangan ’’ : Be woman workers. China’s
the place to see ’em. Females of the boat popula-
tion live with husband, family, and often two or
three other relations on board the sampan, and,
with the children, do all the work, rowing included.
When an addition to the family is expected, the
prospective mother puts down her oar and retires
for an hour or two, at end of which time she takes
her place again at the oar with the little stranger
strapped to her back.
Copy of letter received by N.S. Wales
M.P.
-’s Flat, 3 Nov. ’fl
_ OX Jdl', O iIUV, VO.
Dear Sir, —We have formed a cricket club here, and I
have been asked to write to j-ou for a subscription. We
have 20 members, and I am sure, if you send us a sub-
scription and something towards getting- matting, it will
be the means of getting all the boys to vote for you at
next election, and giving j-ou a substantial majority here.
Yours truly, .
“ Subscription and something ” sent, of course;
but what is bribery and corruption ?
“ Dominie ”: Be bush-morality. When
pedagoguing on a northern N.S.W. river I was
considered a bit “ratty ” because I would never
remain, or allow a male pupil-teacher to remain,
alone in school with a female pupil. Also, I used
to impress upon every young male teacher, when
leaving to take charge of some tiny bush school,
the imperative necessity for this course. I was
laughed at, but—l know what I know! Many
of the big scrub cockiesand butter-splashers have
no more idea of common decency than a pig. In-
discriminate bunking is the root of the evil.

WHY HE COMES.

Perhaps you are one of those people, and are not
aware of it. You have something the matter
with you, but, not having any experience, treat it
lightly, while it gets worse and worse, untimately
destroying you.
The wise man will at once put himself in the
witness-box, and ask himself the following ques-
tions, as will the wise woman :
Do I sleep badly ; do I wake up in the morning,
even when I appear to have slept all night, fagged
and jaded, with lack-lustre eyes and an inclination
only to go to bed again. Do I eat badly, or, when
Ido eat “well,” does my food agree with and
nourish me ? Do Igo about my work dejectedly—
do I suffer from headaches, pains in either the
limbs or body, and indulge in depressing retros-
pections ? Do I, in short, suffer from a loss of
mental and physical elasticity—that exultant
appreciation of life which all healthy people must
have ?
If to even one of these questions the answer be
“Yes,” then that man realises that he wants a
tonic —something to give him an appetite, to
steady his nerves, and to make him strong and
active in the daytime, and afford him calm and
refreshing sleep at night..
Such a tonic is Minerva Tonic, the world’s
greatest and only permanent restorative, the
praise of which is general and unstinted.
Professor Macadam, Consulting Chemist to the
British Government, writes :—“ Minerva is a
judicious combination of iron, phosphorus, and
alkaloid compounds, which will be found highly
serviceable as a tonic remedy for strengthening
the system.”
Very Rev. Dr. O’Haran, P.S., to his Eminence
the Cardinal Archbishop of Sydney, writes :
“Minerva is most palatable and energising.
Everyone who has had experience of it pronounces
it admirable. ”
Mr. G. D. Clark, ex-M.L.A., Grand Lodge of
Good Templars, writes : —“lt is most helpful in
clearing the brain, stimulating thought, and mak-
ing work a pleasure.”
Mr. Wm. Stevenson, Manager of the Presby-
terian, Sydney, writes : —-“It is a strengthening
and invigorating medicine. It is especially suit-
able for the Australian climate. ”
Also Mr. E. W. O’Sullivan, M.P. ; Mr. Stutch-
bury, Chief-clerk of H.M. Customs, Newcastle ;
Mr. Luckham, of the Bulletin office ; Mr. Selby,
Feldheim, Gotthelf and co., and thousands of
others join in recommending it to the Australian
people.

“ Mr. W. G. HEARNE.—Dear Sir, —I had a bad attack

The Lodge-Doctor.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I WAS putting in an afternoon waiting for a train
in a far-back Australian town, and a bilious man
with pig’s-eheek whiskers took pity on my loneli-
ness. He related his victories in love and war
during two beers, and just as I was on the point
of ordering a third in the hope of getting drunk
enough to bear the strain, the buggy of the local
medico passed down the dusty street and directed
his reminiscences into a new channel.
“ He’s a stuck-up bloke, that,” said he viciously.
“Looks it,” I replied, with mendacious hearti-
ness. The medico was a friend of mine, and had
spent an hour that morning relating his local
lodge-troubles.
“My oath ! We had hard work with him at
first. He was that uppish the missus wouldn’t
call him in for the kids unless there was some-
thing real bad. I told him about it one day, and
he said he’d chuck me out.”
“Chuck you out?” I echoed. “I wouldn’t
have stood that.”
“Yes you would ! ” snorted my bilious friend ;
“he’s a big bloke, and knows how to use ’is
’ands ! ” He fingered the back of his head in a
reminiscent way, and went on hastily.
“ Well, I was going to tell you. When he come
first, all the women went to call on his wife —-
Joe’s missis at the pub, old Mrs. Bulldog, and
the ’ole lot. They left cards, and when they’d
all been, his missis wanted to call on them. Then
he up and said no, she wouldn’t; and started
arguin’ that they were all in lodges, and weren’t
no better than his groom. Why, there’s members
in that lodge could buy and sell him. One bloke
gets 15 quid a week out of mining shares alone,
and old Bulldog owns half the town.
I sniffed as one who marvels at the presumption
of an inferior, and the champion of plutocracy
went on.
“ Old Bulldog got narked at it, and after a bit
he got his knife into the doctor proper. He owns
the doctor’s house, and was just joinin’ our lodge
when he found out that the doctor wouldn’t pass
him. So he went to the bloke and had a bit of a
yarn with him, and the bloke says that a man of
his position ought to be above joinin’ a lodge.
Such lip I never heard ! So old Bull, waited a
week, and back he went again.
“ I’m all right now,” says he.
“ No, you ain’t,” says the doctor, quite sharp,
“Well, I hope I am for your sake,” says
Bulldog. “For if I ain’t, out you go from this
house, and you won’t get ’nother in the town.”
“ Go to ! ” says the doctor, “ I’ll stay at
the pub.”
“No you won’t,” says old Bulldog. “ Because
I’ve bought it.”
“ So the bloke had to give it best and pass him.
The doctor’s missis was down with this here
typhoid, so they knew he had him under the
whip. He tried to get on to Mulroon a few days
after with some rot about keepin’ pigs and not
cleanin’ drains, but he shut him up pretty quick
by sayin’ he was Bull’s nephew,”
“ Got many members ? ” I queried.
“ O, every bloke in the town went in after that.
We made him shove on an extra mile, so he goes
out to the mines at the Four-mile. Then lie made
a row about comm* out at night ; but we had the
doctor from Johnny town, and ,made this bloke
pay, so he soon knocked that, off. ’
“ What do you give him ? ” .
“He gets fourteen bob a year from us—medicine
chucked in, of course. The ’Sociation gives eighteen,
but they’re going to cut it down. He goes four
miles out for that, and provides his own splints
and bandages, and things. 'Fre’s luck again. . . .
You’d better hurry up ; she’s comm’ round the
bend. So long.”
Vic.

The carriage was full of experts, and there was
501bs. pressure of mining knowledge to the cubic men of
atmosphere. A man produced a dark, hard substance.
“ Any gold in that ? ” It went from hand to hand. One
man said “ telluride,” decidedly. Another said oxidized
talcous silicate.” The next pronounced it ferruginous
cyanide,” or something which sounded equally clever. All
agreed that it contained gold and wanted to know where
the claim was. The man said : “ I dunno—l ve had the
specimen in my pocket for months. It was soft at first
and I used to nibble it, but it’s got so hard lately I think
I must fhave struck the lode. It s a bit of Turkey
rhubarb.” „ .

A Sydney publican lately promised certain
Sabbatarians that he would not sell on following Sunday
Went out accordingly at 6.30 a.m. on that day, and did
not return till 9.30 p.m. Knocking and whistling had
been going on unheeded all day. Next week s returns
dropped £25, and it took him months to work his old cus-
tomers back. The Sabbatarians had told the publican
oDiiosite that Mr. , in the hotel across the way, had
signed. But not much ! After that, he kept side-door,
front-door, and back-gate going all Sunday long.
* * *
The North Coast Co. (writes a traveller) has a
soft thing on with its steamer-bars. It pays £lO license,
as against £3O for a shore pub. The barman is compelled
to buy everything from the co., and sell at same rate to
passengers, and is allowed 5 per cent, on sales. He retails
large bottle ale or stout at Is. 6d. (shore price, Is.);
whisky, rum, gin, port, or sherry, 6s. per bottle (same
Quality procurable in town at 3s. to 4s. 6d) , spirits and
soda 9d.; cigars—well, not the best brand of sixpenny in
the market. If anyone asks for a glass of beer, the steward
will say, pathetically, “ ev a small bottle, sir. If you
don’t, the balance of the bottle, for which he has to pay
the co. full price, may be left on his hands.
* * * *
“ Has anyone ever seen a conscious consumer of
margarine?” asks Sydney D.T. funnyman. Well, has
anyone ever seen a conscious consumer of Australian
whisky ? Consumers thereof must nevertheless exist by
the thousand, else what becomes of the annual scores of
thousands of gallons of whisky annually distilled—say in
Victoria? Who ever saw a bottle in a public-house
labelled “ Warrenheip whisky ’’—though it is by no means
bad whisky ?

Ed. Bulletin. — Medical bamboozle ! A pre-
scription was recently handed to me to dispense, signed
by a well-known city specialist and bearing the stamp of
an equally well-known Sydney chemist. It was in cipher !
—a neat little arrangement between doctor and chemist
whereby the patient is debarred from having his pre-
scription made up where he pleases and at same time is
compelled to pay an exorbitant price for what is probably
a very simple remedy One could better understand this
sort of thing being done by second or third-raters, but
with alleged tip-toppers it is playing the thing very low
down indeed !—Country Chemist.
« » * *
A unique “colonial industry” is the penguin-
oil factory at Macquarie Island, Antarctic Ocean, run by a
resident 'of Invercargill (M.L.). He has a number of
hands employed there catching penguins and boiling
them down—the product when refined is a first-class
machine-oil. The raw material is periodically shipped to
Invercargill in a vessel that takes down supplies for the
workmen, who sign-on for 12 months at a time. The
penguins are driven like sheep along narrow tracks and
through small gates to the digesters, where each gets a
blow from a club and is knocked into the boiling water in
one act.
• * * *
The Victorian Presbyterian Assembly has just
dropped to a certain financial matter, and considering
that the church is mostly Scotch the fact that it didn’t
drop earlier is amazing. The Assembly is in charge of
£247,000 of church funds. It invested £lB,OOO in the
blighted and reconstructed Australian Deposit and Mort-
gage Bank, which wasn’t at all a Scotch proceeding ; and
it put the rest out on mortgage so carefully that £45,000
is represented by foreclosed properties on which there
will be a heavy loss, and the whole fund only brings in
about £3 7s. per cent. Meanwhile, the various churches
under the Assembly's supervision are mortgaged for
£127,000 which has been borrowed at rates v arying from
4J to 7 per cent. Therefore, the Assembly has wakened •
to three facts : (1) It would have been much more profit-
able to invest its pile in State securities all along ; the in-
terest would actually be more and there would be no
depreciation. (2) It would have paid better to lend the
money to its own churches ; to lend money on bad
security for £3 7s. per cent, and borrow other money
at £4 10s. to £7 per cent, is just as satisfactory as paying
a man to run after you and kick you. (3) At present the
church is paying almost as much for the use of somebody
else’s £127,000 as it is getting for its own £247,000, and, so
far as income is concerned, it might almost as well have
paid off all mortgages and then drunk the other £120,000.
And yet the Vic. Presbyterian Assembly is mostly Scotch.
• * . * »
Since banking first began in Australasia 81
years ago, 32 banks have gone smash—not land banks,
but the alleged genuine article—and only 23 remain alive.
But the private banker goes on remarking just the same
that State banks are bound to be a failure, and that the
privately managed institution, by reason of its eminent
success in the past, is the only kind that can be trusted.
• * * *
The most recent Auckland (M.L.) mining ven-
ture is located on the Hen-and-Chickens, a group of rocky
islands some 80 miles north of the city. A party char-
tered a steamer to go down and investigate, and came
back reporting a copper lode 400 ft. thick, and yielding 2
to 3 per cent, of the metal. The very oysters on the
beach, it was said, were so impregnated with verdigris as
to be uneatable. An American medico is at the head of
the syndicate. The chief difficulty would be in getting the
product away, as there is absolutely no harbor, and the
ocean swell makes landing even in small boats dangerous.
Up to date the islands have been remarkable chiefly as
the last stronghold of the tuatara, a big lizard forming a
connecting link between the lizards of the present and
the gigantic saurians of old. They chum up with the
mutton-birds and penguins, occupying their burrows, and
share in the fish the birds catch. Specimens kept in con-
finement often remain motionless for weeks together, but,
on occasion, can burrow into the soil as fast as their
would-be captors can dig after them.

How are the mighty fallen ! Melb. Y.M.C.A.
now has its headquarters in a wooden shed in the back
yard of a Russell-street establishment divided between a
Hebrew pawnbrokery and an Assyrian old clo’ shop. Time
was when the Y.M.O.A. bought (on bills) a big Bourke-
street block and erected the £20,000 building now occu-
pied by the Salvarmy. But those were the days when
Matt. Davies and McLean had the Mercantile and Federal
Banks to assist in the “ Cawse,” and, with the collapse of
those smellful institutions, the Y.M.O.A. had to put its
palatial edifice ignominiously up the spout.

“Face” : A “swell sharper” lately unbosomed
himself to me. “ The easiest way to make a rise is ‘ ring-
ing the change,’ but, although not worked-out yet, it is
getting risky, and I only tackle it when dead-broke. Bor-
row a five and a pound note and take a friend in for a
drink, slinging down the fiver to pay for it. Barmaid
picks it up, looks at it, and turns for change. You say
suddenly, ‘ Oh, never mind, I won’t trouble you to change
the fiver. I have a pound-note somewhere ’; and, holding
out left hand with single note ‘ palmed,’ you take the
fiver and quickly change it with single note, holding your
hand ahove the counter meanwhile and feeling in your
pocket with the right hand. After an unsuccessful
search, you sling down the pound-note, saying : ‘ Afraid
I’ll have to trouble you, after all. Blessed if I know where
that pound’s got to.’ It is 100 to I‘on ’ her throwing it
into the till and giving you change for a fiver without
again examining it. Have had a few narrow shaves,
though. Tried it once,on a woman at Wagga and she
spotted it but didn’t let on, though I could see she knew.
‘ That’s £4 19s. I have to give you,’ she said, so inno-
cently. 1 Oh, no ; you’re making a mistake—l9s.,’ said I.”

The illuminating of the ruins of Baalbec
(the ancient City of the Sun) for the delectation
of the German Kaiser and his 200 Lutheran par-
sons is a vandalism akin to rigging-up the Venus
of Milo with a skirt and blouse. Wonder there
wasn’t a German band tootling nightly in the
Garden of Gethsemane, lit up with Chinese lan-
terns. The Kaiser’s trip, however, was all busi-
ness. Simply went to Constantinople to convert
the Sultan to the “ Me and God ” religion and ex-
ploit the Porte for trade purposes; also, his
alleged sudden friendship for the Sultan has
“ Made in Germany ” stamped all over it.
The recall of the British Channel squadron
from Gibraltar, just after the Pashoda affair was
settled, showed that the warships had not been
sent to the Mediterranean for a six months’
cruise, although the Admiralty people said so—
and the despatch of five reserve battleships to re-
place them at the Rock indicated it still more
clearly. Five or six years ago the naval manoeu-
vres were directed towards finding out what would
happen in the event of war with France. Selecting
two home ports, geographically suitable, the
squadron demonstrated on paper that the
French fleets at Brest and Toulon, by effecting a
conjunction, w'ould be strong enough to quash the
British Mediterranean force—and then to “deal
with ” the Channel squadron. But the two
British squadrons, acting together, could over-
whelm the Frenchmen.

Esther-
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Esther was a frail little girl, with a face so thin
and wan that the freckles seemed to have fallen
like a merciful veil upon it. She was not pretty.
Her hair was too short to plait, not long enough
to hang gracefully loose, and its grain was alto-
gether too contrary for curls. So it hung miser-
ably about her ears, ragged and tattered like the
remnant of a flag that might have issued from the
nre and smoke of battle.
She was a friendless orphan, which fact made
her a born servant to somebody. Old Mrs.
Mac Smith had secured her services from the State
when she first began to toddle on two legs—two
legs that soon became bowed under the pots and
pans she had to carry in her small arms—and
next to her heart.
1 henceforth the Mac Smith household was per-
petually in a state of war. Her mistress beat her.
Esther screamed, and fought fiercely. But Mrs.
Mac Smith had a duty to perform. So she would
grip the little rebel by the windpipe, and call for
“ Mac.” He always came, swift and sure, and
the enemy was routed.
Thus, for all the eight years of her life Esther’s
existence had been one of screams and pain. She
was such a bold, bad girl, a mere handful, but
with a stubborn heart, primed so full of foolish
courage that it must surely have burst, were it
not for the vent-pipes that relieved the pressure
when Esther called people names, and wouldn’t
work, and scratched the collective face of the
Mac Smith family, with finger-nails kept sharp for
that purpose. And she had two other safety-
valves to her feelings. One was to laugh hysteri-
cally when, for exhibition purposes, she was ex-
pected to cry, and the other was to sob heart-
breakingly when someone tried to cheer her with a
kind word. Mr. Giles, the parson, once said of
her :
“ A regular passion-flower, my dear; but not
quite so pretty.”
A simile so vague that Esther resentfully poked
out her tongue.
The Mac Smith family had only one greeting to
bestow “You little wretch ! ”
• * * *
The canker grew in Esther’s heart. At times
she stole in through the window of the empty eot-
tage next door, and, alone and unobserved, began
talking to herself. It was the one little sociable
break in her martyrdom.
“Why don’t I die ? ” she asked hersen once.
She was eating a green apple. She took another
bite, and swallowed it.
That’s why,” she said. “ Because I’m a greedy
guts.” ° J
And then after a pause, and looking wistfully
at the cold fireplace, she added :
“ Oh, I do wish I didn’t eat.”
Why don 11 run away ?” was the next ques-
tion.
She waxed violent at this.
“Because I’m a looney; and here goes for
squashing old Mother Mac Smith, anyway.” She
brought down the heel of her boot on a large black
spider, creeping on the floor!
“ That’s 43 times I’ve killed her now! ” she com-
Rented, surveying the remains with satisfaction,
au i was twice a frpg, and once a Maori-bug.”
She laughed keenly. “ Oh, I’ll kill her yet,” she
added.
The helpless buzzing of a fly, caught in a spider’s
web, came from a corner. Esther investigated.
She killed the spider, and threw the rescued fly
out of the window.
* * * *
Soon after this the cottage found a tenant. Mr.
and Mrs. Mullampy, a pair newly wed, brought in
their furniture.
Their honeymoon had been a very nice luminary
while it lasted. The cheers of their friends, and
the rice, and the proverbial old shoe, had given
them a send-off into married life with quite a vio-
lent propulsion. But the law of gravitation told,
and they slowed down. Then the rainbow lost
the glory of its tints; the mantle of chimney-
smoke fell on their air-castles ; and they themselves
wi. 16 rl f ht down to the bed-rock of the prosaic.
When they couldn’t get down any further they
commenced to argue. Then the price of meat
went up considerably ; the milkman began to call
regularly; and married life on a solid basis had
begun.
Esther took a great interest in their arguments,
tiptoeing her way on to the very threshold of the
Mullampys’ kitchen at meal-time, she would listen
attentively. And she learnt many things. She
found that she was not the only unhappy person
in the world. Once she saw the husband lift his
hand to strike his wife. “Don’t!” Esther
screamed. The hand was arrested. Esther ran
away.
After that, the child’s presence came to be one
of the unconscious details of their domestic dramas.
Nor did they resent it. In their inmost hearts
they felt that Esther’s shadow, falling athwart the
doorway, was to them Love’s decoy-bird, a crea-
ture swaying them with such a strange influence
that more than once the man was tempted to
crush his pride and call the child in to plead with
the woman he really loved for a kiss. And the
woman, being weaker, could not keep the pain out
of her eyes. Yet she, too, was very proud.
Often, in the midst of bitter recrimination the
husband would suddenly stop shore and glance
fearfully at the door. Esther’s ragged head would
pop quickly back, and the man, with increased
color, would get up awkwardly from the table
and vanish through the opposite doorway.
* * * •
On one of these occasions, when the husband had
left the room Esther dropped something heavily
on the floor. Mrs. Mullampy looked up from the
table, and her visitor, looking very red and guilty,
advanced towards her. In one hand, which hung
limp and lifeless by her side, a large horse-shoe
dangled.
“ Whatever do .”
“ Please, don’t; 1 know I’m wicked.”
“ What’s that for ? ” Mrs. Mullampy pointed
at the horseshoe.
Cos if I .don’t tell no lies I won’t go to
heaven, said Esther, a little mixed, and trying to
nerve herself, in a roundabout way, for a moment-
ous admission. She drew a long breath.
“ To brain him with, please,” she said.
“Who?”
“Him. ’Cos he wanted to hit you.”
Mrs. Mullampy looked at her in silence for a
while. Esther hated to be pitied. She gave her
head a proud toss, calculated to change the com-
passionate expression of the woman’s eyes.
“ I hate him,” she said, spitefully. She tossed
her head again. This time two gum-leaves and a
twig dropped from her tangled hair to the floor.
" My dear child ! ” the woman said, softly.
Esther’s lips quivered. Then she thought of
the spider she had killed on the very spot where
she now stood. The recollection made her more
cheerful. Mrs. Mullampy picked up the horse-
shoe.
“ Would you hit me with this, Esther?” she
asked.
The child shook her head.
“ Well, then, don’t hit him. If you do, you’ll
hit rue.”
Esther’s mouth opened wide.
" Eli n’t a boomerang,” she said, sharply.
Mrs. Mullampy made an impatient gesture.
She bit her lip, as though restraining an exclama-
tion.
“ You don’t understand,” she said.
A sweet temptation came over her, and she
lifted the child on to her knees, and with all the
patience of a loving teacher commenced to ex-
plain. She outpoured the long pent-up secrets of
her heart to Esther. Anon, a querulous note
crept into her voice—a compromise of sweet and
bitter— a woman’s sense of being wronged
struggling with the unreasoning vagaries of a
forgiving heart.
Esther was not listening. Once or twice she
fancied she heard Mrs. MacSinith’s voice calling
her from the next yard. She was tired of sitting
on Mrs. Mullamp.y’s knee. The shrill cry of her
mistress rang out unmistakably now. She felt
uneasy.
“ Let me down, please.”
''Don’t go yet, Esther,” the woman pleaded.
I want to say something.”
But Esther had reached the door.
* * * *
Mrs. Mullampy, after all, was little more than
a girl herself. The romantic story and the coy
admissions seemed to linger about every ornament
in the room when the child had gone. A china
peacock over the mantel-piece, with tail outspread,
repeated distinctly, she thought, her own words :
“ I love him, Esther ! ”
And the voice of that peacock overwhelmed
her. She sat aghast. Then, slowly, like an apple
ripening in an hour, her cheeks grew red. She
snatched at the tablecloth and buried her face in
it Hite a school-girl trying to hide her blushes.
Esther had notgone. Shecautiously peeped round
the doorway. The horse-shoe was dangling, with
one end poked through a tear in her skirt. She
couldn’t keep her thoughts off that spider. She
was tracing with tier eyes the different routes it
might have taken if she had not intercepted it.
Her conjectures brought her glance to the corner-
door. It was ajar, and she saw Mr. Mullampy
looking at his wife through the opening. And she
saw Mrs. Mullampy start and cry out when she
uncovered her head and found her husband stand-
ing beside her.
Jane, he said, softly, “ I heard all. Forgive
me.
The woman was too happy to trust herself yet.
With the blind philosophy of her sex, which
always makes a show of flying in the face of fate,
however friendly it may be, she pretended to be
angry at being found out.
, l , oh ’o, she said > P ettis hly, “it’s all Esther’s
fault. She made me say it.”
" Then God bless Esther!” said the man.
* * * *
The girl had run round the back way, and was
peeping through the window. She saw them kiss.
The woman’s arms were round the man’s neck.
Then Esther began to cry. She felt that she
alone was miserable and unhappy.
“ I’ve a good mind to throw it,” she said, rais-
in# the horseshoe threateningly. “Only I can’t
hit him without hitting her.” ‘
Anyway, for luck,” she added, throwing it
over her left shoulder. It fell heavily on a stray
fowl belonging to Mrs. MacSinith. Esther clapped
her hands in glee.
I hen she took a farewell peep through the
window, and what she saw, whatever it was, made
her say : “ Crikey ! ”
Sydney.
J. J. O’M.
The Guile of Dad M'Ginnis.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When M‘Ginnis struck the mining camp at Jam-
beroora Creek
His behaviour was appreciated highly ;
I or, although he was a quiet man, in manner
mild and meek,
Nor like ordinary swagmen with a monumental
cheek, . ..
He became the admiration of the camp along the
creek
Cause he showed a point to Kangaroobie Riley !
Both the pubs, on Jamberoora had some grog that
stood the test.
(Not to speak of what was manufactured slily !)
And the hostel of O’Gorman, which was called
“ The Diggers’ Rest,”
Was, O’Gorman said, the finest house of any in
the west;
But it was a burning question if it really was the
best,
Or the “ Miners ” —kept by Kangaroobie Riley.
Dad M'Ginnis called at Riley’s. Said he “ felt a
trifle queer,”
And, with something like a wan and weary smile,
he
Said he “ thought he’d try a whisky.’ Pushed it
back and said, “ I fear
I had better, take a brandy.” Passed that back
and said : “ Look here,
Take the brandy; after all, I think I’ll have a
pint of beer ! ”
And he drank the health of Kangaroobie Riley !
“Where’s the money?” asked the publican;
“ you’ll have to pay, begad!”
“Gave the brandy for the beer ! ” said Dad the
wily,
“ And I handed you the whisky when I took the
brandy, lad ! ”
“But you paid not for the whisky!” answered
Riley. “ No,” said Dad,
“And you don’t expect a man to pay for what he
never had!”
—’Twas the logic flattened Kangaroobie Riley !
“See,” said Kangaroobie Riley, “you have had
me, that is clear !
But I never mind a joke,” he added, drily.
“Just you work it on O’Gorman and I’ll shout
another beer! ”
“ I’d be happy to oblige yer,” said M‘Ginnis with
a leer,
“ But the fact about the matter is— O’Gorman sent
me here !
So, good morning, Mr. Kangaroobie Riley ! ”
W. T. GOODGR.

The first night I had slept a little from sheer exhaustion. The
next was terrible. I vomited incessantly; I was chilled to the bone, and
could not warm myself. I suffered horrible pain in the head, and through-
out my whole body. Next day, of course, I was a wreck. They told me I
had been delirious half the night I remember nothing of the day ; but
next night I got a couple of hours’ sleep, and Was rather better in conse-
But vomiting went on steadily, and the fourth night I was a<*ain
delirious. The next day I was as bad as ever; but, thanks to a good
dose of Beecliam’s Fills, i had an excellent
night, and I truly believe that they saved my life. I knew well where I
picked up this fever—in the marshes of Mwenzo, and near Ivituta—and the
killing thing was being unable to stop to cure it; the ten days on the lake
especially—often without food for twenty-four hours—nearly finished me.
I felt certain at the time that X was done for; and X very nearly was.” See
“ Three Years in Savage Africa," by Lionel Decle—Methuen <Sc Co., London.
Mr. Lionel Decle covered a distance of over 7000 miles at one
stretch, between Capetown on the South and Mombassa on the East coast of
Africa. It is worth noting that this “unique journey” would probably
have been cut short; and, to quote the introduction by Mr. 11. M. Stanley,
M.P., “ its vivid record ” would never have been penned had not Mr.
Lionel Decle provided himself with a supply of
